THE RED PAGE.
To wit : Life is a mine in t.he universe of Death.
The lamp of Love hangs in the mine. Angels
nutter round the lamp and make the walls of the
mine dance. Sometimes the lamp is hid when
you want it, and the other fellow hugs your girl.
Then when you lie dead and think of these
things you feel grieved.—Of course, this is an
obviously unfair summary, and the most poetical
idea in the world can be murdered just so. But
the parody has enough of truth to force home the
idea that ephcott’s whole image is awkward and
lumbering, and does not throw one gleam of
poetical light on the familiar theme that Love is
Heaven and the absence of Love is Hell. Too
many of Jepheott’s poems are built this way
You read them once anti bring away no impres
sion from a fog of rough, confused, invertec
language. You read them twice: the fog o:
language lifts a little, and the meaning glimmers
through. You read them thrice, and the meaning
turns out to be a tame old meaning which coulc
be expressed with twice the force in half the space
And often Jephcott seems wilfully obscure. How
many poets persist in mistaking obscurity fo
illumination, and how many readers praise .them
for the error !
• * • •
Jephcott reverses the ordinary electrical condi
tions; it is from a sky of clear words that his
thought lightens. Not terribly, bub often finely
As here—
Love! now that we love eaoh other
And know God’s fatherhood ti ue,
And that Man is our blood-bound hr )ther,
We will alter the world, we two !
(This in Ereles Adams’ vein.) And here—
This is the joy of being a man:
To woo whom you please, and win whom you can ;
While woman still her desire must disguis i
And never look Love in his absolute eyes,
But intangibly weave, and famish, and wait;
The idiot chanee her imperial Fate.
And here—
There’s only one sun in heaven,
Only one earth is ours,
Only one life is given
Us and the birds and flowers J
There’s only one sky to cover
Earth and the whole great sea-*
And give me, O God, one lover,
She but one kiss to me.
Almost J. persuadeth us to be monogamists. Bub
only one kiss ! Catullus knew better.
* * * *
Jephcott writes of “ White Paper”—
Smooth white paper ’neath the pen ;
Richest field that iron ploughs.
“ I caress,” he says,
I caress its surface sheer,
Holding it the Absolute,
Where the things to be inhere,
Waiting their material bruit.
And, like the earth’s song, like the sap’s song—
Evermore a tiny song
Sings the pen unto it, while
Thought’s elixir flows along,
Diviner than the holy Nile.
How our fathers, poring over this white paper in
the dark, traced our secure path on it by starlight
or stake-fires!
O white list of champions,
Spirit born, and schooled for fight,
Mailed in armour of the sun’s,
Who shall win our utmost right!
Think of paper careless sold,
Which few pence had made too dear
On its blank to have enrolled
Beatrice, Lucifer, or Lear!
Think of paper Milton took,
Written, in his hands to feel,
Musing of what things a look
Down its pages would reveal.
O the glorious Heaven wrought
By the Cadmeau souls of yore
From pure element of thought I
And thy leaves they are its door 1
Light they open and we stand
Past the sovereignty of Fate,
Glad amongst them calm and grand,
The Creators and Create!
The virginal Paper has had no more eloquent
aulogium. But there is still room for an epitha-
lamium—When Pen Weds Paper. ’Tis a pretty
theme, and fruitful in imagery. Conceive the
bridegroom Pen, now bold, now hesitant; the
coy and fluttering bride; the march of merry
Ideas strewing roses, roses, all the way; the
jubilate of the populace; the acclaim of future
ages ! Could not Herrick have done it featlv ’
* * * * J
This is firtely expressed of a rail way-journey—
Dangers ’neath their dim red veils
Crouch by the rails.
’Tli.fi “red veils” and the “crouch” are of the
ess' nee of Jephcott’s promise. Other lines—
I breast long heights that hopeless rise
Under huge saies.
Others—
Thro’ the swimming plains of sleep
Swiftly I sweep
O’er the swimming plains of sleep
Slowly I creep.
Hours, hours starry-eyed
Over me glide,
With their swift wide wings at rest,
Set to the West.
It seems unreasonable, in the face of these, to
denounce .Jephcott for want of melody; but his
voice is rarely tuned so Many times more often
he is excruciatingly harsh, careless of his sounds
and accents (“ Shine, gems no steel-stroke loosen-
eth.” Ugh !), bedecking rough lines with words
strained from their sense, obsolete or obsolescent,
and seemingly quite proud of fantastic peacockery,
and quite ignorant that every time he makes the
reader hurry over a cacophony, or pause to disen-
tangle a meaning, he loses impetus, loses force,
loses poetic value. Then lines like these come to
make amends :
O Life, Death makes thee strange and holy,
Even as Love makes thee great and pure ;
With arms round each man stands secure:
We cannot fail and corrupt wholly
While Love and Death endure.
One is not sure if this isn’t the finest thought in
all Jephcott—the alliance of Love and Death to
ennoble existence. How much grander than the
G. F. Watts idea—the vulgar idea—that Death is
Love’s foe !
* • * *
The poetry of the following and final extract is
not obvious at a first reading. (Most of Jephcott’s
verses want reading twice: some, as said, have
little to give in return for three times.) And you
must figure to yourself the scene in the solitary
bush, and the solitary man in the solitary bush-
all his conquests, glories, triumphs, spoils, shrunk
to this little measure—and the solace of a fire to a
solitary man in the solitary bush (you, for ex-
ample). Then the flavour comes and lingers.
Prettily phrased enough, hut with no particular
strength or import; and how many thousand years old is
the idea? To be sure, most id,as are old—all the best
ones, anyway—hut they should be, and can be, so re-
faeed and presented as to give familiar nerves a tingle of
novelty, and so make a more abiding impression. That
abiding impression is the stamp of value in verse. No-
thing is good which you can read one moment and for-
get the next—li e this of Nora Hopper’s in the Ludgate'
There is absolutely nothing to this but a little rhyming*
and a little phrasing—and Nora Hopper’s name. It has
n?™!' ff° r T, 14 \ ou '> ' Tlakes 110 picture, impresses no
thought. But Nora Hopper has prosed and versified
several Celtic legends with some degree of skill and taste
—though some of her results have been uncommonly
tedious—and has been authoritatively recognised as in-
spired with the true spirit of Ossian and Co., and now she
can write what dull and obscure thing she pleases, and
people cry up her dulness for wisdom and her obscurity
for depth of fancy. It is often so in England. But as an
example of an old idea re-faced and made effective—it is
very old, printed by “Ouida” in “Under Two Flags”
u ahur > dr £? others—take a verse or two from Sept.
N.W Review. The New Review, of course, is a magazine
ot a higher stamp, and prints verse which has energy as
well as art, and which is not continually occupied in
sentimentalising over worn old sex-phases for the benefit
of soft-headed readers. And its verse has the atmos-
phere of art to which we in this half-educated country
can only rarely attain—though even in the New Review,
or m the best Eng. literary weeklies, there rarely appears
anything so poetical as Daley’s work, or with so much
rugged force as Lawson’s.
In the Hospital.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sun creeps slowly up the wall ;
The Judas-flow’rs show bright an 1 r. d ;
The scent of pine-trees gaunt and tall
Comes to me as I lie in bed.
And through my open window sound
The parrots’ cry, the cattle’s lowing,
The insect life that strews the ground—
I hear the very grasses growing.
A fleeting swirl of pigeons white
Gleams by me as I fie and gazy
Arid onward, onward to the right,
And westward through the golden haze.
I stretch my hands to them in vain ;
The hopeless tears my eyes are blinding ;
All, God 1 will I return again
To where the sleepy stream is winding?
Out westward where the yellow grass
Lms hot and struggling with the sand ;
Where Drought has stopped, and will not pass,
His hot arms clasped about the land
’Tis there the lowing cattle call,
’Lis there the heated wind is sighing;
Tis there the curlews’ sad notes fall—
Will I return ? . , or am I dying?
Before me on a great bare pine
The parrots rest and harshly scream.
Dying ! . . Despairing thoughts are mine :
No longer can I lie and dream.
The sky is dark ; the Judas-tree
A blood-red blur is slowly growing :
What holds the future, God, for me?—
The parrots scream, “There is no knowing.”
ROBERT ALLAN.
Sydney Jephcott.
“Women and music should never be dated,”
writes gallant Goldsmith in “She Stoops to Con-
quer. Surely his gallantry knocked all solid
value out of the epigram. Music is in a sense
immortal a goddess ever fair and ever young •
and the auld sangs, to which there are no sangs
comparable, have their youth renewed like the
seagull’s with every new generation which sings
them. But women ! —they date themselves; or
.Nature dates them, defying Goldsmith. And
even the wrinkles on the brow of a musical score
are to be counted by a scientific musician. Yet,
so long as it expresses natural emotions, music
should never lie dated. Nor poetry either, with
the same reservation. Homer’s morality is out of
datH, out we fight his battles with ardour, for the
tendency of the natural man is still to fight.
1 ne 27th idyll of Theocritus is still delightful, for
maidsandswainseven nowadaysmakelovejustso—
out of sight of Mrs. Grundy. And, if you look,
it is only the euphuisms, and the preciosities’
and the moralities, the artful and Artless glosses
of poetry, which put it out of date. Wherever a
spring of true lyric feeling has bubbled up in
literature the ground is stid green about it,
though the heart - fountain may have stopped
jetting ten thousand years ago.
* * * *
, mi* 16 l ext sermon comes in the no-date of
lhe becrets of the South: Australian Poems,
by Sydney Jephcott,” issued some half-dozen
years back to an unappreciative public, and still
posted at 3s. 9d. from this office. One wonders
precisely how long Jephcott’s Muse has been
silent (for her utterances since she spoke in
volume-form can be counted on the fingers).
And, so far, she is a Muse of considerably
more promise than performance. “ The Secrets
<>f the South ” are told all in a youthful ferment, a
yeasty whirl of colored verbiage, and when you
sort out the colors and lop off the verbiage there
really isn’t much secret left. As a rule Jephcott
dreams, not thinks; and his dreams, occasionally
pretty things enough, are too lacking in strength
and substance to leave a deep impression. And,
as well as strengthening, he wants clarifying and
harmonising ; and age often brings harmony and
clarity and strength ; —which is where the import-
ance of his date comes in. As we know him at
present he is vague, very vague; rarelv able to
clutch and press home his thought ; often hiding
an ordinary thought in a touzle of fantastic
epithets—which aggravates an earnest reader; and
geneiaUy doing his best to show that Francis
Adams’ praise of him was exaggerated —as it
was, undoubtedly.

This is a sample of the typical Jephcott
LOST.
Lost in Life’s deep-mazed mine, that never
Doth open to a Heaven clear,
We wade above the floods of fear,
Towards the lamp of Love forever
Seeming' to shine so near, so near.
Who heeds these endless excavations,
Hard-won by our laborious breath,
Hewed in the Universe of death ;
Arched in whose adamant the constellations
Shine, gems no steel-stroke looseneth.
But at the central splendor stricken
Still by the missile blaze of Love,
O angels throng Death's sphere above ;
The dense walls 'neath their far dance quicken,
And all with cosmal buoyance move.
But O to find but lampless mazes !
To madden at dark horrors nigh,
To sink against the walls, and lie
With dead face whereon rearing gazes
The light hid at our coming by 1
OUTCAST.
A small fire in its deepest midward burning,
Its dull light trembling up among the trees
That rustle in the icy gushing breeze.
Grey grass around, pervasive darkness urning
The light, beside whose centre sleeps half-shown
Amongst the grey grass, in grey blanket wound,
A pariah shape, while babbles dully round
The streamlet’s modulated monotone.
To sleep to-night among these shivering trees,
In fullness of ail time and travail born,
The man has waded through life’s agonies :
And now alike of hope and use forlorn, ’
Finds but one faithful in his miseries,
This fire—which will be ashes ere the morn.
Alone ! In all the Universe alone 1
In friends, if friends such have, all memories
Have closed o’er his absence like the seas,
Or air his form displaced in years bygone :
0 fire, be fervent in your services,
As Love, Light, Hope, and Angel unto one 1
Reprints.
English Magazine Verse.
0. B. Fry,,writing on “Cricket in ’96” in the New
Review, says “in comparing colonial and English cricket
it must be remembered that we have an enormous advan-
tage in possessing, not only a large leisur.d class, who
can devote all their time and attention to cricket but
also a number of men who make the Fame their
business in life. In Australia there is practically
no leisured class, and apparently there is no professional
cricket.” There must be the same reservation in a com-
parison of Australian and English periodical verse The
number of leisured, cultured" people is much less here
than in England, and maybe rhe proportion is less also.
So that, with a field of possible writers so much larger to
cull from, English magazine verse ought to be of
higher average standard than Australian. In respect of
form, it doubtless is—on the whole. Culture implies
knowledge how to perfect form, and leisure the time to
perfect it. But in respect of poetical message, inspira-
tion and energy, our few verse-writers often seem to get
better results than their rivals on the same plane in Eng-
land. Perhaps this is due, as regards the lower-class
English magazines, to the subordination of everything to
sales. Doubtless Pearson’s Magazine, which gives Kipling
a shilling a word for a story, could afford to buy the best
verse in the market if its readers wanted it. Considera-
tion of the verse which it prints goes to show that its
readers do not want the best verse in the market, but
merely simple little rhymes of sentiment, not too clever
and not too recondite—a kind of sweetmeat for a dull
palate. Thus the difference in quality of Australian and
English verse seems partly referable to a difference of
taste in English and Australian readers—and vanity with
a basis of reason derived from survey of over-the-sea
magas., insists that the general Australian taste is higher
more literary, more truly and intensely poetical. Four
of the more popular English magazines are Pearson’s
the Idler., the English Illustrated, and Ludgate The
character of Pearson’s verse has been hinted.' Sept’
Idler prints only one set of versus—which are neat
rhymes and nothing more. The Englisii Illustrated has
three pieces, which strike a little deeper note, but are in
essence stale and conventional. Here is the shortest :
LOVE AND GRIEF.
Grief raised a tear-stained face to mine—
l-iove, with a smile, looked up ;
I drove Grief harshly from my path,
And drained Love’s sparkling cup.
Languid with sweet satiety
I lay when day was done—
Then lo 1 Love raised her mask, and I
Knew Love and Grief were one 1
M. HEDDERWICK BROWNE.
THE KING OF FAERY.
I am the King of Faery :
A thousand years ago
Mv elfin mother bore me
Betwixt the snow and snow—
My elfin mother bore me,
Lightly as elfins may,
To rule a doubtful country
Between the night and day.
X am the King of Faery,
And wise I am and old,
And of my fairy wisdom
A thousand hands take hold.
But those that seek my helping
Are glad—for all their care :
My thousand years of wisdom
Lie dark upon my hair.
I am the King of Faery
And none there is so gay
Amid my gentle people
That dance the dews away.
I am the King of Faery
And none there is so sad,
Though Una is my lady
And Aodh my serving-lad.
NORA HOPPER.
THE SON.
O why is your hate of me so deep.
F;i t her ?
I grieve so that I may not sleep
For thinking how to turn your heart;
And up in cruel wonder start.
Although this body’s strong to bear,
Father !
I droop and falter round your chair ;
Stand like a woman at your knees,
In deep anxiety to please.
Why is the look refused me quite,
Father!
That on my brother dwells like light ?
At evening when the room is dim
Your shining eyes roam after him.
When I come near your lip is set.
Father!
With something you ean ne’er forget.
What’s vile in him, you think it strong;
What s good in me, you make it wrong.
When you were near to death, you said.
Father !
“ See that he comes not near my bed ! >r
I stood on the dark stair alone,
And swayed about to hear you moan.
Will something ever ’tween us stand,
Father?
Too deep for me to understand ?
Even in the womb you hated me,
When I was dumb and might not see.
0 since I can no way abate,
Father !
Your steady and enduring hate ;
Then strike me dead ! When you shall see
My blood so bright, you’ll pity me.
STEPHEN PHILLIPS.

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A VIGIL.
M.L.
One bird upon the roof,
A chorister forlorn,
Sings to the cloistered morn,
Hid in her cloudy woof,
A song that doth unfold
Itself in plaited gold.
Sing what I ne’;r can say—
The wave may love the shore,
The flowers the dews that pour;
The tired winds love to stay
On cliffs where moss has lain,
Spent with the toiling main.
Dearer to me one heart
Where I would love to dwell,
Woven with magic spell
Into its inner part—
Sunk in its secrecy
Like a star in the sea.
HUBERT CHURCH.
IN A GARDEN.
My garden well remembers
That happy, happy night
When Love’s fast dying embers
Were farm’d again to light.
White roses from the trellis
Droop’d gracefully and low.
O, roses, ye were jealous
Of Mabel’s breasts of snow !
The mellow moonlight lingers
On face and scented hair,
Touching with subtle fingers
Her neck and bosom bare.
My arms again enfold her;
The face of my desire
Leans close against my shoulder
And glows with passion’s fire.
And all my deep resentment
And all my bosom’s pain
Depart, and sweet contentment
Comes to my heart again.
L.D.
THE SONG OF GRACIA.
“And she was called Gracia because she was fairest
among women. And many men sought her, but she loved
one only.”—The Legend of Hezlud.
Spring.
Across the street, across the grass,
Across my life I watch her pass.
No pure star on a dusky height
Hath eyes more bright,
No lily on her bed
A statelier head,
No dewdrop on the beaded thorn
More radiantly doth glow apart !
O, she doth shine all these to scorn !
Fair are they all, flower, star, and dew :
She is the green bud breaking thro’
The winter of my heart.
Two violets, seeking Paradise,
Have hid themselves within her eyes.
Her lips are roses. She doth wear
A sunbeam woven in her hair.
And of the foam-flake of the sea
Her cheek and neck and bosom be.
And like a reed the low wind sways
Her slender figure glides along,
Serenely tall and fairy sweet
In this, the springtide of her days.
And O, to make my life a song
And lay it at her feet.
Summer.
And now the world recedes. Time, Space, are
fleeting.
All things but thee, O Love, have ceased to be.
Here where thy heart against my heart is beating,
And like a charm thy white arms compass me ;
And on thy blue-veined breasts my head is lying,
And all about my face is blown thy hair.
Here let love speak his full heart in sweet sighing,
For speech were powerless now to voice his
prayer.
Softly thy breath, like scent of violets blowing,
Steals o’er my cheek with slow, delicious pain.
Drink whilst thou canst the goblet crowned and
flowing,
For hours like these will never come again.
Autumn.
There is no bud of Spring about this forest,
And in our hearts, too, are the autumn leaves.
Where are the wings, O Love, on which thou
soarest ?
Meeter art thou to toil and bind the sheaves.
But there is mellower light upon our faces,
Thro’ all our veins the steadier currents flow ;
The statelier charms remain and friendly graces,
Tho’ dull and fitful wanes love’s lava-glow.
Winter.
The slow' bell tolls across the square.
She doth not hear its rise and swell.
The frosts of age have silvered there
The clusters of her sun-gold hair.
She sleepeth well.
Strange city echoes here are sent
Of reckless strife for prize and place,
Of hearts with warring passions rent,
But Death’s ineffable content
Is on her face—
A touch, a joy, a something there
That for my sake hath never shone ;
Too w'ell I deem in my despair
Her fairest dream I may not share.
And she is gone
Beyond this earthly azimuth
To those fair stars which poets sing,
Where blooms the tree of fadeless Truth
In gardens of Immortal Youth,
Eternal spring.
Q-
RSSEX EVANS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 7, 1896.
The Queensland Deadlock.
According to latest advices, the deadlock on
the Queensland Federation Bill shows no
signs of improvement, and for the several
hundredth time the question of Australian
unity is hung up indefinitely. The trouble
in the present case is partly the natural
result of the treachery practised by the
Nelson Ministry, and partly the natural
consequence of an unworkable political
system. According to the original under-
standing, each province was to send to a Con-
vention ten delegates elected by the people,
and these delegates, provided they could
arrive at an agreement, were to draft the
Federal Constitution. If they did not
arrive at an agreement, which was at least
possible, there would probably be no Federal
Constitution, at least not till the whole
movement was started afresh from the
beginning. If they did agree but the
result of their labors was afterwards re-
jected by the popular vote of the provinces
there would also be no Federal Constitution.
Considering all the discordant elements in-
volved, it has all along been obvious that
when the Convention meets the Federal
movement will start on a troubled voyage
amid many possibilities of shipwreck. But,
so far, after two years’ labor and confusion,
the movement has not even got to the place
where its perplexities were expected to
really commence. The difficulty of getting
the six sets of delegates and the six Govern-
ments and the six communities to agree on a
form of union was recognised all along, but a
further trouble which was not fully appre-
ciated was the immense difficulty of getting
any Convention at all so that it could meet
together and start disagreeing. The fact
that a gathering of sixty men, repre-
senting six different kinds of provincial
jealousies, six different provinces, each
fully convinced that the other five wanted
to over-reach it and each desirous of over-
reaching the other five, any number of
separate private ambitions, animosities, and
axes to grind, and other conflicting elements,
would find it hard to arrive at any satisfactory
settlement was obvious from the beginning.
The problem of getting the sixty men to
meet was a matter for which no sufficient
allowance was made.
* * * *
The beginning of the Queensland deadlock
was the resolve of the Nelson Government
to entrust the election of the delegates to the
Assembly instead of to the people, as
originally proposed. The Government had
just finished laboriously “ faking ” a general
election, and had come back to power with
its majority practically unchanged. But the
election was won by a stupendous dead lift.
The majority cost even more corruption, hard
lying, judicious disfranchisement of demo-
cratic voters, and “management” in general
than any previous one; it was acquired
with such difficulty that another similar
struggle, in order to secure the re-
turn of ten incurable Tories to the
Convention, was not to be lightly entered
into. The Government-party was resting
from its labors, and the prospect of having
to set to work again and laboriously “ fake ”
the Convention elections was not agreeable.
The proposal that the Tory majority in the
Assembly should simply elect ten delegates
of its own pattern, and leave the people
entirely out of consideration, offered a beau-
tifully simple solution of the difficulty, and
the idea was adopted with acclamation.
But when the Assembly thus stole from
the people their right to a share in the
making of the Federal Constitution, it natur-
ally occurred to the Council that it should
have a share in the steal. The Assembly
distrusted the people because they were in
the main Democratic and could only be made
to return a Tory majority by hard and un-
scrupulous disfranchisement. The Council,
which doesn’t represent the people at all, dis-
trusted the Assembly, which represents them
a little, because it feared that the latter
wanted to steal its authority as well as
that of the public ; because, in fact, it looked
like an Assembly that would steal anything.
Therefore, the Council amended the Bill to
give itself an equal share in the appointment;
and the Lower House disagreed, and the
Upper House refused to consider the dis-
agreement, and the Convention Bill has
grown soiled and frayed since then with being
rushed back and forward from one House to
another to no purpose, and the business gets
no further advanced. The matter which the
measure is supposed to be about has almost
dropped out of sight; the general interest is
centred on catching hold of the mangled car-
case of the scheme the instant it is thrown
over the fence from the other branch of the
Legislature, and throwing it back again with
all possible vigor, and the scheme itself is in
the air most of the time, or else striking the
earth with a thud and rebounding, and the
Federal question has settled down to a
general state of football-scrum and mud-
scramble.
* * * ♦
The difficulty is in one respect opportune ;
it draws special attention to the one thing
which is particularly wanting in the consti-
tutions of all the seven provinces, and which
must be supplied in the Federal constitu-
tion if it is to be a workable piece of
machinery. Not one of the political systems
of Australasia makes any provision for
settling the deadlocks which so frequently
arise under the bi-cameral system. Each
Constitution provides one way in which any
new act of legislation may be effected, by
each Chamber agreeing on it three times in
succession, and the two Chambers taking
exactly the same view of all the details, and
then by the Governor giving his assent and
the British Government not exercising its
power of disallowance. If all these authori-
ties agree on all the details of a measure,
then something may be done. On the other
hand, there are at least seven ways in which
things may be left undone. A new measure
may be rejected in either House, or it may
be left unfinished in either House, or it may
be passed in both Houses with some altera-
tions of greater or less importance, and then
lost because one House dissents from the
other’s alterations. Finally, if it passes both
Houses it may be vetoed by the Governor,
and if it passes the Governor also it may be
vetoed by the British authorities. The two
last-named obstacles, however, seldom create
serious trouble now-a-days ; they exist, cer-
tainly, but they do not constitute an impor-
tant feature of the deadlock. But the two
Houses of Parliament, in most of the Aus-
tralias, live in a chronic state of more or less
violent disagreement. They are, in most
cases, so constituted as to represent conflict-
ing interests in every respect. They are
carefully designed so as to take opposite
views on almost every question of national
importance, and there is no provision what-
ever for holding the balance between them.
Consequently, legislation is a football-match
without an umpire, or a horse-race with-
out a judge, or an arbitration where each
party conducts its own case and there is
no arbitrator to give a decision between
them. The constitution simply provides
that one branch of the Legislature may decide
upon one course and the other branch may re-
solve on another, and then the two parties are
left to shriek at each other indefinitely to no
purpose. The situation is exactly the same as
if the Judge was omitted out of the legal
system, and the plaintiff and defendant were
left to state their cases to each other, and if
they both agreed then a verdict could be
given, and if they didn’t agree then they
were left to state their cases to each other
till they collapsed from old age, or talked
each other to death, or the matter fell
through from exhaustion or paralysis or
similar causes.
In the Queensland deadlock the people are
very little interested in either the Assembly’s
or the Council’s view of the case ; what they
most want is that some kind of a settlement
will be arrived at, and which settlement is
arrived at matters but little. The people do
not elect the Convention delegates either way,
therefore in neither case will these individuals
be anything like fairly representative of the
people. The question really at issue is
whether the delegates will be appointed by
the Tory party in the Assembly, which party
is w'holly out of sympathy with the mass of
the community, or partly by it and partly by
the equally unsympathetic Tory party
in the Council. The people will be
almost equally misrepresented in each
case, therefore they care very little at heart
how the row eventuates. But a considerable
proportion of the people want Federation,
and want Queensland to adhere to the
Convention, and what concerns them is that
the row shows no signs of coming to any
definite result at all. -To them one solution
of the difficulty is about as good or as bad as
the other, but the worst thing of all will
be no solution whatever. The British
legislative system, which gives unlimited
scope for the creation of deadlocks but makes
no provision at all for solving them, here
reveals its weakness most conspicuously.
The community and both branches of the
Legislature profess to desire the same result,
but because the two Houses choose to
squabble over a personal detail, which the
people would probably be equally willing to
settle either way so long as it was settled,
matters are at a standstill.
* * * *
There are various expedients by which the
Convention, if there ever is one, may pro-
vide against such deadlocks in the Federal
Legislature. Some of them are more demo-
cratic or simpler or more workable than
others, and the constitution - makers may
either choose one of the ready-made expe-
dients now in use in various parts of the
world, or construct a new one of their own.
The most essential matter is that there should
be some efficient method of getting a definite
decision on every question submitted to
Parliament. The present system by which
the Legislature is divided into two authorities
almost equal in power, which may, and often
do, take diametrically opposite views on
some detail of a large question, and by
which neither view prevails except by
accident, is cumbersome and stupid and
wholly unsatisfactory. A settlement might
be arrived at by submitting questions on
which the two Houses disagree to a popular
vote. It might also be arrived at by making
the two Houses sit as one for the purpose of
taking a combined vote on any measure or
clause or detail on which agreement cannot
be otherwise arrived at, the decision of the
combined Legislature to be final. A third
expedient is to make deadlocks betiveen the
two Houses impossible by having only one.
Still another device is to give the popular
Chamber power to overrule the Council’s
decision, provided a two - thirds or a
three - fourths or some other specified
majority votes for so doing. Again,
the difficulty may be overcome by giving the
Assembly power to overrule the Council's rejec-
tion or amendment of a measure, provided a
vote to that effect is carried in two successive
Parliaments or in two or more successive
sessions. These are a few of the possible
ways out of the difficulty ; they are not
equally logical or equally politic, but they all
lead to some distinct result, and the worst of
them is better than having no way out of
the difficulty as at present. The policy by
which the legislative power is entrusted to
two separate authorities who are so con-
structed as to think as differently as possible
and to differ as often and as violently as
possible, and by which nothing can be done
unless they think alike, causes waste of time
and energy, and ceaseless friction, and has
nothing save its slowness, indefiniteness, and
exasperation to recommend it.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Permanent and Reproductive.”
During the ensuing year the Government of
New South Wales proposes to spend £2,000,000
of borrowed money on various kinds of works,
and thereby create a fresh outbreak of pros-
perity, and a revival of industry, and all the
other beautiful things that are always done
with borrowed money in the Great Loan Land.
Even according to N.S. W. ideas £2,000,000 is a
tolerably large financial orgy for one year,
though not nearly so large as some past orgies
have been. The expenditure, according to the
Government’s humorous way of putting it, is
for “permanent and reproductive works,”
which presumably means that all the works are
both permanent and reproductive—though it
may be intended to read that some of them are
reproductive and the rest are permanent. A
casual study of the details, however, suggests
that the title is a delusion and the loan policy a
swindle, whichever reading is adopted. Among
the items which are to be paid out of borrowed
money, so as to avoid paying them out of
revenue and thus damaging next year’s surplus,
are “ 1000 magazine rifles, £14,000,” and the
magazine rifle isn’t permanent and was never
accused of being reproductive. Another
£26,000 of “reproductive expenditure” is for
submarine mines and other military equipments;
£25,000 is for the scrub-cutting work which
was invented as a relief for the unemployed ;
£.3690 for a new road at Robertson ; and
£51,000 is for building bridges. Not one of
these items will yield a single penny of direct
revenue, and the Government which calls
them “reproductive” and which describes
scrub-cutting as “permanent” is a most
amazing Ananias. Further on, ,£l] 8,000
is to go in building hospitals, court-houses, luna-
tic asylums, the foundation of Governor Phil-
lip’s statue—think of old gaol-flogger Phillip’s
statue as a reproductive work !—repairs to the
Australian Museum, and the like ; £131,000
goes in non-productive drainage works ; £54,000
for museums, art galleries, &c. ; £6OOO for some
works at Jenolan Caves; and £325,000 for
dredging and other river improvements. Then,
under the head of “ other works,” £6135 is set
aside for wood-blocking King-street, Newtown ;
£56,500 for more bridges ; £49,000 for more
court-houses, gaols, lock-ups, police-stations,
&c., and £3OOO for “the better disposal of
sludge at Cook’s River.” Out of a total of
£2,020,000, something like £1,031,000 is for
works that yield actual revenue, and the con-
struction of which with borrowed money is
therefore more or less justifiable on the ground
that they will repay at least part of the interest
on their cost; £248,000 may be classed as
doubtful; and £741,000 (including the wood-
blocking of King-street, the magazine rifles,
roads, bridges, repairs to roof of Museum, dis-
posal of sludge at Cook’s River, Governor
Phillip’s statue, &c.) is mere degraded swindle
and cooking of the public accounts, whereby
next year’s surplus will be provided for and the
Reid Govt, be enabled to blare once more about
its “ sound finance,” and the “ prosperous state
of the country,” and the way its “policy has
been vindicated.” The nefarious system of
making the surplus or minimising the deficit by
charging works which yield no revenue and
items of casual expenditure to loans has always
prevailed in N.S. Wales, but it never assumed
its most aggravated form till the Reid Govern-
ment came into office. A larger proportion of
the borrowings now goesin non-productive works
than ever before; and all manner of repairs
and patches, which the worst of former Govern-
ments paid out of revenue for want of the
requisite “ cheek ” to do anything else, are now
cheerfully passed on to posterity. The Minis-
try which can charge Governor Phillip’s statue
to loans as a “ permanent and reproductive
work ” should be capable of anything under the
sun in the way of financial dodgery.
Victoria’s Lost Province.
Melbourne Champion wants to know if there
is any way of stopping the exodus from Vic-
toria, and if there is it desires the man who
understands the way to rise up and explain
himself forthwith. The Victorian exodus has
certainly been pretty bad of late years. In
three years—lß92-3-4—the population of the
province only increased by 12,000 ; there was
no actual diminution as is commonly supposed,
but as the increase by the excess of births over
deaths should have been about 60,000, these
figures show that a very large clearing-out has
taken place. Victoria, in fact, has been in a
state of something not much better than stag-
nation, and the Tory press says unanimously
that the reason of it is that the Government
doesn’t go in for “ practical legislation ” —what
kind of practical legislation should be gone in
for the Tory press never states, for the suffi-
cient reason that it doesn’t know. The great
trouble in Victoria now-a-days is actual want
of space, and The Bulletin’s idea of a remedy
is to open up the lost province of Gippsland,
and try to make it as accessible as the
rest of the country, and thereby give the
cramped Gumsucker a little more elbow-room.
Victoria is a very small spot of ground. In
the west and north-west about one-third of it is
a flattened-out and dismal waste of pipe-clay
plains, salt, sand, mallee, and other arid abomi-
nations. There is no permanent water-supply
on these plains ; no artesian water so far as is
known ; practically no population, and no pro-
spect of population that is worth mentioning
until the water - difficulty is settled. The
eastern third of the province is a fertile, well-
watered region which has also no population
worth speaking of, because roads are few' and
bridges fewer, and railways practically none at
all. It has been neglected because the old boom
politicians found it easier to keep building
public works on the central plain than to tackle
this difficult mountain country ; consequently,
the Victorian population is mostly crowded
into one narrow strip of land, and this strip is
about six times as thickly populated as any
other country district in Australia, and its in-
habitants are beginning to slop over into ad-
jacent provinces from sheer pressure. The
Government which sets to work in dead
earnest to open up Gippsland ought to put
an end to the Victorian exodus. In that
region there are four large counties which
are not touched by a railroad, and two
more which have about a dozen miles of line
between them. Victoria is 420 miles long from
east to west, and if one draws a bee-line on the
map westward from Cape Howe it is 220 miles
before it crosses a railroad. There are only
18,000 acres of cultivation in five large counties
of eastern Gippsland : there is no port —the
raw 7 material of three or four harbors is there,
but nothing has been done to improve them ;
and the population is very little more than 0 to
the square mile. Yet Gippsland is about the
most beautiful, probably the best-watered, and
in its valleys almost the most fertile part of
Australia, and if it could be made thoroughly
accessible it would make room for another half-
million Victorians and add a new territory
to the land of the dejected Gumsucker. The
people who want to know how to stop the Vic.
exodus should think over this matter very hard.

The Stinkful Turf.
The Argus recorded some small turf transac-
tions (bogus for the most part) on the night
when Melb. Cup acceptances were published,
under the head of “ Heavy Wagering.” To such
pitiable attempts at working-up a carnival
excitement has “ great ” journalism descended.
There is no possibility of heavy wagering nowa-
days. W. R. Wilson tested the Caulfield Cup
market with a commission to back Resolute for
a pretty big stake, and found to his disgust that
the market was well-nigh exhausted by the
effort of laying £3OOO in all against Resolute at
short prices. A short commission for The
Chevalier for Melb. Cup brought the animal
from 25 to 1 to half the odds immediately. A
genuine investment of “a couple of quid ”

creates a stir among the Hebrew layers these
times, and when one of the smaller fry—how
many larger fry are there ?—lays the odds to a
“tenner” he looks pale and anxious until he
has shared a portion of his risk with a
brother Ikey. If outside punters were
aware of the real financial condition of the
inner racing world they would pay no attention
to the early Cup “betting” which is printed
for their misguiding by sporting editors.
Bogus figures and lying statements are the
rule. A trainer, who hasn’t a feather to fly
with, is reported as winning a “nice stake”
over some horse which he backed for £5 on
“tick.” Stone-broke owners are said to give
their champions a “ spirited support.” As for
the true reason why cronk running is so
general—viz., the inability of jockey-boys to
get the price of their riding fees from anybody
except bookmakers, who will pay them to lose
—it is never even hinted at in turfy columns.
Many jockeys would starve if they were
confined to their legitimate professional earn-
ings, seeing that their impecunious employers
(nominal employers) don’t pay them anything
for months at a stretch. Leaving a few
wealthy or well-to-do owners out of the question,
it may be said that the racing-game is run
almost entirely by bookmakers, the majority of
whom are such small capitalists that they can’t
afford to gamble heavily, and therefore can’t
fix their own intentions until they get a good
idea of how the public sovereigns are likely to
be placed. The Australian racing community
—make no patriotic mistake about it—is about
the most rotten community that exists.
Vic’s Alleged Upper House.
Are the Vic. Consolidated Acts (1890) merely
a text-book by George Higinbotham— only
that and nothing more ? If so, Vic. Legislative
Council is merely the bunghole of a mythical
barrel. Attorney-General Isaacs, in fossicking
out the constitutional position of the Adult
Vote Bill before the Assembly, stumbled on the
paradox that the impregnable eternal Leg.
Council has been recruited for six years by
invalid elections. The Act of ’Bl says that
persons on the voters’ roll may vote at Council
elections without taking out an electors’ right,
and consequently only the few people who vote
at Council elections on some oilier than the
property qualification do take out rights. The
Consolidated Act of 1890 leaves out all mention
of the voters’ roll, therefore under it every
Council elector must have a right. And as the
property voter never has one the inference is
that 144,903 people who thought they were
electors are nothing of the kind, and there
are only 726 real Leg. Council electors
in the province, and some of the fossils
have really no constituents at all, and
the situation is too complicated for ex-
planation. Of course, if the consolidated
Acts are simply the irresponsible private
opinion of George Higinbotham, deceased
(and tlie decision of the Supreme Court in
Mercantile Bank v. Hall has hitherto been
widely accepted as affirming something very
like that), then the Leg. Council can proceed
with its task of thwarting the popular will in
the name of Property. If (in the immortal
language of an Age leader-writer) the unfolded
bombshell proves to be a mare’s nest, then the
48 Men in Possession will continue their watch
and ward in the goods and chattels of Victoria.
But if Isaacs is right, and the Men in Posses-
sion are merely trespassing, then there are
going to be brave times down South. The Age
puts the position in its very mildest form :
It will be an opportunity for the Assembly to exercise
a little gentle pressure. Let the Council pass “ One-man-
one-vote ” and woman’s suffrage, and the Assembly will
readily consent to amend the flaw in the act as far as the
Council is concerned. If the Council prove intractable,
then, apparently there is a means of suspending them
altogether. There is much force in the remark of a mem-
ber of the Cabinet last night that the situation is very
serious.
A irivial price to ask ! Nothing less than the
Referendum would do. But if the Leg. Council
is merely a gang of interlopers, how can it
rat : fy its own existence ? What is the good of
bringing in a bill, as is proposed, to let the 48
say they are really bailiffs and not trespassers?

The Suffield Case.
Thomas Suffield still waits in gaol the tardy
consideration of his case by the N.S. W. Minister
for Justice, and, to all appearance, unless Par-
liament takes a hand in the matter, he is likely
to wait a long time. It is now five weeks since
Minister Gould engaged to lay the papers in
the case on the table of the Leg. Assembly, and
at time of writing they are not yet to hand. In
the ordinary course, papers which are promised
by a Minister are brought forward almost im-
mediately—in a few days, in a week at most.
Why this singular delay in the Suffield case ?
Does the Department feel so guilty—of negli-
gence, or incompetence, or worse ? Or does it
dread the consideration of the case in the
free light of day, and with all the facts
available ? The suppression has a sinister air.
The Bulletin has not yet lost hope, however ;
and meantime it prints some remarks
made by Suffield himself. Suffield, it
will be remembered, was charged with outrag-
ing Croudace’s servant-girl almost under the
windows of her employer’s house at 10 o’clock
in the evening. And he comments acutely :
The Crotjdaces heard shrieks. Did no one else of all
that household hear the shrieks ? There was no evidence
of it. Who then sat up to let the girl in ? The household
must have known she was out. Was she supposed to let
herself in? Then she, a servant of a respectable em-
ployer, had carte blanche to stop out till the small hours.
Did Frank Croudace sit up to let her in ? If he did not,
who did ? And why didn’t the sitter-up (if there was one)
first hear the shrieks ?
Again, Croudace said to his son Frank, “Let’s take
sticks.” Does this not point to some knowledge on the
part of the speaker of the particular kind of danger to be
encountered— viz., from the bull?
Why did the prosecutrix pick upon me? Well, it was
shown that I was about the only man she knew in the
neighborhood. She did not know me, but had seen me
and asked my name. Now, a man named Campbell swore
that he passed prosecutrix on the road on the night in
question, and, if my memory serves me, he said that
“ she stopped, turned round, and had a good look at
him.” Campbell is a man about my own size, and has
something of my appearance. He used to wear a hat
like mine. If prosecutrix met him on that lonely road
in the vicinity of the house where she knew me to be
living, and in the dim semi-darkness of a summer’s night,
don’t you think it probable she would conclude the
passer-by to be Suffield ? Campbell stated in his evi-
dence that he was walking fast.
I am certain prosecutrix mistook Campbell for me;
and, thinking therefore that I was alone on that road
about the time of the occurrence, she felt safe afterwards
in swearing that I was her assaulter.
I was out on the Sunday. Was I watching all day the
trams by which prosecutrix would return from Wallsend ?
If I were not, how could I return home, contemplating
the execution of an outrage, changing my clothes with
the object of intercepting prosecutrix, and hitting the
time of her return exactly, almost to a minute ? Do not
forget, too, that I am supposed to have directed my
step towards Croudace’s house, instead of towards the
bush road, where, were I a brute, I could have accom-
plished a cruel outrage, at the danger of my own life;
and all to procure a passing pleasure which, to put it
bluntly, I could have enjoyed with safety, security, and
reciprocity, any hour of my life, any evening, in Lambton
or in Newcastle.
In view of all the circumstances of the case,
these arguments seem to The Bulletin very
strong in Suffield’s favor. “ I know my own
innocence,” pleads the prisoner, “ but I know
also that I have to convince the public of my
innocence, and so I appeal to the public reason
to say whether on the evidence 1 was fairly
adjudged guilty.”
Lady Tichborne, wife of the present holder of
the title, strikes Australia in the nick of time.
Edward Priestman, who is engineering the
movement to take Cresswell out of Parra-
matta (N.S.W.) Lunatic Asylum, and “restore
him to his rightful place ” as Roger Tichborne,
is just about to move N.S.W. Supreme Court
to deliver Cresswell over, if his pending appli-
cation to Govt, isn’t granted. Lady Tich-
borne can stay and report progress. Priest-
man is badly bitten witii the Tichborne mania,
has spent some £2OO on behalf of Cresswell
already, and has accumulated “proofs”
enough to fill a clothes-basket. Also, he
is armed with a power of attorney
from a R.C. clergyman in England who
was a great friend of Roger Tichborne’s
mother; and is prepared to plank down £SOO
as guarantee that Cresswell will be well kept
and safely returned if the clothes-basketful of
proofs shouldn’t be sufficient. That being so,
there seems no reason why N.S.W’. Govern-
ment should hang on to Cresswell any longer.
He may be Tichborne, or he may not: it isn’t
any business of Govt.’s. All Govt, has to con-
sider is that a respectable person is willing to
take the cost of Cress well’s keep off the per-
spiring taxpayer ; and if Govt, could turn over
every harmless inmate in the asylums to friends
on similar terms it would make a very good
bargain.
If the reports as to night-sittings presided over
by N.S.W. minor Judge Docker are correct—it
is stated that juries in criminal cases have been
sitting before His Honor for 18 hours contin-
uously and not rising till 3.45 a.m. ! —the Judge
should at once be removed from the Bench.
After the gross scandals incidental to the reign
of the unspeakable Judge Windeyer, surely
the people of N.S. Wales do not contemplate
setting up a tinpot imitation of the man from
Tomago ! Before entering upon the protracted
night-sittings complained of His Honor “ put it
to the jury ” whether they would adjourn or con-
tinue. Judges know, or should know, that the
hearing of a criminal charge is not for the con-
venience of the jurors, is not for the convenience
of the judge, but is for the extension of a fair
trial to the accused. These are the three courses
open—(l) To dismiss or otherwise regulate Mr.
Docker ; (2) to pass an eight-hour sitting bill
with regard to criminal trials ; (3) to enact that
in cases where juries are being Dockered, they
shall, at intervals of four hours during every
night-sitting, receive hypodermic injections of
cocaine sufficient to brace them up for the occa-
sion. However, while any person of the Gould
type poses as Minister of Justice, it is hopeless
to effect a radical remedy.
Most of the Sydney “tote” and “double”
shops are in full blast at present, and have
been that way for a long time past, and the
weary policeman w r hose business it is to raid
them and make the owners sit up cometli not.
A few weeks ago the police, knowing that the
law was being habitually and wilfully broken,
and having the names and addresses of the
people who were doing the breakage, went
round and didn’t arrest them, but gently
warned them not to do it any more. Since
then they have mostly kept on doing it, and
the policeman, apparently, hasn’t been around
again. Also the GoD-fearing Ministry doesn’t
seem to have inquired why the police failed to
do their duty and run in these persistent law-
breakers instead of giving them a friendly
warning that they might dodge the due conse-
quences of their misdoings, and wdiy they keep
on not doing their duty, and why the depart-
mental bosses don’t make them do it, and how
much is paid for this immunity, and who gets
the payment, if any. Also the officer who so
badly misunderstoorl his duties as to go Tound
paying friendly calls on the malefactors and
giving them the “ tip ” doesn’t seem to have
been sacked, and his superior, who presumably
sent him on his unlawful errand, is still, so far
as the public are aware, enjoying his billet and
his salary in peace. Wherefore The Bulletin
sadly opines that the Stiggins Government
is a sham and a humbug, and backs doubles on
the sly when its colleague, Local Preacher
Cook, isn’t looking.
The arguments by which North Melb. worsted
Prahran in the recent A.N.A. debate are good
enough for standards. “The magic of private
property in land,” said Prahran, “enables
men to cultivate the barren rock, and turn the.
sandy desert into a garden.” “ Why,” en-
quired N. Melb., “ should men be forced to cul-
tivate the barren rock and sandy desert when
good, arable land lies idle in the clutches of
monopoly ? The magic of private ownership
is the magic of monopoly, which does not enable
but forces men to cultivate the rock and deserts.
There is truly a magic in private ownership of
land, for by what other name could you call
that power which forced Ireland to send, during
a period of famine, more than five times the
produce necessary to feed her people to pay
rent to the absentee landlord? That truly is
magic which has turned the erstwhile smiling
glens of Scotland into a habitation for wild
beasts—which enabled the Marquis of Stafford
to supplant 15,000 small farmers by 39 sheep
farmers. Compared with this nmgic the blackest
arts are virgin white.” “But,” said Prahran,
“Vic. Govt, has failed to collect the rents of
the land it already holds.” N. {Melb. retorted
that the Govt, has collected more than the rents
of the Mallee settlers : what it has failed to do
is to collect their purchase money. “As long
as they pay rent they are surely entitled to
occupy.” “ And,” said Prahran, “Vic. Govt,
has failed to manage the railways satisfactorily.”
To which N. Melb. responded that Vic. rail-
ways pay working expenses and 2 3-4 per cent,
on cost of construction. The cost of construc-
tion was largely the cost of buying back, at
fabulous prices, the land upon which to build
the railways. “ The railway muddle is not
because the Govt, owns the railways, but
because the Govt, does not own the land.”
Truly a neat antithesis.
The following interesting comparative table of
the pawnbrokers’ charges allowed in England
under an Act of George 111. and at pre-
sent, also in Victoria, is taken from a
cogent article on “ Usury and Pawnbrokers’
Charges” in the current number of Austra-
lasian Law Times :
The Victorian Act has not been altered since
1865, and in the forthcoming legislation re
usury, Victoria will be acting tenderly to thou-
sands of her most helpless people, if she will
in this matter follow in the footsteps of effete
and feudal England.
Considering how much has been heard about
that alleged quality of “ British fair-play,” and
how constantly John Bull rises to state, on his
own authority, that this spirit of fairness and
impartiality is his most beautiful characteristic,
a recent cablegram from Hingland is tolerably
sickening. A Transvaal gentleman was in-
vited, or at least permitted and authorised, to
deliver a lecture before the Imperial Institute.
Then, having gothim on the platform, the audi-
ence drowned the lecture in howls of “ traitor,”
“cur,” “ruffian,” and similar hog-language,
the reason of the outbreak being that the lec-
turer had “sympathised” with the Boers,
when buccaneer Jim raided their country, and
hadn’t whooped for the invading filibuster as
the meeting considered he should have done.
In addition to heaving language at the lecturer
the hysterical crowd waved flags promiscuously
and cheered for Jameson, and came within an
ace of winding up the proceedings with a free
fight and a general shedding of nose-blood all
over the premises. This rowdy incident
seems to show that a gathering of cul-
tured Englishmen can be just about as black-
guardly as a South American mob when
once it gets on its patriotic ear. Also, it sug-
gests that the cultured Briton-has just the same
idea of fair-play as anybody else—in ether
words, his idea is that everybody must think
the way he thinks or have a brick thrown at
him. Further, the strong, calm Anglo-Saxon
who objects so much to the hysterics of other
races appears to be as frantic and childish as
other people when his own particular corn is
trodden upon. Supposing “Dr. Jim’’had been
a Boer who carried invasion and butchery into
Natal, what would his neck have been worth,
and how much applause would he have got
from the fair-minded Englishman ? And how
does the Englishman’s enthusiasm over
Jameson because he illegally killed a good
many Dutchmen compare —in a spirit of British
fair-play—with his fury against Major Lothaire
who illegally killed one Englishman ? And
when the Englishman whoops over one butcher
and the Belgian cheers for the other, how much
is there to choose between the hysterics of the
two races ?
The Tas. Great Western debate produced an
unexpected consequence. The shameless Bill
having passed its second reading earlier than
was expected, the members opposing it an-
nounced their intention of stonewalling in com-
mittee, and accordingly began to do so on Oct.
29, at 7 p.m., kept it upall night, and continued
till about 12.30 on Friday, Oct. 30. Then
Henry Dobson arose to move “ that the
question be put,” in accordance with the
rule of the House of Commons and under the
rule of the Tas. .Standing Orders, which provides
that any applicable rule of the Commons not
on the Tasmanian Orders (if on the Commons
Orders when Tas. Orders were last revised)
shall be extended to Tasmanian procedure. The
Acting-Chairman ruled that the motion was
regular, and put it. It was carried on division,
amid prolonged yells of “ Gag ” from the Oppo-
sition. Thus (in the interests of a gridironing
syndicate) was the closure introduced into
Tasmanian politics.
4
Vic. Radicals have always feared that the
politics of Attorney-General Isaacs, “ like the
morality of a bishop,” were too good to be true.
In 1892, as a Pattersonite, he supported the
dual vote, &c., but in 1893, when he danced on
the ruins of Patterson, he hauled up the
Radical flag, and has since posed as an opponent
of boodling bankers and company-swindlers
generally. He has brilliance and energy, and
is a nightmare to the Tories. Opportunist or
not, the gibes of such men as G. D. Carter
rouse his fighting spirit, and force him to nail
his flag to the mast. The wounded eagle felt a
deeper pang to see its own feather on the fatal
arrow—and perhaps some day the reactionaries
of Vic. will shudder to see that every nail
fastening the Isaacs flag is a Tory taunt.
Vic. Artists’ Society.
The Vic. Artists’ Society is holding another an-
nual exhibition of paintings at its ill-fated gallery
in Albert-street, Melbourne. Regarded as a shil-
ling show, this exhibition is seemingly the direst
failure ever achieved by the Society—a “ frost ”of
“ frosts.” In artistic merit, or pretension, it is not
so far below the average of recent years as to
account for the increased indifference of the general
public. Evidently Melbourne has wearied of local
art-eccentricities, or else the humble necessary
shilling charged for admission is now felt to be
more than the sentiment of culchaw can afford-
One wouldn’t care to pay more than a dozen pence
for sole possession of some of the new works that
figure in an annual exhibition of Victorian paint-
ings. Why not fix the gate-money at a threepenny-
bit and implore the masses to roll up ?
* * * *
From this disconsolate gathering of canvasses,
mostly too large for the subjects depicted on them,
works by Longstaff, John Forde Paterson, D.
Davies, and Douglas Richardson are conspicuously
missing. The last-named artist has in former
years usually contributed some efforts that aimed
at the sublime, and, though they did perhaps
sometimes hit the ridiculous, The Bulletin is
none the less sorry at finding their author unrepre-
sented on this melancholy occasion. Among the
few well-known workers who do exhibit, Mr.
Tucker appears to have a place of honor for his
“ Confidences ” —a girl in a limp white robe and a
very questionable frame of mind, resting her head
upon the shoulder of a nice, sympathetic matron.
Nobody, save the artist, can tell whether the letter
which the damsel holds in her hand is an offer
of matrimony, a “breaking-off” epistle, or a
threatening demand from the milliner. She may
be sad or happy. Anyway, the matter is serious.
There is much conscientious painting in sundry
parts of the picture, but the girl’s pose is mysteri-
ous. She may be sitting on a stool. It is impos-
sible to say how she is disposing of her legs, or
whether she has any human legs at all under the
white robe. Bernard Hall shows, in a small
frame, a nicely-drawn and tinted nude figure of a
not well-formed female— i.e., nicely-drawn down
to the feet, which are probably too large and ugly
to be true even to the model. She is entitled a
“ Nymph,” and the subject, against a dark-green
background, is certainly chaste in effect. All the
same, it is disillusioning. J. C. Williamson would
probably hesitate before engaging such a nymph
for tights.
* * * *
F. M'Cubbin’s “ Winter Evening ” has a study
of scattered ti-trees on a piece of sandy cliff with
a gaily variegated sea and sky beyond. Artistic,'
but unconvincing. The tree trunks are the color
of plum juice, also the sand. Nos. 69 and 21 are
lamentable works by Walter Withers, in painful
contrast with his more ambitious “ Storm,” a wet
and windy landscape that commands praise. Here
everything is well-placed and in harmony with
nature. Flagrant examples of Vic. art-coloring
(picked at random from many such) are No. 70,
an old man’s head, and No. 33—“ Beach at Hamp-
ton.” The latter, painted by Handasyde, is
praiseworthy in most things save color, which is
the thing of chief importance. J. C. Waite shows
a heartily-welcome “ Australian Sunset" —sheep
browsing on a glorified grassy plain, a common-
place style of picture, unduly large for the theme,
but essentially pretty and quite free from extrava-
gances. The bizarre influence of Holman Hunt
is suggested by one among sundry sea and shore
pictures by Mather, whose work as a rule seems to
be waning in naturalism.
» * * *
The self-assurance of young Victorian “ art ” is
typified in No. 28, a smudgy, hideous “impres-
sion ”of a child in a pinafore. This is called “A
Wdd Rose” on the frame, and the painter has
attached a verse to the title. Apart from its pre-
sence on the frame this verse has no visible refer-
ence to the child, but it speaks eloquently of the
artist’s regard for that awful infant. Several
works from Sydney—including Tom Roberts’
hard, stiff, ludicrous “Bailed Up”—are displayed
among the aforesaid local productions in oil, but it
is in the water-color department that visiting
talent is best asserted.
“The Vagabond.”
Dear Bulletin, —In the rush of obituary matter
on the late Julian Thomas there is scarcely amen
tion of the man’s generous and kindly nature. As
one who knew him well give me space for a word
or two on the subject. When Miss Sutherland
started rescue-work amongst neglected children in
Victoria she got her first donation—.£lo —from
“ The Vagabond.” To young writers seeking his
aid or advice he was a tower of strength. A story
of suffering humanity never failed to bring tears
to his eyes and money from his purse. It was as
much due to his (often misdirected) charity as to
his unlucky mining ventures in Kimberley and
Queensland that “The Vagabond ” died so'poor.
The nuns of Abbotsford Convent and the Little
Sisters of the Poor for many years offered
prayers for the spiritual welfare and ultimate con-
version “to our Holy Church ” of the famous
journalist who had so often aided them. Now the
gentle creatures are doubtless putting up petitions
to get his soul out of Purgatory. An Argus
leader once referred to “The Vagabond ” as a man
whom children and dogs instinctively followed.
And “The Vag.” himself was fond of telling how,
at his first interview with editor Haddon, the
latter’s Gordon setter —a most conservative animal
—after a short inspection of the visitor, came for-
ward and laid a paw on his knee. “By George ! ”
exclaimed the editor, “now I know you are a
good fellow.” Strolling with him one day, we
came upon a dog which had been run over by a
vehicle and left dying. “The Vagabond” took
the creature in his arms, carried it to a chemist’s
and spent a half-crown, which he could ill-afford,
to give it a painless death by chloroform. The
evening before his death, when driving in from
Heidelberg, he overtook a poor woman and her
ragged offspring trudging ahead. They were
given a place in “ The Vag.’s ” cab and driven to
their destination. Small as these items are,
they indicate a very gentle aspect of a
strong man’s character. Amongst the obituary
Vagabondia in The Bulletin, there appeared
the statement that certain indications pointed
to a remote infusion of African blood. This is
news indeed to the “Vag.’s” friends! And the
portrait which accompanies the ably-written sketch
conveys a most unflattering and vulgarised idea of
Julian Thomas’ powerful if irregular personality.
I have always understood that he was a Vir-
ginian by birth. He certainly knew the country
minutely. He also knew stony-hearted Fleet-
street to his sorrow. I take it that his
early youth was, in a sense, a bitter one,
probably through narrow religious training.
“ Childhood’s mental suffering,” he used to say,
“is terrible because of its impotence.” When
“ The Story of an African Farm ” was read aloud
to him, the passages descriptive of the spiritual
stress affected him most painfully. He had been
through every phase of the agony and emerged
an unbeliever, constantly reiterating the last four
verses of the third chapter of Ecclesiastes. The
serial story, “Under a Changing Sky,” begun
years ago by “The Vagabond” in the Pacific
Weekly, was, he said, fairly auto-biographical as
far as it went. But it did not go very far. The
paper died in its earliest youth. Though ill-
health and restricted means made pathetic “ The
Vagabond’s ” later years, a little group of friends
which he had grappled to him in better days
stuck closer to him than kindred might, and many
a man who passes away surrounded by a host of
blood relations is less deeply mourned than he who
was Julian Thomas. —]

Exhibition Building Carnival of All Nations.
That fine olde Englyshe comedy, “ She
Stoops to Conquer,” which was written away back
in aboriginal times by an almost forgotten person
named Goldsmith, was the first production of the
new Potter-Bellew season at Sydney Lyceum.
The results may be set down as satisfactory, inas-
much as Mrs. Potter wasn’t nearly so extraordinary
as usual, and her rendering of the heroine wasn’t
by any means the blaze of painted anguish that
most of her heroines amount so. This doesn’t
imply that the rendering was positively good, but
it didn’t come within gunshot of being as bad as
many other things. The heroine was a nicely-
dressed girl with only a moderate amount of affec-
tation, artificial in a good many places, graceful
in some ; a girl who for once didn’t wriggle to any
extent worth mentioning, and, taking her all
round, a good second-rate Miss Hardcastle, with
the paint on her countenance put on in a way that
was reasonably artistic for Mrs. Potter, though it
would have seemed rather startling in a good
many other ladies. The Bulletin can re-
member at a moment’s notice at least six
actresses well-known in Australia who would
have done the part better, and about thirty
who would probably have done it a lot worse.
There are no overwhelming opportunities in the
character of Marlow, but Kyrle Bellew played it
well and brightly, and, but for the whitewash-like
pallor which he is so fond of wearing on his stage-
countenance, there would be no ground for objec-
tion to Marlow anywhere. Tony Lumpkin, by
C. M. Berkeley, was a fine, robust lout, whose
whoop will linger about the rafters of the Lyceum
for weeks to come ; the old reliable Cathcart was
as relia le as ever ; and Miss Linda Raymond, as
Constance Neville, was a pleasant picture to look
upon, and a lady that any right-minded young
man of these days would have eloped with at
sight. Also, Oily Deering deserves mention as a
most effective lout—there are nine louts altogether
in the comedy, and Deering was the most com-
bustible-looking one of the crowd. As for Hast-
ings, he is nothing in particular, except another
nice young man like Marlow, only with a more
serious turn of mind, and with a different colored
coat and long boots of another hue, and he calls
for no special mention.
* * * *
Goldsmith’s comedy was staged for three
nights only, and “Romeo and Juliet,” also for
three nights, was announced to begin on Wednes-
day.
#* * *
Bland Holt’s season at Sydney Royal
closes at the end of next week, when the co,
departs for the city of the unreliable bank balance-
sheet down by Yarra’s smellful shore. The pre-
sent show, so far as is known just now, will fill
in the rest of the season well enough, though the
question why Norman doesn’t suicide as he says
he will seems to come no nearer an answer, and the
fact still sticks out like the lies in a prospectus that
the author has forgotten to mention whether Miss
Vivienne is hanged for shooting the other lady or
not. Also, if she is hanged, what kind of dress
does she wear on the scaffold, and does she break
her swan-like neck when she drops, or has the
executioner to dive down below and hang on to
her feet to finish the tragedy? One thing that is
becoming conspicuous about this co. is that Baker
badly wants a rest from playing the hero. The
hero is practically the same in every melodrama.
He isn't allowed to be amusing for the sake of his
soulful dignity, and he is so beastly good that he
doesn’t get a show to be decently original, and he
can't break things or go out fishing on Sundays
because it is wrong, and he isn’t even permitted as
a rule to be reasonably active, because it wouldn’t
seem to fit in with the Sabbath calm in his soul.
Baker’s talents are wasted as the depressed prig of
melodrama, and if he doesn’t get a chance to be a
soulless murderer now and then for change of air,
decay will assuredly set in.
* *
The drama with the glaring boa-constrictor
in it is doing so well at Her Majesty’s that it will
rage and explode and combust until further
notice. A slight hitch which took place the first
night somewhat damaged one of the sensations—
the scene was changed so hurriedly that Miss
Fanny Wiseman, who was just bursting through
the window in Chinese costume to fight the boa at
close quarters with a knife, hadn’t time to pub-
licly demolish that long, curly intruder, and had
to obliterate him privately behind the scenes.
This has since been remedied, and the boa is now
wiped out in the presence of witnesses, and his
assassin afterwards remarks “ Hoo-ki-lah,” or
some equally unreliable statement. The electrical
machine, The Bulletin is happy to say, has
worked satisfactorily all through. The villain
signs a cheque for the blackmail on a curiously-
contrived desk, and leaves the document on
the exact spot where the button is, and the victim
going to pick it up sets the monster in motion,
consequently a globe of fire the size of a bullock’s
head arises suddenly and blows him over. He
hasn’t time to make any remarks, but simply
shuts up like a well-worn telescope when it is
stood up on end. The only pity is that some way
can’t be found to make use of the furnace in which
the bad man proposes at one time to insert his
young wife alive. If the lady could be shoved
into it for just a moment, and then be rescued
with her hair and her skirts on fire, and her eye-
brows partly destroyed, it would be an effective
scene. But even the boa-constrictor drama has its
limits, and the lady can’t well be thrown to the
furnace and the serpent at the same moment.
* * * *
Last week the Sydney Opera Society rose
on two successive nights and perpetrated “ Mari-
tana,” at Sydney Lyceum, in the presence of an
audience which might have been a good deal
larger than it was. It is only due to the society
to state that it did much less injury to “ Mari-
tana ” than has been achieved at various times by
quite a number of organisations, and the lady has
so far recovered from her wounds as to stand being
repeated at the Criterion on Saturday and Mon-
day. At the same time, the public encourage-
ment given her was hardly sufficient to justify her
in doing herself again at such short notice, and
after Monday it might be well if she retired for a
considerable time. The fact that she wasn’t par-
ticularly bad from an amateur standpoint doesn’t
necessarily imply that there is a great immediate
demand for “ Maritana ” in large quantities,
which, as a matter of fact, there isn’t.
* * * *
Rickards’ new variety artists continue to
drop in at intervals, the latest being Miss Minnie
Goepel and the Allisons. These new arrivals,
having been duly sampled by the Tivoli patrons,
have apparently been approved of. As Coleman
and Miss Alice Leamar are still lingering at the
Tivoli, the co. at present is even stronger than
usual, and sundry other people are due shortly.
