* * * *
“ Henry V.,” produced on the suitable
stage of Melb. Royal, is a spectacle which does
credit to George Rignold in three respects. Firstly,
it shows his grit in risking so large a show at the
tail-end of a dull season ; also it testifies to his un-
rivalled skill —as far as this country is concerned—
in stage-management ; likewise it re-asserts his
ability to play the name-part with fine effect. To
see and hear George Rignold play Henry Vee at
the Opera House, a dozen years ago, was to feel
inclined to raise one s hat to him ever afterwards.
To repeat that experience at the larger theatre
to-day, is to feel capable of lifting the hat higher
than before. Rignold, of course, is not the fighting
monarch that Shakespeare drew. The Bard lived
in the times of fighting monarchs, and having a
truthful idea of what they were like on an average,
his imagination didn’t soar to the height of this
modern presentment of Henry V. No king could
be half as regal and heroic as Rignold looks.
Human nature doesn’t run to it. Neither does
spectacular-acting capacity run to it in the case of
anybody save “ ’Andsome George,” as far as The
Bulletin is aware. All his mannerisms make for
this particular stage character, and a good deal of
his youth comes back to him when he speaks the
old familiar lines. A naturally-picturesque per-
formance, and therefore not half appreciated.
Probably no artist ever did so well for so little
applause as Mr. Rignold received last Saturday
night. The house was full, and the actor kept the
people in their places until long past their usual
time for stamping off to catch a tram, but they
didn’t, for the most part, know why they were stay-
ing so late. They were interested in the staging of
the show without understanding that the man
created their interest. Or if they understood they
forgot to clap their hands accordingly. George
got one hearty recall during the evening—after the
startling tableau of Agincourt, in the centre of
which he slew the sham rider of a bogus steed.
The impression that King Henry had really killed
the effigy was too strong to be resisted, and they
clamored for his Majesty to come forth from be-
hind the curtains.
* * • *
The Battle of Agincourt aforesaid, the
triumphal return of Henry to London, and the
Cathedral at Troyes form the three special effects
of scenery and supers, whilst several other situa-
tions are almost as imposing. The effect of George
Rignold and his persuasive elocution governs all
these. A long cast of characters includes J. W.
Sweeney, Frank Harcourt, Hazlitt, Stirling
Whyte, and the full strength of the co., several
members being required to appear twice in
different disguises. The sum total of their minor
achievements is fairly satisfactory. It might be
rather less satisfactory, and still the show would
be all right, because it is a one-man show, and
that central individual is an all-round success.
* * #
“Djin Djin,” at the Princess’s, is under
the disadvantage of being revived at a moment
when parents, as a rule, are not taking any Xmas
pantomime. Its magnificence hasn’t faded, but
the glow of novelty is no longer there and the new
people appear to represent a falling-off from the
original, owing to the fact that their jokes have
not had time to drop out of recollection. “ Djin
Djin,” all the same, is quite likely to flourish a
second time. The alluring Lumiere cinemato-
graphe, brought into the third act, was warmly
received on Saturday, and this one item promises
to go for much.
* * « *
On Saturday the Brough Comedy Co. put
in a first appearance at Melb. Bijou, where the
B. & B. firm of old had made a local habitation
and a name. Brough’s reconstructed company,
judging from a furious attack upon the box-plan
before the season started and the roaring of the
multitude at “ A Mother of Three ” after the cur-
tain went up, will bring popularity back to the
Bijou and restore the partially-unclad bulwarks of
society to its dress-circle. “ A Mother of Three,”
as Bulletin readers have already learned, is a
humorous farce, written by a lady, whose jokes
were conceived in a strictly improper spirit.
The only hope of any improvement in the moral
tone of feminine humor rests with the emancipated
spinster who goes scorching on a bike. Miss Clo
Graves being doubtless a lady of the shrinking
violet type, her farce resembles the deep blue sea
in breadth and color.
♦ * * *
Mrs. Brough, in the character of a mother
of triplet daughters (the title, “ A Mother of
Three,” is a concession to male prejudices), dis-
guises herself as her long-lost husband for the sake
of her dear girls. Th w need a father’s presence to
assist them into matrimony, therefore Mrs. Brough
impersonates their other parent and takes up a
commanding position in trousers. This craze for
connubial bliss on the part of the triplets, who have
each been engaged to innumerable young men, is a
physiological enigma, seeing that papa is wedded
to science in Peru whilst their mother has remained
a chaste grass-widow ever since her honeymoon,
eighteen years ago. Papa returns from Peru,
sorry for having neglected his wife so long,
at the very moment when the lady gets into
the clothes he left behind him. Circumstances,
therefore, compel the scientist to regard his dis-
sembled spouse with a dreamy eye of suspicion.
She seems to have taken his vacant place and led
herself astray, the apparent results of her indis-
cretion being two of the triplets whose birth
wasn’t mentioned to their absent father at the
time. “ A Mother of Three ”is more complicated
and many degrees giddier than “ Charley’s Aunt.”
In fact, a suggestion of possibility would make the
plot almost indecent. As the matter stands, it is
very funny.
* * * *
Mrs. Brough achieves the main object,
which is to win laughter for her extravagant cari-
cature of a she-male in distress. Brough, as the
staggered innocent from Peru, gives valuable sup-
port, also Titheradge and Miss Watt-Tanner.
The triplets—Miss Hardy, Miss Austin, and Miss
Lily Titheradge—are successful in seeming young
and anxious to get married, and a stuttering suitor
is neatly done by Majeroni. Here and there a
touch of nature is introduced by a grimy servant
gal. Miss Temple acts the harmless necessary
drudge in question, and acts her well.
*# * *
Frequent changes of bill at the Alexandra
are seemingly powerless against the blight which
has begun to settle upon Dam pier’s season. Sooner
or later the Haleck is always overtaken by the
same old blight, so unless the management can
invoke a miracle the present season may as well
break off suddenly and leave inevitable emptiness
to take its course, The Bulletin fears.
** * *
A rival dramatic power has arisen unex-
pectedly at the little Cambridge Theatre, where
Dan Barry is giving them something peppery at
the lowest possible prices, and the prospects of
both rival powers are about equally unpromising.
Why this awful superfluity of Melb. shows? A
Vitascope is appealing for patronage at the
Athenaeum Hall, and a mysterious irrelevant ad-
vertisement announces that “ Knowles, the famous
London comedian,” has arrived. [Tatt. Adams’
new Sydney theatre not yet being ready to receive
the artists who are arriving from London under
engagement, they are to play awhile in Melb.]
** * *
A Grand Carnival of All Nations com-
menced to rage mildly at Melb. Exhibition Build-
ing last week. With the assistance of warm
weather the Carnival may be expected to go on
raging for some time under substantial Hebrew
patronage. The manager’s name is Israel, and
the prettiest daughters of that famous tribe lend
enchantment to the scene in connection with
raffles and race-games and refreshment-tents
where coffee is only sixpence a cup and
the smile of the amateur waitress costs
nothing at all except a jealous pang when
she beams upon the next customer. The
less fickle attractions of the Carnival are sup-
plied by Fitzgerald Bros. The old reliable
circus performs twice daily in the quadrangle, and
a large collection of beasts of prey—including poor
young John Burns, the lion, who is fast going
blind —clamours for raw meat in an adjoining
tent. Furthermore, the uncanny contortionists
and the lady on the swirling globe, supported by
horse Mahomet and other specialities, give two
entertainments per diem on a platform in the
centre transept. Above all, there is Mdlle.
Antonio, who supports herself at a dizzy height
under the dome until it is time to drop. Then
the lady ascends a few more flights of space
and drops 170 feet into her net in the old sweet
way that passeth all understanding.

A crippled youth—he lost a leg at Redfern railway-
station—who some years ago made a pittance as a street-
whistler in Melbourne—and he whistled exquisitely—is
now a recognised professional sijtleur in America and
commands 70 and 80dol. a week in the music-halls.
J. E. Sykes was the first of the “ finished musicians” to
tackle Sydney Town Hall organ, as the Mayor put it, and
Ernest Truman has followed suit. There is much weep-
ing and gnashing of teeth among the local organists, who
are besieging the city aldermen with “statements of
claim.” What the upshot of these appeals will be is only
a matter for conjecture.
It is said that Trebelli required much pressing to give
her two final concerts in Sydney. It was only in response
to a requisition signed by many influential ladies that the
soprano expressed her willingness to warble once more.
Miss Dora Ohlfsen-Bagge, the pianiste, daughter of the
well-known Sydney engineer, has been obliged to give up
work a-while to recruit her health. She left some time
ago for Berlin, and hard study is said to have affected the
somewhat fragile player. Miss Bagge was a superb artiste
before she left Sydney ; and, unless the writer is much
mistaken, she will ultimately be accounted the finest in-
strumentalist who ever left our shores.

rival. She described Maxine, prior to the goddess's
advent, as “ a dream of beauty.”
The Frantz Family resolved to learn “biking,” and in
a body presented themselves at Apthorpe’s school, Melb.
They mastered the machine in one lesson, much to the
amazement of other biking tyros.
The wealthy young Englishman to whom a handsome
and tuneful Victorian-born soprano has promised her
hand is not a talkative lover. He is alleged to look at
her steadily for hours together, thinking unutterable
things. The other day the engagement was understood to
be “ off.” Now matters have resumed their placidity.
Sydney Zoo is to be opened on Sunday afternoons to the
public. As a concession to public hypocrisy cash is
not taken at the gates—tickets must be bought in town
beforehand.
Melbourne Bijou, as it now stands, is a very dainty
theatre. It has undergone much beautifying for the
Brough season, and all the colors used in the decorations
are of the palest and most delicate tints. The co. always
seemed much more at home at the Bijou than at the
Princess’s, which splendid theatre fitted their small-cast
plays about as well as St. Peter’s at Rome would fit a
quakers meeting.
Little jokes about Sarah Bernhardt’s figure, published
in English papers, show that the writers have got their
present notions of the tragedienne from days of yore.
This sort of airy comment still crops up frequently:
“Madame Bernhardt says that if she had her choice of
death she would prefer to be struck by a meteor. It is
not, perhaps, rude to suggest that the average meteor
would want something meatier than golden-voiced Sarah,
or it wouldn’t get on the mark at all.” The supposition
that Sarah is as thin as she used to be a quarter of a cen-
tury ago, when her “ bones ” were a cosmopolitan, jest,
still holds good among people who haven’t seen her in the
meantime, if they ever saw her at all. Australia found
the French actress a plump genius, taking pains to make
her figure look less substantial than it really was.
H. : “ Fact copies Fiction. Taking up ‘ The Business ol
Pleasure,’ a book by Edmund Yates, published 1865, I find
a story about an imaginary actor named Dacre, which
bears a startling and most uncanny resemblance to that
of the real actor of that name. The description given of
the man’s appearance and temperament is positively the
same. The Dacre of the yarn is similarly baffled on the
stage. He does not suicide, but dies in a miserable way,
and the actual Dacre appears to be struggling up in every
detail.”
Super. Booker, of Adelaide Fire Brigade, has startled
the local public by reporting that the Exhibition Building
is very badly provided with means of egress in case of
fire. “It can only be compared to a huge matchbox
which, once ignited, nothing short of submersion would
extinguish.” Nor were the patrons of places of public
amusement re-assured when the Architect-in-chief re-
torted that the Exhibition Building is better provided
against an alarm of fire than is the Town Hall.
Lord Fin gal and Myring, the two boss promoters
of the Plunderderry, are said to be the backers of
the man running the drama, “ The Duchess of Cool-
gardie,” in which Hilda Spong is the heroine, in London.
Then why was it not called “ The Duchess of London-
derry ”? Has the “dramer” got a scene in which an
Earl arrives from London to take out of the golden hole a
few tons of gold, removes the iron fence, puts in some
shots, fires them, goes back to pick up the “blasted”
treasure and, to his horror, finds nothing but “ white
quartz”? Hero fiabbergastered, dunno where ’e are—
hardly responsible for his actions, blamed for cabling the
cruel truth to London, instead of sending a private cable
having a few days’ start to enable others to scoot. While
in great despair, is taken out to see the Wealth of Ex-
pectations mine, finds that much worse, and, having no
interest, is instantly cured of despondency and lives the
life of a cheerful Earl ever after. If there is no such
scene, The Bulletin is afraid the dramer will be a failure.
No Coolgardie play is complete without it.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The feeble little pretence at trying the directors
and auditors of the dead City of Melbourne Bank on a
charge of issuing false balance-sheets ended, as everybody
expected all along, in a joyous acquittal. The way the
charge was drafted made a conviction almost impossible
to begin with. The jury was requested to decide whether
the balance-sheet was false, whether the accused issued it
knowing it to be false, and if so, whether they did so
with intent to deceive. Melbourne is about the only
place on this earth where it could be held as possible for
bank officials to knowingly issue a false balance-sheet
without intent to deceive, but in Melbourne pretty nearly
anything is possible. The Judge’s summing-up also reads
in some places like an address for the defence —if the Age
reports it accurately. The case for the defence was
practically that Longtnuir, the Man that’s Dead, had done
all the mischief, and everybody else was as pure as the
driven snow’. Judge Holroyd informed the jury that it
couldn’t convict anybody without also convicting Long-
muir (deceased), which, seeing that deceased hadn’t been
heard in his own defence, looks absurd, both legally and
morally. And the jurymen probably didn’t care to con-
vict a corpse. It w'as also reiterated over and over again
that it might be only the ignorance, or stupidity, or neg-
lect of the board that made it do w’hat it did, and that
the auditors weren’t bound to say anything more than
that the accounts were correct according- to the books
(which weren t correct). And the result of it all is that the
City of Melbourne Bank died from natural causes, anil no
one is accountable. Even the Man that’s Dead seems to
be whitewashed this time. Nobody is responsible for the
bogus balance-sheets, or the trafficking of the bank in its
own shares, or anything else. All these things rose up
and did themselves probably. And it has been laid down
as definitely as possible that if bank officials are paid to do
certain duties, and say they do them, yet leave them un-
done to the ruin of the people who paid and trusted
them, there is no criminality about that whatever. It
isn’t even getting salary under false pretences, appa-
rently.
* * * *
Judge Holroyd, in the City of Melb. Bank case,
on the value of the ordinary auditor’s certificate : “ As to
the auditors, the certificate they gave could not mean
that any item in the statement was correct. It must
mean that the statement was correct so far as the
books disclosed, that so far as they had been able to dis-
cover it was correct. They had a right to rely on the
manager and other officers of the bank in the absence of
suspicious circumstances.” This is interesting seeingthat
quite a number of bank and other auditors simply certify
that the balance-sheet is correct “ according to the books.”
And that kind of certificate, as Holroyd says and as Tiie
Bulletin has been saying for a long while, doesn’t mean
that any item is correct; it actually doesn’t guarantee
one single item. All it amounts to is that the balance-
sheet is just as rotten and dishonest as the books, and no
more so. Then why are such certificates issued, and why
do directors want them and shareholders put up with
them, seeing that their absolute worthlessness is thus
openly acknowledged ?

That solid institution, the United Insurance
Co. (Sydney), shows a premium income for the year of
£97,260 (an increase of £64 on last year), and a net profit
on the fire and marine business of £7331, as compared
with £6062 and £17,908 for two preceding years. The
figures show that insurance business doesn’t leave a
specially gorgeous margin these times. Interest and
rents yield £4134, which is a reasonably good return,
making the total profit for the year £11,465. A 12J4
per cent, dividend absorbs £8528 ; £lOOO is added to re-
serve, making it £53,000; £lOOO to reserve for unad-
justed losses, making it £6OOO ; and £937 carried forward
makes the balance to credit of profit and loss £10,152.
The United has no less than five separate reserves : ordi-
nary reserve, £53,000; re-insurance reserve, £12,500 ;
reserve for unadjusted losses, £6000; for equalisation of
dividends, 12000; and for depreciation of investments,
£lO,OO0 —making £83,500 in all ; also a floating reserve of
£16,416 on profit and loss account, which make altogether
a pretty solid line of defence.
* * * *
The Permanent Trustee Co. of N.S.W. reports
that the profit for the year is £1035. Last year it
amounted to £lOO7, and the one before to £761, so things
are gradually improving. A 4 per cent, dividend absorbs
£lOOO, and the £35 left, added to previous balance of
£795, leaves £B3O at credit of profit and loss. Last year
the Permanent Trustee Co. also paid £lOOO dividend,
leaving £7 to the good, so in two years it has only accu-
mulated £42 towards strengthening its position. This
isn’t a notably large margin. One thing worth noting is,
that though the position is absolutely sound and there is
a steady though gradual improvement in the returns
which promises well for the future, the trustee business
so far hardly seems to pay expenses. At all events, the
co. has £26,194 of its own cash invested, and assuming it
brings in only 4 per cent, (the exact amount it does bring
in doesn t appear, as interest and commission are lumped
together), the income from this source would be 4 1047.
And as the net profits are only £1035 it looks as if the
trustee business hadn't yet reached the paying point,
and the dividends are merely profits realised by the
investment of the shareholders’ own money.
* * * •
The Langridge Building Society (Melb.) is a
striking contrast to the general wreckage of the boom-
stricken city. It didn’t join in the madness of the Gillies
days ; it neither bought huge estates to cut up nor built
rows and rows of unsaleable shoddy houses, nor tried to
get the biggest mass of deposits on record ; in fact it stuck
closely to its own little business and let the boom rave past
unnoticed. As a result it has paid its liabilities as they
came due, has reduced its deposits from £llB,OOO (in 1890)
to £15,000 because it found the deposit business dangerous
and unprofitable, has all its assets unencumbered, a solid
cash balance instead of the usual overdraft, its capital in-
tact, and, after reducing the rate of interest charged to
borrowers, it shows a profit of * 1917 for the year. The
profit is a good deal less than last year, when it reached
£3046, and there is no immediate prospect of a dividend,
seeing that there is a heavy drain to be met in the shape
of losses on realisation of foreclosed properties. Still, the
Langridge keeps well ahead of all liabilities, which says a
lot for the management.
* * * *
The Australian Alliance Assurance Co. (Melb.),
a large concern which does fires marine and guarantee
business, and also has a small and steadily-decaying life
assurance connection, reports that the year’s profit is
£19,978 against only about £lO,OOO last year. A 12 per
cent, dividend absorbs £15,000 ; and £4OOO goes to
suspense account. This co. has about £540,000 of invest-
ments, and among them are £275,000 of Victorian
mortgages, £69,000 of Victorian freehold property, which
is a rather dubious item these times, £24,000 in Victorian
building societies (most Victorian building societies are
pretty cronk at present), and over £32,000 in “ Austra-
lasian insurance company shares.” The premium income
of the life department has gone down in five years from
£31,000 to £20,000, and its investments now yield about
£3 18s. 4d. per cent., whereas it is alleged that the society
bases its estimates on a 4 per cent, limit. Also, the ex-
penses of the life branch keep going up as the premiums
and interest go down, and the insurance fund is also
gradually fading away, so the chances are that the
Alliance will presently give up life business altogether.
The sort of life business it does isn’t worth keeping.
* * * *
The annual meeting of the Bank of New Zea-
land was held last week at Wellington (M.L.), and some
information then supplied throws a little more light on
the last year’s balance-sheet (commented on in The Bul-
letin of 18th July), and makes it look rather better and a
good deal more definite than it formerly did. The net
profit on the year’s business was £96,201, but of this
about £40,000 was set aside to provide for further losses
left over from the old, reckless days of private manage-
ment, and the net profit was returned as being £52,192.
The Colonial Treasurer, however, insisted on writing off
a further £12,000 against known or possible losses, so the
net profit was reduced to £40,083. This was evidently
after payment of 4 per cent, on the £2,000,000 of State,
guaranteed capital. A dividend of 3) per cent, on
£500,000 of preference capital belonging to the State ab-
sorbed £4375, and £35,708 was paid over to the B.N.Z.
Estates Co. to reduce the shortage on that luckless off-
shoot of the bank. Therefore, so far as appears from the
cabled figures, the 8.N.Z., as the result of a year’s work,
met all the interest charges on its £2,500,000 of preference
capital, wiped off £56,000 of its own old bad debts, and
provided £35,708 towards the deficit of the Estates Co.,
which, in such times as these, is a good enough result to
satisfy anybody. There is a long uphill struggle ahead
before the* shortage in the Estates Co., which the old
private management left behind it, is fully met, and the
£75,000 for the goodwill of the Colonial Bank is w'ritten
off ; still, at the present rate of progress these things cer-
tainly will be done in course of time, and the B.N.Z. even
now is in a vastly better position than half of the Aus-
tralian banks.
* * * *
At the Royal Bank’s half-yearly meeting,
held last week in Melbourne, the chairman again
lamented that the Companies Act hadn't passed,
and therefore the institution couldn’t write its losses
off capital. Then a shareholder (moved probably by
The Bulletin’s suggestion that the deficit should
be put openly to profit-and-loss account, so that the
people interested could know how they stood) began
to make inquiries, and was told that “ it was inadvisable
and impossible to state the losses,” and “ it would not be
prudent or wise to fix any amount.” Which is a brilliant
piece of logic. The Royal is willing, as soon as the new
Act passes, to write off losses, and says it would have
done so long ago if the Act had passed. It must state the
amount when it writes them off, yet it says it isn’t pru-
dent to state the amount. Also, it must know the amount
to write them off, yet it says it doesn’t know the amount.
Also, it doesn't know the amount w ell enough to state it
now, yet it knew it so well a year or more ago that it
would have w'ritten it off then but for lack of the neces-
sary authority.

• * * *
The big South British Insurance Co. (Maori-
land) has evidently struck a hard year. Premium income
for the 12 months just ended was £223,477 ; losses and
expeilses, £225,085 ; loss, £l6OB. Interest on investments
and other items yielded sufficient to leave £7990 to the
good on the year’s work, but as the 15 per cent, dividend
absorbs 19694 and £430 goes on depreciation, the co. ends
the year £2134 worse off.
* * * *
Colonial Permanent Building and Investment
Society, Melbourne, reports a loss of £823 for the past
year, comparedwithlossof £l5Ol previousyear. Thesociety
is now a few hundred pounds to the bad, and it is
sorrowfully remarked that the assets aren’t likely to fetch
their alleged value. As assets never do fetch their alleged
value in Melbourne now, this seems quite probable.
* * « *
Melbourne Permanent Building Society shows a
half-year s profit of £BB4 ; and this big reconstructed
institution, which is still tottering under a debenture-
liability of £322,000, and is trying hard to live on i 386,000
worth of foreclosed house property and £56,000 worth of
unsaleable vacant land, begins to look like pulling through.
Debenture-interest was reduced some time ago from 5 to
2) per cent., which makes all the difference. The profit
just equals £1 Bs. per cent, on capital, and, while making
£BB4 on one hand, the society seems to have lost £llOO
out of the contingency fund on the other. Still, it looks
a lot healthier than it did, and its liabilities are being
steadily reduced.

If it is a fact, as stated by Sydney Teleoraph,
that N.S.W. Land-tax assessors are to get £1 Is. per day
and expenses for attending when appeals are under con-

as they seem likely to do, w'hat will the expense of collect-

mate of the amount the tax should yield was based on the
present assessments, how much will the tax yield when
all the disputes are adj usted ?
* * * *
Still another rise of id. on the 21b. loaf is an-
nounced in Sydney. Since the beginning of the Freetrade
epoch of cheapness the price of bread has gone steadily
upwards. It doesn’t necessarily follow that Freetrade
sent it up, but it is dead certain that Freetrade didn’t
keep it down, as promised.
* * * *
Auckland (M.L.) has decided to lease its tram-
way system to a British syndicate for 30 years, the reason
being that the syndicate, which proposes to convert the
trams into an electric service, will “ bring capital into the
country.” Which is just the thing that such a co. won’t
do, and which no such co. ever does. It will bring rails
and wires and electric machinery and new cars into the
country instead of having them manufactured locally, and
then it will take capital out of the country in the shape of
the receipts which will go to pay dividends in Hingland.
The idea that a foreign syndicate which gets a valuable
concession necessarily “ brings money into the country”
is played out.
* * * *
The balance-sheets of the National Bank (Mel-
bourne) and the Colonial Bank (Melbourne) appear in this
issue. Both were reviewed at length in this column
last week.
* * * *
The prosecution of W. G. German, late
secretary of Manly Co-operative Ferry Co., for issuing' an
allegedly false balance-sheet, fiaseoed at the Police Court.
There was no allegation against the ex-secretary of
having in any way misused funds. There is likely to be
some more law over this matter—in the form of an action
for damages.
* * * *
A fortnight’s work at the British concentrating
mill: — Carbonates treated, 2614 tons, producing 687 tons
first grade concentrates (69'4 per cent, lead and 14'50z.
silver), and 227 tons second-grade concentrates (55 per
cent, lead, and 15oz. silver), and 25 tons concentrates
from slimes (63’5 per. cent, lead and Boz. silver); sulphides
treated, 2285 tons, producing 445 tons first-grade concen-
trates (71 percent, lead, 240 z. silver, and 5 per cent, zinc),
41 tons second-grade concentrates (61 per cent, lead,
2002. silver, and 9 per cent zinc), and 22 tons slime con-
centrates (72 per cent, lead, 21oz. silver, and 4'5 per cent,
zinc). To ascertain the quality of the work done, it
would, however, be necessary to know the average assays
of the stuff treated. But, anyhow, these returns are
capital. If the machine has lost much metal,
then the ore is much better than has been generally
thought. So long as thorough good smelting stuff like
this is available, and the B.H. Proprietary Co. can buy it,
the treatment of the dry ores in the latter co.’s mine will
be possible. The operations at the British must now' be
yielding a considerable profit. One would, however, like
to know something more about the slimes. The slime-
concentrates are so limited in quantity and their value in
silver and lead so high, while the zinc concentrates are
brought, down so low, that there is just the danger that
too much of the contained metal is being sacrificed in
order to produce a good smelting material.
What shareholders in B.H. mines have yet to
learn is that there is concentrating and concentrating.
Running- a mill is not like running a smelting furnace.
From the latter you get your bullion and can command a
market. You have, however, to depend upon the more
uncertain smelter for a market for your concentrates.
And you have to produce your concentrates accordingly
—to work it up very fine (that is, reduce the zinc con-
tents to their lowest, at a loss, of course, of some silver)
or to send out a rougher product, for subsequent treat-
ment perhaps in a roasting or reverberatory furnace
before blending and smelting. Thus while the British,
selling its product to a direct smelting Proprietary, fines
dow'n its slimes to 47 tons (from 4899 tons ore), Block 14,
which does a preliminary roasting, produced at about the
same time and sent to its works 342 tons slimes for only
2637 tons ore. All of which goes to show that there never
was so much need for shrewd directors who will attend
constantly to their duties as there is to-day.
* * * *
The Junction Consolidated, one of the recently-
revived mines, is developing well; and at the Silver Hill
Junction, in the same neighborhood, the starting of anew
winding plant has been made the occasion for resurrecting
a number of old reports setting out measurements and
assays which, in the present day, can only be regarded as
highly satisfactory. No sensationally great things need
be expected from either of these properties, but they
stand among the best of those few “ outside show's” which
are even now capable of paying co-operative parties or
very economically-managed small companies.
.North’s 600 ft. prospects are a little better, but
divs. are still far, far away. The Western lode (if what
they have is the Western lode) is very different indeed
from the lode in the Junction and Junction North.
* * * *
At B.H. Proprietary mine, writes a Bulletin
correspondent, the contract system is being carried out
to its fullest extent. It used to be a standing joke :
“ Soon the offices will be swept out by contract.” This
prediction has not yet been verified ; but a few days ago
an old and faithful retainer, whose duty it was to keep
the offices (which are commodious) in order, was dis-
charged and a boy put on in his place. The cheese-paring
does not appear to have yet reached the highly-paid
officials.
British Broken Hill mine, formerly managed by
Col. Morgan, is endeavoring to outdo the‘“ big” mine in
severity to its employes, writesa Bulletin correspondent.
Drill-Sharpeners are expected to put through 500 hand-
drills in a day ; the Proprietary only looked for 250 at
the hands of each man before" it put the blacksmiths’
work out on contract. Several British blacksmiths have
ineffectually tried to do the higher tally, and it is stated
that arteffort will be made to get men elsewhere who will
be equal to the task. Boys, so-called, who are really
fully-developed men, are given ss. per day for the arduous
labor of “ striking.”
* • *
Barrass and Conley’s wonderful show at the
Peak, Cobar, is coming out under the wing of Col. Wad-
dell and ex-Mayor (of Sydney) Manning in the form of a
£200,000 co., in which B. and C. are to get one-half in
paid-up shares and £40,000 cash, and a working capital of
£20,000 is provided. To be offered first, we are told, in
the form of a syndicate of 100 shares of £640 each, to find
the cash and working capital. The Bulletin suggests
that the syndicate first put up £lOOO and spend it in sink-
ing another 100 ft. on the Blue and Brown lodes, and
driving on each 100 ft. from the bottom. That will give a
fair idea of the value and extent of these ore bodies. At
present it is impossible to say what they really are. The
syndicate which bonded the mines now known as the
Cobar Gold Mines, of which the Fort Bourke was the
chief, crushed over 2000 tons from different places before
they paid oVer the purchase money. Tonnage was already
exposed by eight years’ development work, which is not
the case with B. and C.’s mine. If such work was ex-
pedient with the Fort Bourke, how much more so in the
case where the purchase money is over ten times as great ?
* * * *
Eleanora, N.S.W., first div., sixpence, total
£2500. Bought six years'ago for £30,000 cash, since
which the co. has spent another £20,000 at least in plant
and mining. Last year the co. was £7OOO in debt, largely
spent in machinery still unused. The mine has improved
slightly, the management a great deal. Which prompts
the writer to enquire how far the next mine, the Gari-
baldi, is from the div. stage ?
* * * *
The mining manager, W. Blake more, sends a
great report of the Girilambone copper-mine, N.S.W.,
recently re-opened by some Sydneyites. He sees nothing
to prevent the mine turning out 100 tons of copper a
month, and thinks it can be landed in Sydney for 130 a
ton. The only reason he can give for the failure of the
mine in the past is the fact that no machinery existed for
treating the ore before it went to the smelters (by which
it would seem it was sent direct to the furnaces’instead
of being first concentrated) and that it is unsuitable for
direct treatment.
* * * *
Whispered that the tributors working the Cobar
copper-mine are now netting something like £l5OO a week.
Well, they deserve it. What the men who worked the
mine a few years ago deserve is quite another matter.
They never got anything for gold contents, even if they
knew it was there. The tributors are getting about
2500 z. of gold a week. Wonder if the London copper-
buyers would find it out if the tributors were innocent of
its presence. Also, if the million’s worth of copper sold
in former years was all used up with its gold unextracted?
The Bulletin smiles.

Beyond much doubt, the mine would pay were £lO,OOO
spent in plant and development for an output of 1000
tons monthly.

* * * •
From a W.A. mining-manager who keeps an eye
on the General Gordon mine and its surroundings : “ No
fresh developments in the parent mine, and they are not
too active over the crushing plant which was to be crush-
ing ‘ early in October.’ Glass, the manager, has re-
signed. He is a cold-blooded truth-teller, and has been
acting up to his Ballarat reputation. However, the
mine is head and shoulders above those around it, floated
on its strength. Perhaps the rich lodes and formations
referred to in the prospectuses have also been stolen.
They are not visible there now to ordinary eyes.”
Pambula— “ Mining is very quiet here ; severa 1
mines are under offer for sale, thus retarding- develop-
ment.” Never work a mine while under offer (a well-
observed W.A. practice) seems to be the game all over
N.S. W. Nine-tenths of the alleged mines of N.S.W. are
either under offer or have been undey offer and are ready
to go again. Why are so few Charters Towers, Bendigo,
or Ballarat mines under offer? Because they are all
worked byco’s? Nothing of the kind. Good mines are
seldom for sale.
* * * •
J.P.-W. to The Bulletin: —“A new broom
sweeps clean.” N.S.W. Mines-Minister Smith, shortly
after last general election, crowed loudly about his inten-
tion to squelch “ shepherding,” the ruination of the
mining industry of the province. Probably the boss
shepherder of N.S.W. is a fat man who makes a southern
field his chief stamping-ground. This gentleman must
have at least a score of leases taken up in his own name —
and more about to be granted—upon the greater number
of which no labor-conditions whatever have been fulfilled.
One case in point. A windlass and rope have been hold-
ing 40 acres for five years ; five men were at one time
working there for five weeks when the rope and windlass
were erected. These facts are notorious in the town and
district, yet nothing is done. If you wish to try your
hick with any of these locked areas, the price asked is
something tall. Can nothing be done to compel this one
fat-man to disgorge and allow the town he keeps in chains
to timve on ?
* * * *
Hannan’s Pride of the Valley, a Sydney-run
show ; directors empowered to sell London or elsewhere.
Six months ago it might have been sold. But the boom is
over, and Bull is thoughtfully chewing his cud.
* * ♦ *
Bayley’s Consols : “Reef 2ft. to 3ft. wide, and
the stone carries a little line gold.” Have they stopped
working the water? As a water-mine it has been a great
success, but as a white quartz mine the outlook is gloomy.
Bavley, the finder of the Reward mine, W.A.,
died iast week on his estate in Vic., aged 31. Had he
never found that mine, the chances are he would have
lived another quarter century, while Westralia might
possibly still be living on pearl-shell, sandal-wood, and
jarrah, and a lot of other things would never have
happened. Heaven knows how long the Reward mine
would have remained unfound in such a desert and
desolate country. There was only one of its kind there,
anyhow. Now that he is dead, the writer expects a
revival of the yarn that he never found it at all—as big a
lie as was ever told. Success killed him, like many a
better miner, but, be it said to his memory that the big
turn he gave to Westralian history was not all the result
of easy luck. He and Ford (his mate) went into the wilds
of tile country and suffered tremendous hardships.

By wire—“ A meeting of the Coolgardie Man-
agers’ Association will be held shortly to discuss the
advisableness of sending ore for treatment to the Water
Trust and Public Crushing Co.’s works, near Northam,
where 80 head of stamps of the Californian type have
been erected, besides modern appliances for saving gold.
The water for the works is unlimited in quantity, and well
suited for crushing.” To keep this plant running will
require over 1000 tons a week. Where are the mines near
Coolgardie that can do it? Some excuse for failure will
soon be found. Carriage too long, or crushing charges
too high. Coolgardie has too many idle batteries of its
own lying around to attempt to keep one going over 300
miles away.

* * » *
J, A. Wallace, Vic., suggests Govt, rewards for
treating quartz tailings at a cost of 2s. 6d. a ton for a 94
per cent, extraction, and for treating complex ores at 7s.
6d. a ton. What he suggests for quartz mining he is doing
himself in alluvial. Wallace, at Yackandandah, Vic., has
spent fully £40,000 in building barges and placing all
the machinery on them necessary to break down
and raise thousands of yards of ground daily. The fact
that he has just completed two new ones at a cost of
£14,000 is evidence that the business pays: Wallace pub-
lishes no returns. The bulk of the wash consists of old
alluvial tailings, but as long as threepence in gold can be
got out of a cubic yard it pays Wallace on account of the
gigantic scale on which the wash is handled. What he is
doing there can be done in other places. In a few weeks
a start will be made to use the same system in N.S.W.,
and, better still, on a piece of ground Over a mile in
extent which has not been turned over, and is paying
handsomely now, though worked on the old paddock and
tip-dray system.

Queensland goldfields show an increase of
10,9570 z. for the first three quarters of ’96 over the first
three quarters of ’95. Mount Morgan keeps on growing,
and shows an increase for the past nine months of 1896 of
28,8230 z. over the same period of 1895. If the smaller
Rockhampton fields be added, another 10,000 oz. increase
must be placed on. Charters Towers, however, is decreas-
ing. Total decrease for last nine months as compared
with same period of ’95 equals 25,2110 z. Gympie also
shows a drop of 83940 z. for the same time. On the other
hand, Croydon and Etheridge are looking up. Croydon
shows a total increase for nine months of 19,4800 z. (allow-
ing for tailings) ; the Etheridge an increase of 39710 z.

Maitland (N.S.W.) has run a Chinese
mission for seven years past with the aid of a cheap
Chow missionary who gets <£3o a year. The name
of the toiler in the vineyard is KumYee, and in
seven years he has presumably drawn <£2lo. As a
result he reports that, “on one or two hearts the
truth seems to be taking hold ”■—it hasn’t en-
tirely taken hold of anybody yet. There are
about 300 Chinese in the district, and, at this rate of
progress, it will take 1050 years and <£31,500 cash
before the truth seems to be taking hold of the
whole lot of them, and by that time they will pro-
bably be dead—most of them, anyhow. There
was a deficit of Is. 9d. in the year’s work (income,
<£29 18s. 3d. ; salary of toiler in the vineyard, £3O),
and a collection was taken up to fill the gap, but
the stolidly indifferent Mongol only panned out for
10gd. No le3s than thirty females get their names
enthusiastically mentioned in the local paper over
this matter, so it seems to pan out well in the
matter of advt. Thirty female enthusiasts ! .£3O a
year to the toiler in the vineyard!! Is. 9d. deficit!!!
IOJd. collection !!!! And one or two hearts on
which the Truth seems to be taking hold after the
tired expounder has banged the loud tocsin for
seven weary years, and held open-air meetings at
the Chinese gardens on the windward side of the
sewage!
* * * *
The absolute want of humour in the
W.C.T.U. organisation is best shown by the way
a few dozen of its hopelessly ordinary members
get together annually in a room and elect each
other to look after the different sections of the
universe. At the latest Sydney outbreak of the
smileless organisation Mrs. Patterson was ap-
“'TWERE BETTER FAR WE NE'ER HAD MET!”
pointed to promote “ peace and arbitration ” for
the ensuing year, and considering the dreadful
way Abdul Hamid is going on, and the row in
Madagascar, and the Matabele trouble, and the
awful hunger to annex the remains of Africa, and
the war in the Soudan, and other things, Mrs.
Patterson should have her hands full. The fact
that Miss Smith wasn’t appointed to superintend
the drought, and Mrs. Jenkins to attend to the
department of planets, comets, and fixed stars,
and Mrs. Thingumbob to look after the devil
generally, was probably an omission which will be
remedied next year.

W.C.T.U.—Who Could Tickle Us?
. ,
Dear BULLETIN, Bush law is a greater
pest out back than the heat, sand-flies, ants,
snakes, and scorpions all lumped together. A
shearer was summoned up Louth (N.S.W.) way,
the other day, for unlawfully (wet sheep) leaving
plaintiff’s hired service. Plaintiff was a J.P. and
squatter, and two neighboring J’sP., also squat-
ters and also shearing at the time, sat on the
bench. And how did they sit on that shearer !
I erhaps justice was done, but how long is the
squatter to be let hear his brother-squatter’s case ?
—R. H.
Dear Bulletin, —Coming from the West,
the other day, a Salvation Army “ staff captain ”
was my only companion in a first-class railway-
carriage (never mind how I got there). I admired
his fine silk travelling-coat and cap, and when
nearing a very cold spot late at night envied him
his soft fur rug. At every refreshment-station
he filled himself with beef-tea, toast, cocoa, cake,
and other luxuries. He didn’t whisper any “ Hal-
lelujah-tack ” into my unregenerated ear, but was
busy reading Corelli’s “Sorrows of Satan ” while
I was thinking of his poor, half-starved sister
officers in the back-blox. —R.H.

* * * *
Since the institution of Women’s Suffrage
in S.A. the local Assembly galleries are daily
crowded by ladies, and members are in danger of
complete ruin because of the necessity of pro-
viding afternoon-tea for their fair constituents. A
lady never thinks of “ shouting ” back either.
* * * *
Westralian Governor “Jerry” Smith seems
to be always in trouble. When in Adelaide, on
the way to the Melb. Cup, Smith much angered
Premier Kingston by commenting on the local
Hospital disputes—a strange breach of etiquette.
Fortunately, Kingston is a very religious man, or
he would probably have followed the example of
the Westralian station-master who advised Jerry
to go to blazes.
* * * *
At a recent M.L. prosecution of a tem-
perance drink-seller, the Govt. Analyst certified
that her celebrated “ plum wine ” —so much appre-
ciated by the local teetotal parson that he bought
up the entire stock—contained 2£ times more
alcohol than best Dunedin ale. A similar lesson
was taught by a Sydney “ ginger-wine ” prosecu-
tion not long since. The Bulletin begins to
think the teetotal fanatic has his head screwed on
the right way, after all.
* * * *
The recent attempted escape of a prisoner
from Darlinghurst Prison, Sydney, and the admis-
sion that he had secreted lock-picking and bar-
filing materials, should direct attention to the
new methods adopted in Continental and Ameri-
can gaols for coping with such criminals. There,
bars in window, doors, and elsewhere are mere
hollow pipes and are filled with water. When a
pipe is severed the water escapes, and this escape
at once registers itself in the gaol office. This
system has, so far, defied the wit of gaol-
breakers.
* . * *
Telegram-faking is a queer artj but some-
times the faker “ cops out ” in constructing a sen-
tence from a word or two per wire. For example,
the other day the Age gravely stated that the
Emperor of Germany had publicly announced his
regret that all German colonies were a failure,

And the London wire, “Victoria critical,” was

Miss FRANCES ROSS, of the Bland Holt Co. at Sydney

He was boots and chief manager of the
lamp-cleaning department, but his rival the
“ rouseabout ” had on the day in question antici-
pated him and “diddled” him out of certain
perquisites in the shape of tips from a few
departing drummers. Things generally had gone
wrong with him, and in a paroxysm of soul-sodden
disgust he seized a short pencil and proceeded to
write out his resignation. Having completed hi 3
task, he handed it to the landlord to whom it was
addressed. It ran as follows :
George, —I hereby chuck up the (blanky) job ; dated)
12 9 96.— Sydney Hunt.

Miss Mabelle Davidson (her
photo, appears herewith) is about
the most attractive young amateur
prima-donna that Melbourne has
been introduced to of late years.
Thoroughly at home on the stage,
Miss Davidson has a voice, face,
figure and intelligence to carry her
through leading parts, and given
the same opportunities to distin-
guish herself she would probably
outshine the successful Juliet Wray,
who burst forth from an amateur
into a professional a year or two
ago.

The Vic. coroner who orders
sane suicides to be buried at night
by policemen is now suspected of
wilful satire on the Law.
* * *
In Melbourne the coroner’s-
jury service is becoming a profes-
sional pursuit. There are certain
men officially recognised as jurors,
and these get post-cards to attend
when required. Four shillings a
time is not to be sniffed at these
days, even though it has to be
earned by corpse-viewing.

In a Maoriland S.M. Court
the other day, an old resident was
charged before two local justices
with a petty theft. After hearing
the evidence, the presiding beak
said “ that there was not the slight-
est doubt in the minds of the
Bench that the accused was guilty ;
however, they would take his long
residence into consideration and
give him the benefit of the doubt.
He would be discharged. ”

N. : “Whoever heard of a
clergyman’s widow marrying an-
other ? I never did and yet I knew

black men.”
For the last two weeks there has been an
armed peace between the Adelaide Hospital fac-
tions. Editor Bonython, of S.A. Advertises,
offered to arbitrate—an offer that was accepted,
but, so far, without result. The striking doctors
refuse to go back with the imported Dr. Napier,
and Kingston very properly declines to sacrifice
the man who stepped into the breach when the
patients were practically deserted.
• * » *
The Northern Territory has the very small-
est death-rate in Australasia —smaller even than
that of Maoriland. Yet its birth-rate is so exceed-
ingly small that the population is dying out. For
every 1000 people there are six births, eight deaths
and one marriage per annum. The Territory,
unless something happens to it, will soon be
known as the land of the aged, unmarried China-
man. For that matter, no judicious infant would
choose that region as a place to be born in, and it
says a lot for the Australian infant that he so very
seldom is born there.

* * «
A Bundaberg cane -grower,
having a dispute with one or two
Kanakas, remarked, in a moment
of irritation, that any “boy” who
cared to break his engagement might
do so. To his utter astonishment,
the whole gang instantly downed
their tools and departed. When the
matter was referred to the Poly-
nesian inspector, the agreement had
to be cancelled, despite the indig-
nant planter’s asseveration that his
remark only applied to one or two
individuals. Tommy Tanna is some-
what prone to take language liter*
ally—when it suits him.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: !, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

* * * *
Judge Williams, of Victoria, has taken to
making severe remarks about the Crimes Act,
which makes the seduction of a girl under 16
penal, no matter what the circumstances may be.
The present law practically assumes three things :
(1) That no girl 15 years and 364 days old, or a
little less, ever lies about her age or invites seduc-
tion, though a girl 16 years and one day old may
do both ; (2) that though a girl under 16 isn’t
responsible for her actions in this respect a boy
under 16 is ; (3) that no girl under 16 ever perjures
herself in the witness-box, and her statements
there can, to all intents and purposes, be implicitly
relied upon. And all these three things are an
ass and a Social Gimlet, and there is no sense in
them.

C. R. : “Is French the only language in
which a thing can be put neatly ? Plain Saxon is,
if you know how to use it properly, not so bank-
rupt in this potentiality. Speaking at a Church

be described as ‘ a love of togetherness.’ Quite so.
Bet your blushes!—‘a love of the altogether-
ness ? ’ ”
* * •
A M.L. prohibition-district J.P. had the
other day only just time to flee by the back-door
of a sly-grog saloon when the police burst in the
front. Next day he sat on the Bench, armed with
all the terrors of the law, read the saloon-keeper a
severe homily, and fined him <£l 4s. Calling in
the afternoon for his usual nip, he was received
with a volley of blasphemy that made the glasses
rattle. “Steady!” said the magistrate, “I’ll
toss you up which of us pays the fine.” The
landlord won ; and half-an-hour later the J.P.
thus addressed the court-clerk: “I find that
owing to a slight misapprehension Blank’s fine is
much heavier than the law requires, and after due
consideration 1 have decided that a fine of 10s.
will meet the requirements of the case. You will
please make the necessary alteration and—here is
the money.”
* * * *
‘ln Vic. Assembly, the other day, attention*
was called to the case of one constable. Pigott,
who rescued two women from a burning building
and suffered considerably in the attempt. Some-
body wanted to know whether the department
intended to recognise the constable’s bravery by
promotion or otherwise. Chief Sec. Peacock
replied that the Commissioner of Police had made
a “ suitable entry ” in the record of the scorched
hero, and he would be allowed full pay whilst in-
capacitated from duty.—

The teetotallers’ millennium—The Ginger-
beera.
A case which recently arose in
Melbourne leaves room for a good
deal of moral discussion. Two
young men burgled a clothier’s
premises and took 113 coats.
The clothier advertised that
if they were returned he
would pay a reward of £5
and let the matter drop.
Thereupon the coats were
laboriously humped back
to the tailor-man’s house,
and, while he was counting
them to see that they were
all right, a member of his
family, seemingly by pre-
arrangement, bolted for
the police. Two constables
promptly arrived, and the
thieves ran for their lives,
minus the =£s and the 113
coats, having almost bent
themselves double carrying
the goods back to no pur-
pose. The tailor, so far as
appears, considers that his
conduct was justifiable on
straight business principles,
whereas the two men are
understood to regard him as
a low, deceptive character,
and the public don’t exactly
know what view to take.
* * *
The other Sunday in
a Victorian church lovely
woman asserted herself quite
unexpectedly by rising up
in the choir and aiming her
hymn-book at a fellow-wor-
shipper. Being a woman,
she naturally hit the wrong worshipper, and
the person who received the unlooked-for volume
on the ear stood up and said things, and the ser
mon stopped suddenly. Then the culprit stated
hysterically that the young man whose eye she had
attempted to blacken habitually made faces at her,
and after that some friends led her out in a highly
nervous state and with her bonnet considerably
dishevelled. This sort of thing does a good deal
to brighten up the tabernacle, which otherwise
has a tendency to be dull and rather heavy.

Spagnoletti : “ Australia has sundry indus-
trious dealers in artificial teeth. Like diamonds
in necklaces, they are knocked out and reset. It
doesn’t pay to bury new teeth in view of the large
supply yielded by corpses nowadays ; and not one
woman in three has her own. The astonishing
cheapness of dentists’ sets proves much.”

• * * *
Apropos the Victorian case wherein the
court hesitated to admit as solicitors young men
who had served in the province’s militia : a now-
practising N.S.W. attorney, when he presented
himself, found that he was in a tight place.
Devoted to music, he had added to his small in-
come by occasionally playing in an orchestra, and
shared the pay with the pros.’ This was, it seems,
known to one member of the court, who, more
than half angry, asked, with more acerbity than
usual, if the applicant for admission hadn’t earned
money outside the law lately. The applicant
came near owning up to the orchestra work, and
would have spoilt himself thereby; when Chief
Justice Martin hastily interposed : “ Why do you
hesitate? Didn’t the applicant accept pay as a
private soldier in the Soudan ? ” “Oh, yes, yes,
yes, your Honor.” And so the old C. j. saved
him, to the dismay and disgust of at least one
learned brother.

who usually advertises for some kind elderly gent

If a “ bobby,” whilst on duty,
Should cheerfully contrive
To rescue screaming beauty
From getting burnt alive,
He has the satisfaction
When he’s carried home to bed
(Unless he falls in action,
And is altogether dead)
Of knowing that his wages
Are not docked when he’s away,
Since the Government engages
To allow Full Pay.
He may not be promoted—
For a rise he didn’t 100k —
But the incident is noted
In a big official book.
His conduct is recorded,
So, after passing hence,
Perhaps he is rewarded
By a ruling Providence.
And meantime (as was stated)
In the ’Ouse the other day)
Though incapacitated
He receives Full Pay.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Nov. 3, 1896.
My Dear Myee—
The “ enthusiasm of the London critics ” over
Hilda Spong as the Duchess of Coolgardie boils
down, upon close inspection, into Clement
Scott’s rather unaccountable “ tremendous notice”
and mildly-encouraging pars, from most of the
others. Still, considering the freezing contempt
the ’Ome potentates have for anything new, as a
rule, Hilda’s beginning is quite triumphant.
When Henrietta Watson, a far more finished
artist, appeared at Toole’s last year after an
absence, the critics patted her on the back patron-
izingly with, “ And who, you will ask, is Miss
11. W. ?” English playgoers are very short-
memoried, for the above query was about a girl
who had not been out of the running for more
than a few years : they are also extremely groovy,
and if they once like an actress will go on fancy-
ing her in girlish parts long after she is a grand-
mother, and indignantly crush the irreverent
stranger who may dare to observe that Ellen
Terry is rather an elderly Beatrice or that Ada
Rehan’s figure is a trifle matronly. If Hilda
Spong only “ catches on ” while she is still young,
she will be a great draw to playgoers who like
t hair heroines to look convincingly youthful and
charming.
Ethel Haydon and the new duchess of Drury
I jane were school-fellows for some time in Brighton
( Vic.), where Ethel was much the quicker of the
two. But Hilda was always much in earnest, and
especially so when she began on the stage. She
often used to say that she looked upon acting as a
thing to be taken seriously. And she improved
much as she went on. Perhaps her best character
was the sketch of Mrs. Cleeve which she con-
tributed to “Mrs. Ebbsmith.”
A preliminary meeting was held on Wednesday,
28th Oct., under the auspices of Lady Hampden,
to start a local branch of the “ Mothers’ Union,”
an organisation which is, by its own showing, a
paradox in action, and a fatuous, flabby paradox at
that. The first object is “To uphold the sanctity
of marriage.” Now, Mothers already United (to
fathers) have all the Union they want, and Mothers
not previously United (to anybody) would be
hooted out of the Union. So cui 'bono? Inci-
dentally, any Mother requiring the information
will be prayerfully taught the best way of setting
a good example to her boys and girls ; but if a
woman hasn’t sense enough to know how often to
spank her own little Tommy for the good of his
soul, she’s not the sort who can extract much in-
struction out of one hour’s talk per week with old
ladies of both sexes. There are branches of this
humorless organisation in many places, and a
muster-roll of 100,000, which is no more convincing
than the same number of names would be at the
foot of a petition. Some people will sign any-
thing, or join any society, provided it is hail-
marked by names giving due weight of religion
and aristocracy. And why not a “Fathers’ Con-
federacy ” or a “ Solidarity of First Cousins twice
removed ? ”
Philanthropy is always blighted by too much
Bible-class. Charitable institutions of all sorts
are run on the Sunday-school basis—as if
men and women could be walloped into good
behaviour like naughty children. A dipso-
maniac can’t be restored to health with-
out being given his daily stimulant of infan-
tile hymns accompanied by harmonium drone.
The dipsomaniac is frequently a man of strong
mental calibre, an invalid whose over-acute sensi-
bility makes him a neuropath, and he naturally
feels an amused contempt for the dull, nerveless
Sunday-school level of intellect. Goody-goody
folk can’t even give the factory-girls a banquet
and dance without throwing in a church service,
setting apart special seats at the feast for the girls
who are privileged to have their impressions of
Jonah and the whale guaranteed by an Hon.
Flowers were handed up lavishly on Saturday
night at all the concerts and theatres. Local
florists must have been doing a roaring trade all
day. A thoughtful admirer at the Centenary Hall
handed Miss Isaacs a yellow cluster exactly match-
ing her satin gown ; and Miss Blanche Thomas,
in pale green, carried off a bunch of pink roses
much enhanced by the contrast. Miss N. Young
was in a paler shade of green—the two tints struck
a cold chill when the wearers sang a duet —and
she staggered under a huge basket of flowers.
Then some extra trophies were distributed to the
men : the only one who got no bunch was Herr
Vollrnar, and he seemed quite lonely. .
Dr. MTlroy’s burglar is the victim of science.
The doc.’s wife, hearing a noise in the middle of
the night, cautiously whispered her husband that
there was a man in the room. Turning up a
convenient gas-jet, the doctor suddenly sprang out
of bed and grasped the intruder, while the lady
rang up the police on the telephone. A bobby
rapidly wheeled himself to the spot on his bicycle
—the modern version of the onward relentless
rush of Destiny-—and the burglar was captured.
The only unsupplied long-felt want now is a
Rontgen apparatus to detect the man under the
bed without his knowing it.
A fairly representative d.c. assembled at the
Lyceum first-night to see the fair Cora B. Potter
flop to conquer—for it was much more than a
stoop. But she was, when she forebore to curtsey,
a refined and fascinating Kate Hardeastle, and
her gowns were Potteresque—which means that
they suited that lady of curves and angles in her
own characteristic way. Her first appearance was
in dust-colored silk, with overdress of green taffeta
made with tabs at the waist and long direetoire
tails, lace about the neck and a wide belt and sash
ends of black satin; her handkerchief was of
grass-lawn, a point worthy of note, as that is the
latest fashion in colored pocket-handkerchiefs;
though, methinks, very awkward if one has a cold
in the head. She also carried a large black hat
with plume nodding over her nose. So it was no
wonder that young Marlow didn’t recognise her
when he saw ber again. The barm aid-dress was a
weird and wonderful arrangement of variegated
hues, but, cap and all, it suited her amazingly.
Lynda Maymond, as Constance Neville, wore a
rather monotonous “ Dolly Varden ” of rose bro-
cade in one scene, and an expressive hat of black
and pink.
Knee-breeches and silk stockings comedy needs
a large quantity of artificially-fatted calf for its
support. But Bellew’s Romeo legs are famous, so,
of course, his Marlow extremities were all right in
silvery grey satin touched with a little old lace
and in buckled shoon. Montgomery as Hastings
had a gaudy sort of travelling coat in bilious
satin with capes of purple velvet and froggings
of gold, but none of them filled their last-century
clothes like J. F. Cathcart, who wore a fuli-
bottomed wig and buckram-lined coat as if he had
been born in them.
Saturday, opening day of the N.S. W. League of
Wheelmen’s Summer Carnival, proved so cool that
winter garments were resuscitated, and the smell
of moth-brick was loud in the air. The gayest
colours were seen on the riders’ jackets. M‘Donell,
winner of the long race, wheeled round in parrot-
like array, and the boys whistled “ Pretty Joey ”
as he passed.
Mr. A. H. Fullwood, the well-known Sydney
artist, was last week married to Miss Clyda B.
Newman, eldest daughter of Mr. J. Hubert New-
man, of Woollahra.
There was a soul-satisfying air of solid wealth
about the Hebrew concert at the Centenary Hall,
York-street, on Saturday evening last. Thither
the tribes went up in fairly large numbers and
most expensive apparel. Many diamonds were
flashing about in the hall and oh the platform.
Perhaps the snappiest costume was worn by a
Judge’s wife—a duet in black brocade and pink
pleated silk muslin. All the composers and per-
formers were Jewish, including Cowan, Sullivan,
Brack and Mendelssohn. Among the performers
Miss Nellie Young, Mr. Hallewelland HerrStaell
did a turn at the Trebelli concert as well. Mr.
Sydney Moss accompanied, Herr Vollmar con-
ducted, and Miss Kahn, a clever pianist, quar-
relled with her instrument violently in several flats
in a Liszt rapsody.
A pretty and quiet wedding—for a change I
should like to see a “ hideous and vociferous ”
hitching—happened at St. Stephen’s, Willoughby,
on Wednesday of last week, when Miss F. A.
Lamb, daughter of Mr. J. de V. Lamb, was
married to Mr. J. L. Newall, who comes from
England and has taken his bride there per P. and
O. A “ quiet ” ceremony means that the bride
didn’t adorn herself in white satin with yards of
encumbering train. On this occasion Miss Lamb
was sensibly clad in the dress she afterwards de-
parted in, and so saved the guests from under-
going the blank of that “great gap of time ” when
the bride is changing her attire and they have
nothing to do but stand awkwardly around with
handfuls of rice ready to hurl among the tulle and
roses of the bride’s best hat.
The pretty little kiosk lately built near the Art
Gallery had a deluge of a christening on Friday
afternoon, and the opening, for which Mrs. Green-
law and Mrs. Pechey had sent out invitations,
fizzled out. They have taken the tea-rooms on
contract to supply hot and thirsty lovers of art
with refreshments, and a more exquisite landscape
than anything they will find inside the Gallery is
the view of “ our beautiful ’arbor” from the veran-
dah of the kiosk, a dainty structure like a house
out of a new box of toys.
“ Can Australian women cook?” is the conun-
drum awaiting answer just now, and a shuddering
“ No ” is wailed in answer over a whole dyspeptic
continent. Our cooks, though sometimes worthy,
lack variety, have naught of genius, and to-day we
dine as yesterday and many yesterdays. We have
the plain, solid facts of beef and mutton hurled at
us daily, either roast or thinly disguised in hash ;
we lack the imagination to plan a subtle entree or
a pudding that isn’Qfarinaceous. With acres upon
acres of waste land suffering to be used as poultry-
farms we are forced to regard chickens as luxuries,
and when we do seize on some old bird (hatched
most likely at Guvmentouse in Lord Aug. Loftus’
days) and slay him for the table, we crudely bake
him, and haven’t sense enough to mitigate his
muscular development in a stew as do even the
Spaniards, and they are considered the worst
cooks in the world. Worse than that, we fry
chops and serve them up with a floury viscid
gravy. We can even evolve gravy round bacon
and eggs ! In fact, there is no culinary crime that
an Australian backblocks cook isn’t capable of.
Town girls are better. The suburban daughter of
a comfortably-off professional man can usually
turnout a neat little dinner. But she has a fatal
fondness for jam sandwich, and “angel,” “rib-
bon,” and “ rainbow ” cake and other like cakes
easily faked with cochineal and desiccated cocoa-
nut. And only think how we deflect in coffee !
The prevalent craze has struck an eminent sur-
geon’s wife, who begged her husband to get her a
bicycle. “My dear,” he replied, “I am already
making artificial noses for three scorchers.”
There is, I read, at last hope for the bald. At
the London oxygen-treatment hospital, established
by Baroness Burdett-Coutts for the treatment of
ulcers and “incurable” sores, by enclosing the
afflicted part in an air-tight chamber and pump-
ing in half oxygen and half air, it was noticed
that the growth of the hair round the wound was
increased considerably. A rubber cap was fitted
to the head of a prematurely-bald patient, and lo !
a strong head of hair—not ’air—in a brief period.
The hospital has been a great success, though doubt
is expressed as to the permanency of its cures.
My Melb. correspondent writes: —
“ Saturday’s Derby made a record on its own
account as regards coolness. No horse has annexed
the blue ribbon on a more icy day. T® an almost
frozen assemblage, Newhaven, panting and sweat-
ing after his win, seemed the oniy warm thing at
Flemington. ‘ The wind which blows between
the worlds ’ had apparently been laid on for the
occasion, and it empurpled women’s cheeks and
noses, tore at silken skirts, revealing dainty lin-
ings, and disclosed to the curious the fact that the
Melb. girl still delights in ’Murkan foot-wear with
long, attenuated points—-carefully stuffed with
cotton wool at the toe-caps to preserve the sym-
metry.
“ As a dress-parade the Derby is taking the rank
once held by the ‘Great Tuesday.’ Hard times
are teaching Vic. women economy ; and as in a
Cup-day crowd high-priced garments are noted
but little and suffer much from the unavoidable
crushing, the finest raiment is now kept for either
of the two Saturdays. Most of the gowns were of
such airy, flimsy material as to be inconveniently
light in a tropical summer. The many medicos
present must have noted with grim satisfaction
their business going up by leaps and bounds; a
packet of mustard-leaves in the drawing-room
would have been hailed as a seasonable addition
to the toilet-tables. But the south wind wasn’t
responsible for all the red noses present. The buffet
was thronged throughout the day by damsels
demanding ardent liquors to keep out the cold, and
one short-skirted maiden was generously helping a
party of half a dozen girls, all sweet sixteen or so,
to something out of a square black bottle.
“ The Govt. House party all shrouded them-
selves beneath vast unoeautiful wraps. Lady
Idina Brassey, who generally manages to go one
better than anyone else in the matter of guyful
garments, sported a purple pelisse of unique and
astounding ugliness. Lady Brassey’s gown of
Derby-blue silk had skilfully-arranged front and
shoulder flaps of White chiffon, and she wore an
irrelevant-looking little toque of pink roses on
brown straw. Mrs. Freeman-Thomas came out
from under her wrap during the afternoon, and
disclosed a dainty gown of grass lawn over white
silk. Lady Hampden and Lady Magheramorne,
the two best-looking occupants of the vice-regal
pen, were habited in black. Daughter Dorothy
froze gently all day in a white-and-black muslin.
Melbourne’s *"est-dressed girl ’ wore her brother’s
ulster most of the day, and a pink nose and a flam-
boyant waistcoat were the distinguishing charac-
teristics of the Melb. beauty.
“ The wedding of Miss Ethel Rosenthal to Mr.
bydney Phillips, at South Yarra last Wednesday,
was celebrated in an unusually picturesque fashion.
The binding together was done in the quaint,
chapel-like ball-room of “Rhianva,” but the rest
of the affair was entirely al fresco. The wedding-
feast was spread on tete-a-tete tables on the
flower-bordered lawn. The rattling of plates and
glasses was drowned by the music of a brazen
band which hurled, inappropriately enough, the
most cynical portions of the “Faust” music at
the assembled feasters. The bridal pair left for a
Maoriland honeymoon, after which they take up
housekeeping in Sydney.
“ A steady stream of Melb. sassiety girls seems
to have set in ‘on the road to Mandalay.’ Miss
Maud Dudgeon, the other day married in Me'lb.
to Lieut. Manser, should certainly not feel lonely
on arriving, for three Vic. girls are already married
and settled in that nigger-infested city—a daughter
of Col. Bull, governor of Pentridge prison, and
the sisters Connelly, late of Bendigo. The place
where there ain't no ten commandments ’ seems
to possess particular attractions for the Vic.
sassiety beauty.”
W e never mention him now,’ is evidently the
rule in a certain vice-regal mansion since the
escapade of one of its lordlings. At a recent
function an innocent old professor inquired of the
private sec., within the hearing of a score, where
Lord was to-night?’ The sec. hastened to
tell his nearest companion some obviously forced
and irrelevant information, and left the where-
abouts of his colleague a mystery. But most of
them could guess.
“ Since Duncan Gillies left us, his old home
in East Melbourne has been broken up
owing to the death of the elderly lady whose
paying guest he had been for 40 years.
M.L.A. Zox is another such Koh-i-noor, from
a boardinghouse-keeper’s point of view. He has
occupied his present apartments since the child-
hood of his landlady’s pretty daughters, by whom
he has come to be regarded as a beneficent adopted
uncle.
“Talking of mushroom titles: Melb. sassiety
regularly gnashes its teeth at Gov. Brassey
because he ajjparently cannot discriminate be-
tween local blue-bloods and State school-teachers
and such like. In bitterness of heart they dub the
brusque baron ‘ democrat,’ and describe him as a
yesterday.jump-up. 5 Really, to hear them talk,
one would imagine that until the Sunbeam skip-
per picked up a nugget a week or two back he had
habitually scratched his head, chawed raw onions,
and spelled cat with a k.
Meanwhile, the suspected Radical in vice-
regal clothing goes his own goatish way—bikes off
to breakfast with retired haberdashers, and at
theatres shuns the Governor’s enclosure and elbows
his way through the crowd to a side-box, thus
cutting the preliminary “ God Save ” out of the
programme. Malcontents put everything down
to ‘ meanness. ’ They credit the offender with a
European reputation for economy, and ascribe his
fondness for the company of gilded drapers to
pelf-worship.
“ Gov. Brassey has thrown up his experimental
farm in W.A. in disgust because the authorities
over there were chary with their concessions.
Ambitious Melb. parents who meant to send their
boys to His Excellency’s manager as farming-
pupils express themselves as “bitterly disap-
pointed in Sir John Forrest ”!
“ The pet charity of the moment is the Cottage
by the Sea—a resort for convalescent children.
Many subscribers grudge the outlay on furnishing
and decoration, which is finer than youngster
relish, while the building is too small to hold more
than a few inmates. The matron of a similar in-
stitution habitually insisted (and perhaps does
still) on keeping the little convalescents indoors all
Sunday for Sunday-school, church, &c. A fort-
night was the limit of each child’s stay, and two
whole days of fresh air was a big percentage to
lose, but nothing would induce the autocrat to
hear charges their hymns on the beach. Econ-
omic management is the fad of yet another estab-
lishment. The needy parents of a two-year-o'd
with tuberculosis of the hip, got their invalid
admitted with much difficulty, and hoped great
things from the sea-air. But the child looked
little better at the end of her fourteen days—and
small wonder, for, as an attendant formally com-
plained to her father, she had screamed for an
hour every morning because she did not get sumu
with the porridge which constituted her break-
fast.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
The Riverina Frozen Meat Works put up a
record on 28th Sept, by slaughtering 2900 beasts
in one day.
A flock of Red Birds 2000 strong is coming over
the seas to Australia.*
Good rains all over eastern N.S.W., and
just in time to save the country from perdition.
The clergy missed a soft snap by not praying for
the needful last week. Since it comes 'without
being prayed for, the clergy are plainly shirks of
supererogation.
To see what work is like in the wool season
the backblocks person with the robust air ami
sunburnt intelligence is advised to visit the estab-
lishment of John Bridge at Circular Quay, and
watch the up-to-date staff flickering abiiit on
bicycles as models for less progressive salesmen, or
attending to enormous stacks of sheepskins and
hides, or managing the loading of half-a-dozen
big vessels almost at the base of the building.
The firm of John Bridge and Co. is at the s une
time one of the oldest-established and most pro-
gressive of .Sydney wool houses.
PRINCESS S THEATRE. -This (Thursday) evening. -V
fashionable theatre party (numbering upwards of one
hundred) will be given by Sir Wm. and Lady Clarke to
the officers and men of the Rupertswood Battery (who
will attend in uniform), in honor of Her Majesty the
Queen having attained the longest reign of any British
Sovereign. The guests will include Lady Brasse', Lady
Emily Boyle, Captain and the Hon. Mrs. Freeman Thomas,
the Earl of Shaftesbury, Lord Ricnard Nevill, Hon. T. a!
Brassey, Lady Idina Brassey, Miss Street, Lady Hoik'd
Smith, Hon. Wm. M'Culloch, Mr. Rupert Clarke, and
house party.— Melb. Age, Oct. 15.
A QUESTION FOR THE GODS.
I’m a very small soshul pertater,
And I don’t take a nab when 1 go
To the Princess’s amphi-theater
For a commonplace bobsworth of show;
But there’s no admiration more willin’
Than mine for the classes Perlite,
So, of course, I expended a shillin’
On Rupertswood Battery Night,
And I found it a glorious sight—
I did—
A ’appy and glorious sight.
After waitin’ from twenty parst seven,
1 mannidged to get a good seat
With the gords (it was really like ’Eaven),
Where a cove could enjoy the elect.
Well, I leaves yer to guess my emoshun
When the band played a nashernal air
And a thrill of h’instinctive devotion
Informed me their Nibses was there,
I laid myself out for a stare,
I did-
A ’orty serciety stare.
The “ Chinatown ” Company acted
A drarmer that seemed rather sorft,
Whilst the nobs in the circle distracted
The thorts of us cherubs alorft;
I paid very little attenshun
To things wot occurred on the boards,
Havin’ come with the simple intenshun
Of sniffin’ at ladies and lords.
Thinks I—“ Wot a chance this affords !
My word !
This the charnce of a lifetime affords.”
The part o’ the prograrme I studied
(Except when I glanced at the stage)
Was a cast of performers blue-blooded,
Whose names ’ad been starred in the Age.
All them mummers I wished at the devil,
For they seemed to be trying to foil
My attempt to discover Lord Nevill
And fair Lydy Emily Boyle—
’Er ’ands isn’t ’ardened by toil.
You bet!
She don’t earn ’er livin’ by toil.
I can’t take my h’oath, not for certin’
But I’m pretty well sure, all the same,
That before it was time for the curtin’
I knew ev’ry noble by name.
Still there’s one thing I couldn’t discover
(No more can my bloomin’ old gurl,
Who’s as wise as they make ’em, Lord love ’er f
Though she don’t put ’er ’air into curl) —
Is “ House Party ” a dook or a earl,
D’yer know,
A Orstralian dook or a earl ?
