Personal Items.
Speaker Abbott is said to have his eye on
the N.S.W. Agent-Generalship.
Justice Cohen dislikes being called a
Hebrew. “ I’m simply a Jew,” he says.
Mrs. Sam Lyons, wife of Premier Turner’s
senior partner, has just auctioned her sumptuous
furniture.
Mason, apparently the next Vic. Speaker,
was once schoolmaster on the Government train-
ing-ship Nelson.
Maoriland poet Tom Bracken has de-
veloped goitre badly. A national subscription
is being organised for him.
Mr. R. JEFFERY , a champion rifle-shot of
Australia.
Photo, by Kerry.
Mrs. Harrison Lee is in England advocat-
ing the limited company matrimonial ideas which
long ago excited the ire of Chas. Kingsley.
“ George Dibbs, one of the grandest men
in the country. ... I regard him with the
most cordial affection and esteem.” —Butterscotch
Reid.
Premier Reid : “A man of sense ought to
be satisfied with plain ‘ Mister,’ and, if I accept a
title, my friends may be sure that I’ve gone a good
bit ‘off.’”
“ Gone to ask her Father to send for me ”
is the uncomfortable inscription put by a Melb.
widower on the tombstone, as a bar to his sighing
after No. 2.
Louis Becke’s projected Pacific trading co.
appears to be, to some extent, based on expected
State concessions in the Philippines and other
Spanish possessions.
Melb. Health-officer Dr. James Jamieson
is an enthusiastic and insatiable bibliophile. His
collection of first editions is perhaps the finest in
the seven Australias.
Purves, Q.C., is said to meditate a trip to
Europe. Purves, though Australian, had a Con-
tinental education —much at Berlin, which he has
not seen for a generation.
Edwin Arnold’s poetry on Queen Victoria's
long reign, received by late mail, makes his veteran
relative, a Surry Hills (Sydney) boarding-house
keeper, gnash his infrequent teeth.
The largest area under cultivation in Aus-
tralasia belongs to John Grigg, of Longbeach,
Maoriland. That worthy cultivates 14.000 acres
of freehold and 1000 acres of reserves.
There have been seven Presidents of the
Leg. Council and eight Speakers of the Assembly
since N.S.W. received Responsible Government.
Three Speakers survive, but only one President.
Nat Levi, M.L.C., was the first Jew ever
returned to Vic. Parliament. He beat brainy R.
D. Ireland in ’6O, when the latter, having accepted
office in the first Heales Ministry, had to stand
for re-election.
How Australian artistic and scientific
societies lickspittle wealth and rank ! “ Madden,
C.J., President of Royal Geographical Society of
Australasia vice Mueller.” That is the latest.
Madden vice Mueller as a scientist!
The recent prominence of Col. Morgan
recalls an incident of the Barrier strike when
Morgan was on the Proprietary. Assailed by a
number of strike sympathisers, the kernel drew
his “gun” from his hip-pocket and drawled:
“Waal, I don’t keer whether yew send me tew
Heaven or hell; I’ve got heaps of friends in both
places. But, by the living jingo ! I’ll fetch some
of yew with me.” The Bulletin wonders m
which mythical quarter the majority of Morgan s
friends are located.
Dear Bulletin, — Be the mystery of Julian
Thomas’s birthplace. I heard him say several
times that his father was a Virginian, and that he
was born on the Janies River in Virginia which,
perhaps, accounts for his once taking the name of
Sydney James. Of his mother he never spoke.
He was at school in America, and ran away when
twelve years old, was brought back, and ran away
again a year later and joined a famous slaver
captain whose name I forget. He made several
voyages, and on the last was left on the Guinea
coast through his ship being chased by an English
cruiser; thence he made his way overland and
coastwise to the Gape; then to England, where
he seems to have had a good time, as he spoke of
owning a schooner-yacht. Two years ago he said
definitely that his age was 63. He also mentioned
that he owned 500 or 600 acres of land on the
James River which was valueless on account of
arrears of the land-tax, which he commended.—
W.E.S.
In Dariinghurst Gaol is lying William
Dunn, sentenced to death by Justice Windeyer
on Bth Nov., 1882, for arson in George-street,
Sydney. A woman, who was on the premises un-
known to Dunn, lost her life in jumping from &
window. The criminal set fire to the premises in
order to obtain the ss. given by the Insuranoe
Fire Brigade for first information.
Salomons and Pilcher, Q.C.C., are both
off to Europe again. These able and lucky gentle-
men have now got into the habit of visiting Sydney
for a few months, working like demons, raking in
a few thousands and then going ’Ome for a good
time, allee same Chung Cow and Go Bung from
Canton. In a word, Ah Salomons and Ah
Pilcher. Also Japaneee Jack Want.
Scene : Swan Hill (Vic.) railway-platform,
during passage of Gov. Brassey to Mildura.
H.Ex. patiently waiting while the local Bom-
bastes clears his throat preparatory to mouthing
the “ address ” in eulogy of Her Gracious, her
minions and dominions. The local stock abori-
ginal—washed, combed and generally titivated for
the occasion—and with the thirst for what the
white pfeller’s tchillin would prooure him—
silently edges alongside Her Majesty’s representa-
tive, tugs at his coat-tail, and softly, oh, so softly,
whispers in his ear : “You give it pfive tchillin,
Mr. Gubnor.” Mr. Gubnor imperiously brushes
away poor Jacky; but, unabashed, the sable
warrior returns and again softly whispers: “ Him
nudder white pyfeller tell it me you gib it pfive
tchilin ; only pfive tchillin, Mr. Gubnor ! ” plead-
ingly. Here they haul the deluded blackfellow off
without his having received even the proverbial
“ Gillies browney.” And now the greeting from
the black man to the white man is : “ What you
make it me fool fo’? You tell it me him blenty
good pfeller ! What for (disgustedly) you white
pfeller hab it nudder plurry mean pfeller for Gub-
ment man for ? ”
An American tells The Bulletin that
many years ago, ere Pastor Bevan came to minis-
ter in the leading Melb. Congregational church,
he was given the pastorate of the wealthiest
Presbyterian congregation in New York city —the
“ brick ” church on the corner of Thirty-eighth-st.
and Fifth-avenue, at a salary of 10,000dol. per
annum and a palatial manse. The rev. gentleman,
however, “ queered his pitch ” on his first Sun-
day. The church was crammed to hear the new
Boanerges from across the pond, and shortly after
he had begun preaching a lady rose and marched
out —possibly overcome by the heat. Dr. Bevan
stopped short, and remarked that as soon as “that
woman ” had ceased disturbing the congregation
he would proceed. “That woman’’proved to be
a member of a very high-toned Knickerbocker
family, and the poor pastor’s unfortunate slip
brought quite a hornet’s nest about his ears.
Several of the leading papers alluded to the
occurrence, and no doubt the unhappy contre-
temps was a potent factor in his speedy return to
England. In America you mustn’t call even the
char-lady a “ woman.”

“ Australian literary talent has no show in
Australia unless it has the hall-mark of London
approval,” says Louis Beoke. Melbourne Leader,
commenting, says : “ This conclusion coming from
Mr. Becke savours of ingratitude.” On this point
it is wise for papers like the Leader to be dumb.
Throughout the couple of years or so during
which Becke’s stories, the ones that made him
famous, were running in The Bulletin, and
whilst the Leader was filling up with the humor
of the immortal Bowsers —made in America,—
neither the weekly Syme nor any other solid,
respectable organ of its class discovered the
slightest merit in him, or dared to hint that his
work was deserving of a second thought. The
same remark applies to other Bulletin men who
have scored in England.

Mr. R. Jeffery is essentially a champion
shot on the rifle range : he has never pointed gun
or rifle in the sporting field, nor drawn trigger on
anything but the bull’s-eye of the target. His
shooting experience even at that has been com-
paratively little and intermittent. An English-
man, he began at the age of 16 by joining the
Isle of Wight Rifle Volunteers, and for some four
years with them he had casual and amateurish
practice. When 21 years old he came to Queens-
land, but throughout six years did not fire one
shot. Then he joined the Q. volunteer rifles, and
put in his Saturday afternoons at Toowong range
—the worst in Australia, sinoe the sun setting
slap on the targets would almost dazzle an eagle’s
eye. In ’9l there, Mr. Jeffery obtained his first
win—a second place in the Queen’s competition.
He returned to England then and won nothing,
came back to Brisbane, went to Melbourne, prac-
tised with the rifle club there; came to Sydney
six months ago, and practised with the Sydney
Rifle Club the last three months. He familiarised
himself with the Rand wick range’s light effects,
studied its peculiarities of hillocks and gullies, learnt
the moods and ways of its wind-currents. And
when the competition came he beat everybody,
Australian or other. He won the Jaques prize
with 65 points out of 70, the Manning with
67, and the Dangar with 64. His target
in the Dangar 900yds. was a pioture ; five bull’s-
eyes and two centres —33 out of 35. The perfec-
tion of this shooting, with _ the Bisley foresight
and orthoptic disc thrown in, is all the more re-
markable beoause Mr. Jeffery, shooting in a twi-
light which was quickly fading into darknesß,
wound up with three bull’s-eyes. His winnings
for the meeting total <£111; the evergreen Sergt.
Keating comes next with .£7O. Mr. Jeffery is a
moderate drinker and regular smoker, but during
competition-time abandons both glass and pipe;
“ they unsteady the nerves, and smoking also dulls
the eyesight.” He thinks the merit of Aus. rifle-
shooting is rising. He is 34 years old, and by
avocation a jobbing-compositor. He has light-
brown eyes, and remarks that light eyes are gener-
ally understood to shoot best.

“A REMARKABLE PHENOMENON.”
“A telegram received yesterday by the N.S.W. Government Astronomer, from Coonabarabran, reports the occurrence of a remarkable phenomenon. A loud rumbling sound was heard from below the surface, which appeared to travel in a south-easterly direction. The farmers working in the
fields were much alarmed at the inexplicable sound, doubtless fearing a disturbance of the earth’s surface.” —Daily PAPER.
“ That rumbling sound” has been heard pretty generally throughout N.S.W. since the Land-tax assessments were sent out, and there has been a very decided “disturbance of the earth's surface.” “But,” says the man with the eyeglass, in the distance, “people
will get accustomed to it in time, and think nothing of it!”

GEO. H. BENNETT, M.P.,
Tells you to use Warner’s Safe Cure.
One of Melbourne’s foremost citizens and well-
known business men, Geo. H. Bennett, M.P., in
a letter Oct. 5,1895, says : “A friend of mine ad-
vised me to try Warner’s Safe Cure for the liver.
I did so, and I feel sure that if many people who
are suffering from liver complaint only knew how
easily they could be cured by Warner’s Safe Cure,
they would not delay in obtaining it.”
If your liver is perfectly sound ; if you are
never giddy, never have a sour stomach, with
flatulency, a bitter, oily taste in the mouth on
rising ; if you never suffer pains in the side or under
shoulder-blades; if you always sleep well during
the night and are never drowsy during the day;
if you do not see specks before the eyes, nor suffer
nervous excitability ; if you never have sick head-
aches, or are not troubled with occasional sickness,
the above testimonial will not interest you. If
you suffer from any of the above symptoms, why
should you hesitate one moment longer to employ
this remedy, knowing it is a positive cure ? You
owe it to youself, family, and friends, to seek suoh
means as will ensure good health and longevity.

a customs duty of 2s. 6d. per ton, but it got there
just the same.

S.M. Herald reports Premier Reid as
having declared that the Referendum Bill is “ the
first proposal of the kind made in a British-
speaking Parliament.” The British language?
Sample of it: “ Pogue an arran huich usquebaugh
I dunno, but somewhere about the firmament;
salaam sahib, wockalongamaloo, bangshwang,
plenty talkee, exactly ” —or words to that effect.
By Gum ! Let us invent an Australian language.
Brisbane Courier logic: “In Victoria
Democratic forces are in power, and Victoria is in
a state of unparalleled depression. In Queens-
land Democratic forces are not in power, and
Queensland is about the most prosperous of the
colonies.” The Courier does not point out that
the depression in Victoria is the direct result of the
very same kind of legislation that is paramount in
Queensland to-day, and that Democratic legisla-
tion is only in its cradle in the former province.
As to attributing Queensland prosperity to the
rule of the Boodlewraith one might as well attri-
bute the Westralian revenue increase to the rule of
the Six Families.

For 1895 Brisbane is beaten in the matter
of exports both by Townsville and Rockhampton,
the latter coming out half-a-million ahead of the
capital. On the other hand, the imports of Bris-
bane are six times those of Rockhampton, and
four times those of Townsville. A volume on the
need for tripartite autonomy couldn’t say more
than these few figures.
John Haynes, M.P., may fairly claim that,
though he did not carry bis Abolition of Capital
Punishment Bill right on to the statute-book of
N.S.W., he has yet placed on record in its favor
such a pronounced expression of Parliamentary
opinion that any Executive, except an avowedly
hanging one, will feel morally and politically jus-
tified in cheating the gallows.
Speaker Abbott’s proclaimed determination
to preserve the precedents of the Chair in relation
to the appointment of a refreshment-room waiter
harmonises badly with the new precedent created
by himself the other day, whereby he bluffs the
public out of their ancient right to enter the
Strangers’ Gallery of the Assembly without the
intervention of the formal ticket.
A. C. Begg is a candidate for Dunedin city
at next elections. His quality is shown by his
selling his Roslyn tram-shares recently because
the cars were run on Sunday, by his advocacy
of prohibition, and his platform pronouncement
that the workingman had no r’gh: to starve,
because plenty of sheep's heads and plucks were
to be had free at the freezing-works.

A Northerner tells The Bulletin that the
Q. Chinese have a trick of posting their “ natural-
isation” papers to friends in China, and thus
enabling the latter to enter Queensland. One
Tingha Chinaman boasted that his papers had
been “home”2o times, and had brought out as
many Chows. “If they must come,” remarks the
correspondent, “why not give them a substitute
paper, and return the original when they clear
out ? ”
A well-informed Maoriland correspon-
dent :
We are now plunged into the electioneering vortex. I
fully expect we shall emerge from it with a big majority
of Liberals, unpledged to Seddon and determined on
having the Referendum, Elective Executive, and perhaps
Hare System of Voting, though the latter is doubtful.
The Cold-tea party will gain no seats. The old Conserva-
tive party is doing a hari-kari “on its own.” Clutha
Tam MacKenzie is retiring to a lucrative billet in London ;
Duthie is sliding out of politics to devote his high talents
to tin-tacks and cash ; Button is giving up the game in
holy disgust (he’s a Christian lawyer and Y.M.C. A social
purity patriarch); J. G. Wilson, a decent squatter,
ashamed of the crew, is going into private life—his wife is
a well-known poetess ; Mitehelson got such a fright over
the Bank enquiry that he also wants to retire, but Eden
folks are pressing him to stay. Of the few who are left,
most stand a fair show of getting licked, including Capt.
Russell and Dr. Nevvman. “ Dismal Bill ” Rolleston is
trying to re-enter politics by the door of a different con-
stituency from that which rejected him three years ago,
but with no hope of success. Our politics will therefore
take a new shape after the elections. Stout, Geo. Hut-
chison, and Bell will probably be the only Conservatives
left in the House.
There are but five
Catholics in S.A. Parlia-
ment. Of these one is
Speaker, one Chief Secre-
tary, and one Lands Minis-
ter, while another is the
best debater in the Labor-
party. The fifth has just
been elected to fill the
vacancy caused by the death
of Lands Minister Gillen,
who also was a R.C.
A Yic. parson writes
to the press in an exasper-
ated tone of voice concern-
ing the “One-man-one-
vote” idea : “ Thus the vote
of a gentleman who owns
thousands, and has the res-
ponsibility of a high social
and hereditary position,
may be wholly nullified—
ten times over, in fact—by
the votes of ten of his
grooms; certainly a posi-
tion lacking both ordinary
common-sense and reason-
able national security.”
That is just what the one-
man-one-vote idea is in-
tended for. The “gentle-
man ” in question is no
bigger than a groom, and
has no more legs or features
or head, and he doesn’t live
any longer, and if he and
the groom both lost their
clothes there would be just
as much dignity in one as
in the other, so the thing
doesn’t look so very aston-
ishing after all. But what
does seem astonishing, when
you look at it carefully, is
that a parson who is paid
by the poor, and who is em-
ployed to show the poor the
road to a heaven where
nobody will have a cent,
and who is supposed to
follow a Redeemer who
was too low down to be
even a groom, should be
allowed to issue such amaz-
ing rot. There is something
surprising in that when any-
one lets the circumstance
soak in.
Mackay planters are moving now to secure
the enactment of a maximum wage of <£2o per
annum for the “free” Kanaka, that individual
to be penalised in case he declines to accept!
Extend this sort of thing to Chows, Japs., and
Hindoos, and Australia as a white man’s country is
doomed. The planters deny that the Kanaka is a
slave. What is the difference in principle between
instituting absolute chattel slavery and enacting
that a man shall not be entitled to receive more
than a certain amount for his work ?
A Queenslander of many years’ standing,
who lately revisited N.S.W. Leg. Assembly after
an absence of over a score of years, compares the
manners and appearances of the present members
favorably with those of the old ones. Members on
the horseshoe bend particularly impressed him.
“ Legislation is taken seriously now,” he says;
“there’s a look of work about the shop which
U3edn’t to be there. Why, I remember playing
‘nap’ at the ‘bend,’ where now all is watchful
alertness to trip Government or Opposition.”
Bismarck’s latest revelation expounds a
beautiful piece of villainy. According to his
story Germany maintained, at one and the same
time, a treaty with Austria and Italy by which
they were to stand or fall together in case of an
attack on any one of the three, and a treaty with
Russia whereby it was pledged not to come to the
rescue of its allies or any one else if Russia was
the assailant. The cable mildly observes that
William of Germany has written to Franz Josef
“ explaining ” the matter. But there isn’t enough
explanation in all the dictionaries of Europe to
cover such a situation.

THE SEVENTH REPORT of the Directors of THE NATIONAL BANK OE
AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
The Directors beg- to report to the shareholders as follows in connection with the business of the bank for the
naif-year terminating 30th September last
The net profit, less expenses of management, interest paid and accrued, rebate, &c.,
Amounted t 0... ... ... ... ... ... ...£14,850 7 3
Balance from previous half-year ... ... ... ... ... 12,436 13 5
The increase to the paid-up capital of the bank has amounted to £52,280 10s. during the half-year, the total now
standing at £1,918,211 9s. 9d.
As intimated in last report, the date of payment of one-fifth of the deferred liabilities of the bank was anticipated
in May last, and, encouraged by the success of that movement, the directors dealt similarly with another like amount
of the"same liabilities in September, thus reducing the amount of the long-dated fixed deposits from £4,906,732 Is. 6d.
to £3,265,633 12s. 6d. during the six months.
One of the auditors appointed by the shareholders at the general meeting in May last having desired to be relieved
of his duties at the September balance, the directors appointed Mr. W. G. Sprigg to act on that occasion in conjunc-
tion with Mr. W. H. Tuckett.
The Ordinary General Meeting will be held on Wednesday, 4th November, at noon, at the head office, Collins-
street, Melbourne.
By order of the Board of Directors.
F. G. SMITH, Chief Manager.
The National Bank of Australasia Limited,
Head Office, Melbourne, 23rd October, 1896.

THE SEVENTH REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS
—OF—
THE COLONIAL BANK OE AUSTRALASIA LIMITED,
Presented to the Shareholders at the SEVENTH ORDINARY GENERAL MEETING,
To be held at the Bank, 126 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, at noon on
THURSDAY, 29th OCTOBER, 1896.
Report.
The Directors beg to submit to the shareholders their Seventh Report, with a Balance-Sheet and Statement of
Profit and Loss for the half-year ended 30th September, duly audited.
After providing for expenses of management, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current, and tax on
note circulation, the net profit, including the balance brought forward from previous half-year, amounted to £5591
14s. 2d., which the Directors recommend to be carried forward to next half-year.
The accounts placed before you disclose an increase in the volume of business, but the profits for the half-year
have been materially affected by the continuous reductions in the rates of interest.
The amendment of the Companies Act, referred to in previous reports, giving power to reduce capital, not having
been passed by Parliament, the capital cannot yet be written down in order to provide for losses.
The Seventh Ordinary General Meeting of Shareholders will be held at the Head Office of the Company, 12(1
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, on Thursday, the 29th day of October, 1890, at noon.
By order of the Board,
SELBY PAXTON, General Manager.
Melbourne, 22nd October, 1896.
Balazic e-Sh.eet, 30th Sept.,

P. E. Marsden, the New South Wales
cyclist, put up a Tasmanian record for the half-
mile—on a Bostedo—which he covered in
lmin. 9 l-ssec. from a standing start. He was
unpaced. Marsden will also undertake to break
the Melbourne-Sydney road-record early in
November on a “ Bostedo.” *

Mr. Arthur Griffith wishes to notify intend-
ing patentees that he has commenced practice as a
Patents and Trade Marks Agent at 39 Castlereagh-
street, Sydney. He has entered into arrange-
ments with leading agents in Europe, America,
Australasia, and South Africa, so that inventors
may rely upon having their interests protected.
Mr. Griffith’s charges will, in all cases, be so
moderate as to place a patent within the reach of
the poorest inventor. For £2O, exclusive of the
cost of plans, Mr. Griffith will obtain Provisional
Protection for any invention in the seven Austra-
lasian colonies and Great Britain.*

claimed the exuberant Premier Reid. “ I’ll live

offered a Right Honorable—which title he imme-
diately clapped on his visiting-cards. And Stout
and Berry and Dibbs and numbers of others pro-
tested loudly in the same strain before the event.
A politician’s protest in such matters is like a
school-girl’s cry, “ I’ll never get married.”
Mr. Justice Innes, of N.S.W. Supreme
Court, who died in England last week, was a good
horseman in his early days. When C.P.S. at
Sofala during the first Turon rush he had a mount
at Tambaroora races. Finding he was not weight
he utilised gold-dust in the charge of the Com-
missioner, rode his race, and, as “a memory”
says, won. On the course that day were men who,
had they known the character of the 141 b. make-
weight carried by the young jockey, would have
thought nothing of lying in wait at the back of the
course and “ dealing it out ” to him.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The opening event of the V.R.C. meeting pro-
duced a painful display of crude horsemanship. The
boys on Reka and St. David, who made a dead-heat, were
flogging away at their mounts in approved donkey-race
fashion, when one jockey’s whip smote the wrong horse
across the nose. An appeal for “ unintentional interfer-
ence ” was upheld by the stewards. There were but three
competitors in the struggle ; one of them had dropped
out of it long before the finish, and yet the other two,
with the full width of the course at their disposal, got
locked together.
Newhaven’s unique Derby-win was a satire on
the prophets’ general expectation of a good race. Had
the runaway bolted off the course after going a mile,
Resolute would have been reckoned a decisive winner.
Nothing troubled the St. Albans colt with the exception
of Officer and Coil, and they came up to him at the
distance-post only to drop back again, beaten by half a
length or so. It was a matter of Newhaven outpacing
Resolute, who, for his little part, was equal to beating the
other six animals after making all the running from them.
The easy winner and the far-away second, judging from
their unruffled appearance after the Derby, could have
gone further ahead respectively, in a two-mile race. Coil
and The Officer were comparatively “baked.”
Resolute’s near fore-foot is the very dicky one
that needs particular attention. When the shoe was
removed an hour before the Derby, the big colt didn’t
care to press it on the ground, but a light plate made
things comfortable again. He ran, of course, in plates (on
the two front iioofs only), and showed no sign of lameness
after the gallop.
Cup betting this time testified to the book-
makers’ opinion of the older horses. Carnage, a brilliant
Derby winner, started at 12 to 1 for the Cup, and other
pre-eminent youngsters in Strathmore, The Admiral, and
Trident were hackahle at a fair price on their days for the
2-mile handicap, seeing that they had first-class veterans
to run against. This year there was nothing to represent
“class” except Newhaven and perhaps Resolute.
“Everybody knew ” that Newhaven’s running
in the Caulfield Guineas was all wrong, and that he would
win the Derby as he liked. All the same, everybody
allowed the colt to start at 2 to 1 against him in a small
field, and but for his having been an early favorite New-
haven would have gone out at a longer price than 2's.
Strange mistakes the Millers make with their
horses when they come to deal with big events on the
fiat. They reckoned the common-looking Buridook quite
good enough to get a place in the Derby, hut had the
mortification of seeing him tailed off at the end of a mile.
Sydney sports had a bad Derby Day at Flem-
ington, their ill-luck beginning with the disqualification
of Reka in the Trial Stakes.
As far as the present writer could see, the
strangest feature of Broken Hill's performance in the
Hotham Handicap was the outside price that he started
at. If David James and co. really fancier! Broken Hill for
the Cup, why didn’t they hack him heavily for the race on
Saturday, which was a sufficiently long journey to give
the Cup-horse a chance to outstay most of them ?
After all, the most in-and-out horse-racing is
rather more consistent than the performances of bike
champions, barring an honorable exception in the case of
Bilmartin.
Coil is better than Le Var, who beat Resolute
in the Caulfield Cup, nevertheless Resolute beat Coil in
the Derby.
* ♦ * *
Newhaven’s easy win in the Melbourne Cup,
with Bloodshot and The Skipper second and third, has at
least one merit: it i 3 something of a knock to the Ikey
Mo brigade. Newhaven, who led all the way, as in the
Derby, started favorite, and it is rarely that a favorite
scoops the big prize. At the same time, it seems doubtful
if this favorite carried anything like the money that
might have been expected, and it is quite possible
that the Tribes have made a solid profit out
of the Cup, though not the profit they ex-
pected. Anyhow, there was a lot of money on
Resolute, Hopscotch, Marusa, and various other animals
which didn't show anywhere in particular. The second
animal was a 100 to 2 chance, and The Skipper started at
about 100 to 3, and so far as second and third places were
concerned the tipsters were mostly far out. Tiie Bulle-
tin here rises proudly to state that it said Bloodshot
wouldn't win, so its tip had something to recommend it.
* * * *
The English critics think Daimio “ a big lump of
a horse lacking quality, scarcely looking thoroughbred-
in fact, a good sort of a van horse.” The Eli dish handi-
cappers are evidently determined, however, that he shall
not he in the van in England if they can stop him.

Since the Melb. tote raids the betting shops
have to take extra precautions, and guard against the
visits of the female detectives. Any stranger who enters
a cigar shop to enquire the odds is at once ejected, while
lady punters are politely informed that betting is illegal,
and consequently non-existent. Still business goes on as
before, and the talent sport buggies and diamonds as usual.

article, by “ Sterling” (the veteran Bradshaw), containing
elaborate “tips” for Melb. Cup and other events at the
V.R.O. spring meeting. Braddy's prediction for the Cup
was “ Satn Cook’s best” —Broken Hill, Sabretache, Duhal-
low, or Marusa.

* * * *
The interminable Dawson question turned up at
Vic. Rowing Association again last week, when the hack-
boneless body decided by a large majority not to allow
cash-cyciists to compete as amateur-oarsmen. A list of
proscribed professional sports was made out, cycling and
sailing being among them, but ultimately “ His Excel-
lency’s favorite sport ” was deleted. As things stand, a
man may sail yachts for cash prizes without let or hin-
drance, but if, as cyclist Dawson did, he starts in one race
which he loses, he is deemed unfit to associate any longer
with gentlemen amateurs. The majority responsible
for the adoption of this regulation contained few of the
known oarsmen of the province, and was composed prin-
cipally of spindle-shanked harrier boys, who pretend to.
row during summer to keep them in form for their excit-
ing winter recreation.
The decision has had the effect of causing four
of the principal Vic. clubs to announce their intention of
seceding from the association and forming a new body,
whore the cash-cyclist or the cash-anything-else will be re-
cognised as an amateur oarsman while he sticks to trophies
in that particular sport. The principle of a professional
in one sport being a professional in all is utterly illogical,
and the defectionists are bound to knock out the old
association sooner or later, considering the growth of
cycling. In another year or two every man will have a
bike and nearly every young man will race, and the sole
effect of the V.R.A.’s resolution will be to impoverish the
ranks of its own clubs.
Not content with doing its best to kill row-
ing, the harrier crowd is trying to work up dissension in
cycling. A week or two back, the amateur cyclists ap-
proached the (so far) professional Vic. League of Wheel-
men, and, a conference being held, relations were so
cordial that speedy amalgamation seemed certain. The
amateurs would, by such a course, gain everything and
lose nothing. They would participate in the profits of
the League meetings by reason of substantial trophies ;
two races of their class would be placed on each pro-
gramme, and they would share in the immense touring
advantages which the League member possesses over the
ordinary cyclist. But such a close association with the
“ kawmon ” professionals seems to have “ narked ” that
portion of the amateur cyclists who regard the running of
20 miles through mud and rain as the greatest of all
pleasures, and an indignant protest against the Amateur
Association having “ any connection with a professional
body ” has arisen in a weird wail. It is to be hoped, in
their own interests, that the majority of the trophy men
have sufficient sense to pay no attention to the shriek,
and let the shriekers do their worst.

The Sorrows of Satin —the Melb. Bicycle Club’s
squeal about the L.V.W. color regulations.
* * * *
Bilmartin’s disqualification will mean a heavy
loss to him. During the next six weeks the various
cycling meetings contain half-a-dozen championships of
£2O or more apiece which were all Bill’s own, not to men-
tion a score of open handicaps and the big Austral prize
itself. Bill is bound to be near, if not on, scratch in this
event, but when 300 sovs. are waiting at the other end of
two miles there is nothing short of 4cwt. of bricks tied on
his hack wheel that would keep him far off. Besides, the
increased number of entries (178) means that in the final
heat competitors are hound to l>e distributed fairly well
over all the marks, so that the back men will have a fair
chance of being paced up.

Cyclists Megson, Payne and Lewis left for Aus-
tralia on Friday last, the sum of their achievements in
England seeming to be that Bayne got fined ss. for riding
without a light.
♦ * » *
A couple of years back, when Australian cycling
was almost a thing unknown in England and America,
the leading wheel journal in each of those countries sub-
mitted to its readers a full-page portrait of “ Mr. R. V.
Sprocket, the Australian speed king,” with accompanying
fulsome letterpress. In due course copies reached this
country. Local cyclists were astounded, for the name of
“speed-king” Sprocket was utterly unknown in cycling
circles here. Ultimately, however, one adventurous youth
discovered the missing monarch on the 320 yard mark in
a two-mile event, where he has been ever since, and is
likely to remain. Who paid for the insertion of that puff
is a mystery.
* ♦ * *
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League had such good luck
with the weather last Saturday that it gave vent to its
feelings at 3 p.m. by depositing haif-a-cartload of glasses
and bottles under the reporters’ table.
The racing was extremely satisfactory, because
the unexpected happened every time, and because the
accidents were frequent and picturesque. That in the
last race was everything that could he desired. Some
dozen riders in the final sprint suddenly came crash ; the
last of the bunch striking the prostrate bodies of their
fellows, shot high in air as if thrown from a circus spring-
board, and fell ker-slump. Whereupon several under-
takers hopped over the fence, dodged the police, and
rushed forward to leave their cards upon the corpses.
Alas ! machines were mangled, but not even one little
bone was broken.
The unduly-abused Orient Plate could have been
much worse. The race was a carefully planned attempt
to dislodge the ponderous curse that roosts upon the back
of every 25-mile race. But, after all, the curse won. Up
to the 20th lap the struggles for the £1 pacing prizes kept
public interest jogging, but then the Curse began to tell;
it brought down Carpenter, Walne, and MTnnes, and
threw Middleton against the fence, where he lay stunned
with his arm-muscles torn. Later on Davison fell, and
when the distance was but half through only seven out
of 33 starters were crawling round the course. M‘Donell
and Body here got away from the field, and pacing one
another, lap and lap about, took the £1 pacing prizes in
turn. Finally, after lapping the field, they sprinted each
other home for first prize. M'Donell won ; his cut out of
the race was £7l; £5O first prize, and £2l pacing prizes.
Ken Lewis, in his heat of the League Cup, rode
a difficult race with judgment, but in the Orient Plate he
much disappointed his admirers. He made a fatal error
in underrating Body’s lasting powers, and in hanging
back in consequence among the ruck, while M'Donell and
Body inch by inch stole oiff. In the beginning, M‘Donell
wanted Lewis to pace with him lap and lap about, for the
express purpose of slipping away from the field. Lewis,
however, considering that the game was his, preferred
playing a lone hand. It ended by his being lapped by
M'Donell and Body, and being able to only just snatch
the miserable third money (£6) from the game little
M'Coombe.
Davison, the Hillgrove plugger, promises to
never have a sprint. In his heat of the League Cup,
while he builocked his hardest, big-gear Pither caught
him 10yds. just after starting. And although he came
through his field and won, it was only because he plugged
steadily from start to finish.
M‘Coombe, in his heat of the League Cup (half-
mile) sprinted the last 500yds. in fine style, arid won easily
from Chapman. M'Coombe is an improved rider, and,
next to Martin, the most consistent rider upon the track.
His honesty in always racing up to his form will debar him
from ever winning a big prize ; he is not class enough to
run straight and win big money.
Parsons was called up. He started in his League
Cup heat with a lightning sprint—simply jumped the
20yds. that was between him and Kellow’s wheel. When
the bell rang Parsons seemed to have the field at his
mercy, but coming into the straight against the strong
wind he died right away, and couldn’t get placed. The
racing board called on him for an explanation, which was
rather unnecessary, because Parsons must ride to win
every time in order to keep his reputation up. The win-
ner of Parsons’ heat was Percy Hunter, the League’s
secretary.
• * • *
Boidi and Guiseppe, the Italian cyclists, were
popular with the Adelaide crowd, who were alternately
calling out “Ice-cream!” and asking “Where’s the
monkey?” The average Australian has a fixed idea,
founded on years of agony, that every Italian either sells
ice-cream or grinds an organ.
* * * *
The men who have been road-racing throughout
the winter came out on top at the initial meet of Melb.
cycling season. Road-champion J. Carpenter won the
chief handicap, also the first-class mile. His success in
the last-named event was helped by a piece of over-
smart pacing by little brother George; who got off
lightly with a fine of £1 for his share in the transac-
tion. In a fair-run race Clinton would probably have
won.
The Bulletin stated last season that Ken.
Lewis in proper trim would probably prove the best of the
Australasian riders ; and this season's results, so far, bear
out the opinion. Ken. has a fine turn of speed, but
suffers from asthma, and is, consequently, erratic in his
training.
All the Melbourne training-tracks are now
crowded, and there must be quite twice as many men in
training as there were at the end of last season. There
are the usual batch of promising novices, and one or two
quite out of the common. High gears are much less in
favor amongst the majority, and there will probably be a
further reaction towards 70 to 76 inch.
A further prediction of The Bulletin’s that
75 per cent, of Melbourne riders this season would mount
either American or locally-made machines has been more
than fultilled. Hardly a new English machine is to be
seen on any of the tracks, and there are between 400 and
500 men in training.
* * * *
The Y.R.C. vents its spite on the competing
cyclists by imposing a fine—for it is nothing more—of Is.
on every machine entering the course. As there is no
accommodation provided and the terms of the permissive
occupancy from the Govt, give no power to levy such
blackmail, the charge is utterly indefensible.
* * * *
Sydney Cricket Ground people hope with their
new cycling track, with its world-unique accommodation
and big cash prizes, to make a huge haul out of the
cycling boom. The club argues to itself that big money
will command the riders, the public gods ; that the gods
and the splendid accommodation will command the public
—as for the rest—may not the S.C.G’s hand hold the rope
which shall ring the death-knells of the N.S.W. Wheel-
men’s League and the rival show ground !
* * * *
The American cycle-trade papers predict that
the cycle will have as good an innings next year as this,
that the demand will be as pressing, and prices as high.
The craze still reigns in N.S.W. ; several Sydney agencies
having not a wheel for sale.
* ♦ * *
In the States road-racers have wide-tyred bikes
ridden over the course to leave smooth tracks in which
the racers can make pace. This probably accounts in
part for the fast American road-race times.
* * * *
Is cricket the only clean sport ? Cycling, which
begins to stink all over the earth, seems to be following
football. With the increased money-prizes come straight-
out charges of stiff running, boring, and deliberate
attempts to unseat favorites. At recent S.A. wheel-races,
certain riders were openly declared all over the ground to
be “ stiff,” and remarkably accurate forecasts of several
events were certainly given.

“Denver” Ed. Smith was in luck in getting
Mick Dooley to look after him for his fight with Joe God-
dard, which was fixed for 31st ultimo, for if any man can
tell him Goddard’s moves Mick can. Dooley undoubtedly
beat Goddard in Melbourne, as the latter was down longer
than 10sec. ; but, with Mick’s usual luck, the fight was
not claimed for him, and Goddard was allowed time to
recover.
* * * *
Bicycle-racing, as a sport, is condemned by its
own professors. When they don’t go fast enough to win
it is because they are not properly “paced,” or haven’t
“struck form,” which appears to be something quite dif-
ferent to getting fit in the ordinary athletic sense of the
term. Take the prattle of young Parsons on his somewhat
inglorious return from ’Mu'rka the other day. “If I had
had good pacing I think I would have done very well.
My last appearance was in a match with Jay Eaton, of
three one-mile heats, and three heats of five miles. In
each case he beat me by two out of three heats. The
reason of that, to my mind, was chiefly his superior pace-
making.” A bike-race, on Parsons’ showing, is really
contested by non competitors, the men who profess to
ride against one another being merely the tails of the
comets who are supplying them with pace (otherwise
‘heart,” or pluck) and judgment. Wise pacemakers
make a wisely-run race, weak pacemakers make a slow
disciple, no pacemakers mean a farcical display by two
men who daren’t adopt a bold policy. The sport will die
of ridicule in due course. Bill Beach didn’t want a flying
“ eight ”to lure him along in front of Hanlan. He raced
on his own.
» * * *
The Austral Cycle Agency will shortly receive
big shipments of the world-famous Humber bicycles direct
from England. The Humher, since its introduction to
Australia, has gained an unequalled reputation for re-
liability, lightness, and speed. Gov. Hampden rides one
of these machines. Also, it was on a Humher racer that
Martin accomplished all his sensational Australian vic-
tories. *

S.A. talks of electoral cricket. But under any
such scheme Geo. Giffen, Jack Lyons, Clem. Hill and
Jones would all play for the one district.
ft
Cricket in Tasmania. A city club has a patron
—a big landowner who is often good for a subsidy. Said
patron plays for a country club. In a match between
teams representing the clubs referred to, the patron was
given out and duly went to the pavilion. Then, in the
course of half an-hour or so, remorse or something struck
the city team, the umpire admitted he mif/ht have made
a mistake, and the patron was offered, anrl accepted, an-
other innings ! And yet they wonder why cricket and
football don’t flourish in Tasmania.

“Our advice to Mr. Ranjitsinhji, who was
selected to play for England (says Bombay Champion) is
to stay in England, where he seems to be honored ; for, if
he ever returns to his home, there will not be a single
European cricket club which will grant him the privilege
of membership. ... He is an Indian , and therefore
these sportsmen would keep him at arm’s length.” But
perhaps it’s “ dirty nigger ” only when he can’t play; it
might be “noble Ranjitsinhji” when he’s a great
cricketer. Read Kipling.

yet seen only one American make of bicycle which can in

A really smart buttoner for a Sydney Chinese
lottery will sell £2O to £3O of sixpenny tickets in a
week. His commission is 10 per cent. The largest
amount that can be won for sixpence is £75.
* * * *
The Urrullgurra (N.S.W.) football club chal-
lenged a Kempsey team to a friendly “ rough-and-
tumble.” But when the Kempsey lads reached the village
with the aboriginal name (30 miles from Kempsey) they
found their opponents playing a cricket-match with the
blacks of that place. And, to their amazement, the
Urrul-&c., boys refused to relinquish their game with the
blacks, and Kempsey went home desperate. The follow-
ing Saturday a cricket-match was arranged between Tar-
rabel and Urrullgurra teams, and again the latter team
disappointed the opposing players—for the second time
preferring a game with their dusky brethren ! Now,
some observers state that white Australians are fast
reverting to the habits and appearance of their aboriginal
compatriots. Is there here a proof of the tendency, a
subtle acknowledgment of kinship ?

“Marriage is a joy for a month and a
sorrow for a life, and the paying of settlements
and the breaking of the back under a load of
misery and the listening to a woman’s tongue.”—
Arabian 'proverb.
The match-making record of the world.
The Diamond Match Co., of Barberton, Ohio,
U.S.A., in one day produced 12,356 gross of boxes
of matches, that is 1,779,264 boxes of 100 matches
each. The 13 machines are automatic, but need
the supervision of 340 workmen. The biggest
English factory employs 8,000 workmen, but it
cannot anything like equal the output of Barber-
ton’s 340 men and 13 machines.
Purves, Q.C., making a pathetic appeal to
the jury on behalf of the Melb. City Bank direc-
tors : “When we kneel beside our beds at night
and say ‘ Forgive us our trespasses as we—er—as
we—um —’” Meanwhile, amidst “laughter in
the court,” Deakin, M.L.A., and the five other
council for the accused hold a hurried conference
but the missing words remain missing. The god-
less Vic. educational system again. Also the pic-
ture of Purves on his knees is—well, it is a bit
sudden, so to speak.
Be the recent flood at Blenheim (M.L.),
the local War Crt states that “ God in a marvel-
lous manner kept the quarters dry, though they
looked like a house in Venice.” Wherefore the
local scoffers are asking if public-houses at a
similar elevation weren’t dry also, or if it is really
meant that the water stood up in a heap around
the home of the big drum, yet didn’t come in.
And people who are not scoffers want to know if
these wild-eyed individuals really think that the
Ruler of more worlds than there are grains of
dust in this mean little earth was personally
occupied in keeping their floor from getting damp,
and if the Salvationist has any idea of the rela-
tive sizes of things.
“ One of the most unfortunate features of
the Queensland tick-plague,” remarks a Bulletin
travelling correspondent, “ is that within the area
proclaimed infested are some of the best, in fact,
the best herds of the north. For years the Towers
and Townsville have obtained their meat-supply
from a few surrounding stations, and the quality
of meat has been equal perhaps to any in Aus-
tralia. Should the plague touch the well-culled
and highly-bred mob of Herefords, on Lindhurst,
it will make the owner —Barnes, of Casino,
N.S. W.—wriggle and his former partner, Fulford,
weep. For years Fulford made the improvement
of that herd of “ Bailies” his sole thought by day
and the subject of his dream at night, and the re-
sult might well make any man proud. Not far
from Lindhurst lives another cattle-man who is
doubtless on nettles. This is Mytton, of Wondo-
vale, who, for years, has been one of the largest
' suppliers to the Towers and Townsville markets.
Two other Northern men who will doubtless feel
anxious are Edkins, of Mount Cornish (Mutta-
burra), and Thornton, of Tower Hill. These are
two of the most famous stations in Central Queens-
land, both on account of the high quality of the
stock and the history of the early days on the runs.
It was from Mount Cornish that that colossal
cattle-theft of something like a thousand head
took place years ago, and of late years the fame of
the place has spread all over Australia on account
of the hospitality of Mr. Edkins and his hand-
some and charming wife. Thornton, of Tower
Hill, is widely known as one of Queensland’s pre-
mier amateur jockeys. He breeds, trains, and
rides his own horses, and over hurdles has few
superiors anywhere. He is well-known in Sydney
and Melbourne as a crack pigeon-shot and an all-
round good fellow. And such men are to have
ticks ! ”

A Much-valued Testimonial from one of
Australia’s Most-Prominent Explorers.
Grand Hotel, Spring-st., Melbourne,
March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmusskn,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to inform
you that I have derived great benefit from your
celebrated Alfaline Remedies during my explor-
ing expeditions throughout Australia, and I con-
sider them invaluable to all travellers in the bush.
—Yours very truly, B. BUTTON,
(Formerly a Councillor, Brighton, England).
Witness : A. F. Beker, Manager, Grand Hotel,
Spring-street, Melbourne.
Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 18711 was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

EX-CHAMPION JAMES J
CORBETT.
AN INTERESTING INTERVIEW WITH
THE GREAT PUGILIST.
WHAT HE SAYS OF HIS COMING
FIGHT WITH FITZSIMMONS.
From the St. Louis, Mo., Chronicle.
James J. Corbett’s history as a ring hero will
reach the end of the chapter when his battle with
Bob Fitzsimmons is won and lost, for, as the world
knows, Corbett is matched to tight Fitzsimmons.
Corbett, attired in a frock coat of the period, plug
hat, and shod in patent leathers, sat in his apart-
ments in the Southern Hotel at St. Louis the
other day, and delivered himself of a few opinions
regarding his coming fight.
“I am fully aware that Fitz. will give me the
most scientific battle of my career,” said the cham-
pion to a Chronicle man. “Heis a hard hitter
and cool headed. I saw him fight Dempsey and
posted myself on his style. He is my equal al-
most in height and reach, though after carefully
comparing his method of boxing with mine, I can’t
see where he has any advantage over me, as I am
younger, stronger, and shiftier, hit oftener and
mix my blows more. Fitzsimmons is foxy—he
was cute enough to feign grogginess in several of
his battles, thus throwing his opponents off their
guard. He can’t fool me by working the groggy
dodge. I will take no chances with him, 1 think
I can whip him within fifteen rounds. After my
fight with Fitzsimmons I will devote my entire
attention to my theatrical enterprise and retire
permanently from the prize-ring.”
Corbett is now the picture of health, weighing 214
pounds. “I am bigger, better, and stronger than
ever before,” replied the champion when asked
about his health. “The rheumatic complaint
that held me captive off and on some time ago has
left me completely, and 1 know I am cured of it.
I contracted rheumatism before training for my
fight with Peter Jackson,’’ continued Corbett.
“ How I got it or where I got it is a mystery to
me, but that I had it is a fact that I am not likely
to forget very soon. I suffered a good deal with
rheumatism after that fight. Some days my arms,
wrists, and fingers would be so stiff and swollen
that I could not use them at all. My legs also
pained me, but to a less degree. Then, acain, all
this would leave without any apparent reason, and
I would not be troubled again for weeks. Of course
I doctored for my complaint continuously, but the
attacks seemed to come every few weeks just the
same. A short time after my fight with Jackson I
went east and met and defeated Dominick M‘Caf-
frey, though I was handicapped by rheumatism
in my right leg in my encounter with M'Caffrey.
These periodical attacks of rheumatism affected
me until a few weeks before I began training for
my fight with Mitchell. One day I sat in the
Coleman House, New York. My eye chanced to
cross an advertisement of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. I had never heard of them
before, and as an experiment purchased a box. I
consumed two boxes, and was pleased with the
results, for the pains began to leave my arms and
legs. After taking four boxes according to direc-
tions, I found myself greatly improved. The
improvement is permanent I am sure, for I
haven’t been troubled with rheumatism since.
Before using the pills the rheumatic attacks re-
turned every month or so, especially if I caught a
cold. When I trained for my fight with Mitchell
in Florida, I suffered from malaria and used the
pill as a tonic with splendid results. I found that
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills were efficacious in build-
ing up the system after a malarial attack.”
The above I attest as entirely correct.
Signed, JAS. J. CORBETT.

DENTAL ART.
There are, perhaps, few cities in the world where
scientific dentistry has attained to such proficiency
as in Sydney. Anyone interested is invited to
inspect the establishment of Mr. J. I. Marshall,
28 Wynyard Square, Sydney. There, in conve-
nient proximity to the well-appointed waiting-
rooms and surgeries, is a laboratory replete with
all the latest appliances required for supplying
artificial teeth of the highest order of merit. Mr.
Marshall is always pleased to show these speci-
mens of the dental craft to enquirers.

The Friendly Societies are establishing
female branches, and Lady Lamington herself is
enrolled as a female forester. A new horror added
to life will be the female Oddfellow, Orangeman,
or Hibernian—with all the dreadful and inevitable
sequences of club nights and latch-keys.
Miss Ada Cambridge (Mrs. Cross), the
Australian novelist, published a book lately
entitled “A Humble Enterprise.” One of her
English critics describes it as being “extremely
restful ” —as though it were a new kind of water-
bed for invalids or a patent cure for insomnia.
This is. the very nadir of irony.

Certain- Melbourne G.P.O. people, now in
trouble through disposing of obsolete stamps, seem
to have imagined that the wholesale dealers who
bought the goods were anxious to keep their trans-
I actions* dark. That’s where they made the mis-
i take, apparently. Healers, although ready to
purchase “specimens ” under illicit circumstances
rather than let outsiders pick up the bargains, are
professionally interested in protecting the scarcity,
so to speak; of stamps that ought to be valuable.
Hence the cheerful alacrity of the' principal phila-
telic tradesmen in stating all they know re the
] leakage of obsolete stamps from the place where
| they are officially out of issue.

C.M.G. : “Melb. Leader lately published \
two pages of portraits of the operations of local
Salvationists saving souls. Advertising piety is not
a new thing, but this is peculiarly sickening. You
get ‘Capting ’ Brown, and ‘Brigadier ’ Smith, and
‘ Lieut.’Sarah Jenkins artfully posed in the very
act of snatching brands from the burning, said
brands having the impress of the camera-man’s
fingers all over them. Uniformed saints are
shown vvith their best heavenly expressions, piously
pawing ’orrid examples at the prison gates, and
propping up a beastly drunk, more in sorrow than
in anger, whilst the most picturesque lasses form
themselves into taking groups, administering j
righteousness and grub to suffering sinners of their
own sex in the city’s slums. Disgusting this, with
the idea of the faked saint and the faked sinner
photo’d for the'glory of the ‘Loard.’ The photo
‘ process ’ has much to answer for ; through its
agency our papers are littered with the banal mugs
of all sorts and conditions of conceited nobodies,
and with irritating waxwork groups—Lady Tangle- 1
foot’s garden-party, the Mudyille Cycle Club, the
Geebung Harriers— 4 sassiety ’ gossip in picture
form. Every .man wants to inflict his photo upon
his neighbors ; it is the curse of our time that the
‘ process ’ enables it to be done so cheaply.’’

There is a fashion in lying as in dress. The
fighting-lie and the snake-lie have just lately
disappeared before the tick-lie. Thus a Northern
paper : “ The hides were then scraped and the
ticks collected. They filled a large oil-drum and,
when pressed, yielded about five gallons of
blood ! ” '
“The Bushman's Song,” “Last Week,”
and “ Daylight is Dying,” the three songs from
“ The Man from Snowy River,” recently set to
music, are now having an enormous sale. They
xnay be had from Angus and Robertson, Sydney,
_«nd all musicsellers. Post free, 2/2 each.*
The Armenian difficulty is causing much
discussion amongst the Mahommedans in Sydney.
A well-to-do Afghan, the other day, laughed at
.he idea of the Sultan being deposed. “Why,”
ne said, “ the Sultan alone is a match for all
Europe.” But if, by any unforeseen circumstance,
he should be endangered, then he (the speaker)
and every true Mussulman would take up arms.
Before the Sultan, who is Commander of the
Faithful, could be overthrown rivers would run
red, and when finally the infidel triumphed, dark-
ness would come upon the world. For so says the
Koran.
“ Society totters when the thinker takes it
in hand.”
Characteristic par. in an English Shanghai
(China) paper, the Celestial Empire :
A wild scene of excitement prevailed in Fogg's buildings
to-day, the cause being a dog, which made for the nearest
person —it unfortunately happened to he a foreigner, and
biting him severely on the leg.
“Unfortunately” is good.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
An excellent preventive against mosquito
bites is a mixture of a small teaspoonful of carbolic
acid to an ounce of glycerine. Or a weak solution
of carbolic acid alone may be sponged over the
body.

Business, Robber, etc.
An insolvent grocer, examined before Judge
Moleswortli, asserted that “cutting” in the Melb. trade
was carried on to the extent that a housewife could ob-
tain almost all Tier groceries under Cost by buying one line
at one shop and another at another, as each grocer chose
certain lines to“cut” in. No wonder the trade is rapidly
going into the hands of the “ octopus ” firms, which have
Branches spread over all the suburbs.
* * * •
An interesting, but fiscally confusing, element
has been emtlQdipd in the latest Q’sland Customs legisla-
tion. The £5 a ton duty on sulphuric acid was favored
by the Govt., but stoutly resisted by the representatives
of gold-mining districts, on the ground that cheap sul-
phuric acid is a necessity on many fields. At this stage a
firm of chemists (Elliott Bros.) interpleaded, alleging that
the great expense they had gone to in erecting a sul-
phuric acid factory in Brisbane would be lost were the
duty'on sulphuric acid struck out. Eventually a com-
promise was agreed to and accepted by Parliament—the
±5 duty will remain Customs law, but Elliott Bros, have
agreed to supply to Q. mines sulphuric acid at the price at
which it could be landed from Sydney if the duty were
abolished. In other words, Protection is the law, but
Queensland miners in this instance enjoy Freetrade.
Others than miners are excluded from the privilege.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — The pranks of the N.S.W.
Income - tax Commissioners lately set out in your
“Wild Cat Column” appear, to a layman, very like
something worse than mere “official impudence.” Per-
haps those important personages would not be offended if
it were pointed out to them that they are running con-
siderable risks. There is a common law misdemeanour
known as “ extortion,” an offence created to protect, in
some measure, the community against the rapacity of
public officials. Extortion, it is said, “consists in any
officer's unlawfully taking, by color of his office, from any
man, any money or thing of value that is not due to him,
or more than is due, or before it is due ” (4 St. Comm.).
The definition certainly does not err for want of compre-
hensiveness. The punishment is fine and imprisonment,
and sometimes forfeiture of the office. If some of the
people victimised by the circulars foolishly sent out by
the Commissioners were to apply to the Supreme Court
for a rule calling on them to show cause why an informa-
tion should, not issue against them for extortion, it might
perhaps bring them to their senses. If the statements
made by your contributor are correct it would seem that
a very fair'argument could be made why such an appli-
cation should succeed. Whether it succeeded or not, it
would certainly have the effect of pointing out to the
Commissioners and all other Govt, officials some of the
half-forgotten liabilities imposed on them by law. —Rus.
Roberts.

A Tas. Leg. Councillor, in a temporary fit of
lucidity, the other day moved that no Judge should be
allowed-to.hold a personal interest in mines and similar
ventures. 'The proposal was met with a storm of disap-
probation. . Amid the chorus of scorn, the only distin-
guishable words were “ interference ” (grunts), “ liberty ”
(more grunts), “subject” (several savage snorts),
“ pshaw ! pooh !” Then a volley of sudden sharp cracks,
and the reporters dodged to avoid the shower of flying
collar-studs.

What a Godsend to back-country diggers would
the jerked beef of old days be ! It contains nearly all the
nourishing properties of the fresh article, and, properly
cooked, is highly toothsome —preferable in every waj r to
the “tinned dog” and other canned perdition on which
the miner lives —or dies. Jerked beef is made by simply
cutting the flesh of fat cattle into sheets, and drying it iii
the sun. It can be turned out cheaply, but should be
worth a lot of money on the Westralian fields. In the
early sixties it was extensively made in Victoria, and
shipments were sent to England. Jerked beef, otherwise
known as tasago and charqui, is a South American notion,
as the buffalo knows to his cost.
* * * *
Two small seams of good coal have been found
near the surface at Frankston, 27 miles from Melbourne,
and in view of this and other encouraging discoveries the
idea of putting a big duty on imported coal is gaining
ground in Victoria. Melbourne Chamber of Manif
facturers is inclined to favor the idea, on the ground that
the local coal discoveries have delivered it from the tre-
mendous exactions of the old Newcastle “ ring.” Govern-
ment is also favorable, partly because the dutv would
yield a little revenue, partly because the local coal pro-
duction creates railway traffic, and therefore deserves
encouragement, and partly on general principles. The
chief opposition, so far, comes from the steamship com-
panies whose occupation of humping coal from Newcastle
to Port Phillip is seriously menaced.
* * • *
Somebody writes to inform The Bulletin that
no order can be given to any Victorian woollen mills ex-
cept through the Melb. wholesale houses, which charge 15
per cent, commission. Our horrified informant suggests
that this is a scandalous state of things in a State-pro-
tected industry, but really it is not a circumstance upon
the local hat-ring, as regards commercial iniquity. Retail
orders for cloth must be executed through wholesale
houses in any country. The houses buy the stuff in large
pieces, and supply short lengths to their customers (the
shops), and 15 per cent, is not an extravagant profit, con-
sidering that the houses seem bound to get odd bits left
over, &c. It is the hat-ring which runs the factory in the
first place, and then monopolises the distribution of the
hats (at about 30 per cent, profit) to the retailers, that
represents the dark side of the industry in Victoria. Mel-
bourne shopkeepers talk airily to their customers of
getting hats from the Denton mills. They can’t do it,
however. They have to procure their stocks from the
syndicated wholesale warehousemen who control the
factory.

Dear Bulletin, ; —Let me supplement your re-
cent par. concerning Ramornie Meat Works. The works
paid last season Bs. (id. per 1001 b., dressed weight, for
prime bullocks, and 6s. to 7s. 6d. for cows. Put the
average of a mob of bullocks at 7001 b. ; at this weight the
owner would nett £2 19s. 6d. less 2 per cent, discount for
cash (bills are not given). The firm gets as “ bunce ” the
hides, which in the home market to which they go, may
safely be averaged at 20s. each, tongues (which are
canned and sold at 2s. or 2s. 6d. each), tails, which are
also canned, the web and gut fat, hoofs, horns, hair,
shanks, &c., &c., all of which are utilised and sold. The
meat-extract, which you remark is sold retail at a 600
per cent, profit, should turn the firm in about the same
percentage. The extract maker is supposed to make at
the rate of 81b. of extract on an average per head. This,
at the wholesale price of 6s. per lb., totals £2 Bs. To this
add the price of hide, tongue, tail, and other etceteras,
which may be placed at 61 155., and the total is 64 3s.
Tlun comes the meat; put, we will say, 5001 b. of meat at
3d., £6 55.; this swells the amount, to 1210 Bs., and, besides
all this, there is tallow and bones taken from the carcase.
The expenditure in comparison with this is a mere flea-bite
per head. The chief expenditure is in labor-saving
machinery, which is constantly being introduced. Nearly
all the work is done on the contract system. The
slaughtermen get Is. per head for bullocks, and 9d. for
cows. The boning is another contract. Hide folding
another. Wood-cutting and hauling another and so on.
There is money in meat preserving, and anyone with
capital to invest could not go wrong in venturing, in a
small way for a start, on one of the Northern Rivers—
where cattle can be bought so cheap.—Yours, Bovis.
The ease and facility with which partnerships
in big trading concerns are dissolved nowadays in Aus-
tralia, remarks a Bulletin correspondent, is in marked
contrast to the complications that ensued with similar
proceedings a quarter of a century ago. Then it was a
matter of months of labor and almost endless conferences
between the partners. Now, it may be said to be a matter
of days, hours or minutes. The notification of desired
severance is made, and not in the private offices of the
firms is the knot untied, but in the bank-manager’s
sanctum. The financial magnate pronounces the divorce,
and the retiring partner gets the shoot, at times not very
pleasantly.

The owner of a Riverine station, heavily
mortgaged to on- of the bi<f finance go’s, lately received a
letter from Ins employers to this effect: “The company
would he glad to know what personal property'you have
on ‘Stony-broke’ station, together with full particulars

wb >sj clauses resembled those of an octopus (joke here)’

sheep down to his grandmother’s photo., the mangle he
had promised to buy for his wife, “and also all the pre-
sent and future increase thereof,” replied : “ My' personal
property on the station consists of one pure-bred Alder-
ney cow and two Leghorn cocks and a hen. One of the
cocks is thorough bred, the pedigree of the other is doubt-
ful.” Shortly' afterwards he received another letter
asking whether he had any other property besides that
mentioned. The reply was : “ Since writing you last the
cow has calved, and two eggs have been laid which I
believe belong to me.” The mortgagees have now entered
into their kingdom.

The transmutation of silver into gold is the
little thing troubling: five metallurgical chemists in
America, arid if one of their number (Dr. S. H. Emmens)
is to be believed they have got over it. A big correspon-
dence is going on between the editor of the Engineering
anp Mining Journal and the alchemist who refuses to be
draw'll into revealing the secret. He declares he has
made 4oz. pure gold from 6oz. silver,,that the loss in the
process is only 25 per cent., and that the product has re-
sponded to every test. They reckon on a profit of 12s fid
on each ounce of silver they use. The new substance is
called “ argentaurum,” and “national calamity” is
already mentioned. One thing seems to have been lost
sight of : nearly' the whole value of the process would
depend on the fact of its invention being' kept a profound
secret. You could sell millions’ worth of “ made” gold ;
but once let the world know you were making it !

Dear Bulletin,--Merit.ion of a Sydney money-
lending society that works with a modest £2O capital re-
minds me that 1 am the inventor (as far as I know') of a
Co-operative Savings Bank Loan scheme which w'as first
put into practice at a London warehouse. Let me explain
my system for the benefit of all bodies of impecunious
young men w'ho may be gathered together in business.
We received our salaries monthly at the warehouse afore-
said. The savings-bank was formed by an issue of 20 £1
shares among some nine employes, who applied respec-
tively for one, two, or three shares, on, which 2s. fid. per
share was called up every month until our secretary—the
chief man in the counting-house—had all the capital in
hand. Then we started to draw loans on the 20th of Hie
month, each borrower being entitled to apply for hair as
much again a-« the amount standing to his credit in the
share ledger. Thus a holder of two shares might borrow
£3 if the loan capital wasn’t over-applied for, and as some
of the shareholders seldom wanted to borrow there was
generally a 50 per cent, margin available for the few
applicants. If they all wanted a loan each of them was
entitled to borrow just as much from the bank as he had
put into it. The loans were never issued before the 20th
of the month, and on the Ist of the following (the firm’s
pay-day) they were re-paid plus Is. in the £ interest.
Then for 19 days the money lay dormant in the cashier’s
desk, which feature of the system served to keep our im-
pecuniosity within respectable bounds and guarantee the ■
solidity of the bank. Every six months the interest pro-
fits (£6) were divided among the shareholders. A £2
shareholder who had borrow'ed his “ couple ” religiously
every month got back the 12s. interest he had paid,
whilst the prudent investor who hadn’t borrowed at all
drew an income at the rate of shixty per shent per annum.
—Cockney.

M. M. Roseingrave is an Irishman who
was born an athlete ; he came to Sydney from
Ireland last January. Ho is just on 6 feet high,
weighs, stripped, 11 stone; and is 23 years old :
he is the second youngest of five brothers, the big-
gest of whom reaches 6ft. sin. Koseingrave began
contests when he was sixteen; at seventeen he
did a 22ft. 4in. long jump; a sft. Bin. high jump,
and a 48ft. lOin. hop, step and a jump. When 20
years old he high-jumped 6ft. OJin. ; long-jumped
23ft. lOiVm., but the latter was disallowed as a
record because Roseingrave when taking off came
M. M. ROSEINGRAVE. [Kerry, photo.
over by half an inch. As it is, he recorded the
world’s amateur long jump on Sydney Cricket
Ground lately with 23ft. 7£. He also holds a
record hop, step and jump with 50ft. lin. He is
one of the finest hurdle racers ; at the recent big
Sydney sports meeting no Australasian or other
competitor in the 120 yds. hurdles could get to
him by yards. He never trains for his contests
on system ; he does not have any of the rubbing
down which the orthodox athlete ranks second
only to sleep. At the same time, he is a man who
never runs into flesh; his is the limber fibrous
type. He has never done any sprinting ; in fact,
the only athletic exercise to which he has “ laid
his mind ”is jumping. Dumb-bells he uses about
once a year.


Dear Bulletin,— Re Centralian frogs and
Percy Hodgkinson’s letter of Sept. 12. We were
a small and enthusiastic prospecting party, and
had braved many privations, the chief trouble
being the awful need of water. For two days we
had neither tasted, touched, nor handled
moister than a tin of sardines, when one evening-
after a long tramp, we struck a shallow two-acre
lagoon, well-shaded. We were at a loss to account
for the fact that the water had not evaporated
under the blinding sun that had scorched the
country for weeks, but wasted no time in specula-
tion. Next morning, on awakening after ten
hours’ sleep, we found, to our horror, that the
water had disappeared, and only a drove of gigan-
tic frogs with aldermanic corporations sat dumped
down in the heaviest shadows, blinking sardonic-
ally into our amazed faces. We had neglected to
charge our bags, and the mysterious disappearance
of the water seemed to mean death to us all. We
remained in camp throughout the day, too hope-
less and miserable to exert ourselves. Imagine
our wonder and our joy when, a little after sun-
down, the shouts of the boss of the party, Pascoe
Dow, 1.N.F.R.N.L., L.1.R.E., called our atten-
tion to the fact that water had re-appeared
in the lagoon. It is useless to attempt
to enlarge upon the profound astonishment that
possessed the party, or to detail the thousand and
one stories advanced to account for the apparent
miracle. We determined to make sure of a supply,
and filled everything that would hold water ; and
it was well we did, as at eight o’clock next morn-
ing the lagoon was again as dry as a bimetallic
leading article. The superstitious fears of the
majority of the party led to a hurried departure
after that, so I was not enabled to investigate
closely ; but I am now convinced that the portly
frogs we noticed were the new water-conserving
variety, and my theory is that they swallowed the
water at sun-up in order to prevent loss by evapo-
ration, and disgorged it again at sundown to revel
in their native element. Travellers in Central
Australia now drive droves of these frogs before
them through dry country, to avoid the necessity
of carrying water and the risks of death from
drought. They are easily driven, I believe, and a
couple of good sheep-dogs will manage 80,000.
Care has to be taken, however, as sudden and
simultaneous evacuation sometimes leads to floods.
—Yours, Silas Snell. N.B.— English papers
please copy.—S.S.
