A CHARACTERISTIC AUSTRALIAN PLEASANT
SUNDAY AFTERNOON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Won’t you let me ? —Won’t I let you what ?
Can’t you guess ?—l’m not going to bother guess-
ing your silly riddles !
Try !—No, thanks. How pretty those she-oaks
Where ?—Over there on Harbrow’s hill.
By Charman’s ? —Yes.
Yes, they are pretty, aren’t they ?—Yes, I
think so.
They are she-oaks, aren’t they?—Yes, of course
they are ! How silly you are !
They’re not he-oaks, are they ? —Oh, don’t be a
goat!
I’m not being a goat. I thought they must be
she-oaks.—Well, I said they were she-oaks ! Why
do you think they must be she-oaks ?
Because they’re pretty. —Well, aren’t he-oaks
pretty too?
Not nearly so pretty as she-oaks. —Rot!
All right! . . . . Are we going to drive round
by Eumlumering ?
No, we’ll go through old Naree.—Why do you
go that way ?
I like it better. —Why?
Well, it’s more shady, for one thing.—Oh, you’re
always on for shade ! It’s not hot.
You say so, but Trivet’s beginning to sweat. —A
lot you care if Trivet sweats !
Don’t you think I do?—Well, perhaps you do,
but that’s not why you’re going home old Naree
way.
Why do you think I want to go old Naree way ?
—I don’t know.
Why do you want to go by the main road ?
I didn’t say I did.
Do you want to go home by the main road ?—I
don’t care which way you go.
Oh, I thought you did ! —No.
I say, will you let me? —Oh, don’t be silly ! Will
I let you what ?
Will you let me hold your hand ?—What for?
Oh, just for fun.—l don’t see where the fun
comes in.
I do, though.—How can you hold my hand and
drive too?
Oh, that’s easily done!—You’ve tried before,
then ?
No, never. —How do you know it’s easily done?
Why, just like that, see?—Well, I don’t see the
good of it, anyhow.
Don’t you like it?—No, you silly idiot!
Not when I squeeze it like that?—Oh, don’t!
You hurt me!
All right, I won’t hurt you.—Oh, let go, let go !
There’s the Bolderives’ trap !
Well, they can’t see. . Besides, I don’t care if
they do !—I do, though.
I don’t believe you do care.—Why ?
Because you let everybody see you the other
evening.—What do you mean ?
Oh, you know —at the Mechanics’ Ball. —What
on earth are you driving at ?
Didn’t you go out after dancing the Alberts ?
Well, what if 1 did ?
Oh, nothing. —No ; tell me, what are you trying
to get at ?
Who were you with ?—I forget.
Oh, do you?—Yes, I do.
I suppose you forget that Charlie Ailing nipped
on to you for the Alberts ?—Well, what if he did ?
But that’s not all. —Well, go ahead.
You both went out and sat in Hansman’s buggy.
—Well ?
Little Teddy was under it.—l know. The
little brute !
Yes, but you didn’t know it then!—l didn’t
care if there was a dozen of them under it!
Teddy was on for a lark; so he went and got the
lamp off the gatepost. —Yes, and flashed it on us.
Yes ; and Ailing had his arm round your waist,
and you let it stay there. —Well, there’s nothing
in that!
I didn’t say there was.—Well, what’s all the
fuss about ?
I think it’s you makes the fuss, not me. —I don’t
make any fuss.
You won’t make a fuss if I——? —Now, don’t be
stupid.
Why? —Because I don’t think it’s nice.
I do ! —Why do you want to kiss me ?
I never said I did, did I ? —Well, you as good
as said you did.
I never said a word about it.—Well, I say!
What did you put your arm there for ?
Because I wanted to kiss you.—You said just
now you didn’t.
No I didn’t.—l think you’re dotty.
So I am. You make me dotty.—How ?
You’re so pretty.—Oh, stop that.
Yes you are.—You don’t think I’m as pretty as
Mil Tibson, do you?
Oh, no; not at all! Not a little bit! —Well,
why don’t you try and kiss Mil Tibson ?
Oh, she’s too pretty. I only like kissing the
ugly girls.—Oh, thanks !Do you think Mil’s
pretty ?
No, she’s not my style. Do you?—Yes, I think
she’s a very pretty girl. What’s your style?
You are.—-Oh, of course, you had to say that.
I could say it all Sunday. I say, won’t you give
me one ? —No.
Do!—No.
Why?—l don’t want to.
I’ll take one. —You dare !
What’d you do?—l’d scream.
Is that all ?—l’d pinch you.
It’s worth it. —Is it ! I’d hurt you very much.
With that little hand ? Now, what can you do ?
—l’ve got the other free.
I’ll have that too. —You’ll have to drop the
reins, then.
Oh, Trivet’s all right.—l’ll click, and make him
bolt.
You would, would you?—Yes, I will, if you
don’t let me go.
Oh, I’ll soon settle that ! I’ll drive him up facing
the fence, by the tree. Now, what can you do?—
Ah, let g.>, Harry. Mr. Walker, let go ! Gee
up, Trivet !
It’s no use. You may as well cave in.—Ah,
don’t, Harry, don’t! .. . What a silly boy you
are ! . . . Do stop it. ... Ah, do stop it !. . .
You are a silly old duffer. Just look at my hair.
. . . Now, that’s enough !
Just one more.—Well, only one. ... I didn’t
say fifty. . . . Goodness gracious, Harry, stop !
Here are the Bolderives coming back !
By George, so they are ! Woa bade ! Click-
click. Gee-up, Trivet! I don’t think they saw
anything ; do you ?

The Boer of the Rifle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gov. Hampden, at Sydney rifle meeting, said the best
wish he could accord Australia’s riflemen was that if ever
they were called upon to defend their country they would
prove as good marksmen as the Boers.
Worthy tribute to the trekkers of the boundless
vasty veldt,
Dwellers by the Land of Ophir, heirs of Argo-
nauts of eld ;
These be brave men, O my brothers, heroes cast in
stubborn mould,
Sturdy sons of sterner fathers who have fought and
bled of old;
Let the pallid Jingo whimper o’er the gold-bugs of
the Rand,
Glorify the bush marauders of the British Char-
tered band ;
Let the hollow-chested Johnnies who have never
seen a war
Prate their blatant idle chatter—we will take it at
its par.
Now and then in moments tranquil ’tween the
clamors of the age,
When the war-clouds cease to hover and the cables
cease to rage,
It is wise to weigh and ponder on the follies of the
race
Swayed by prejudice and passion into acts of deep
disgrace.
Is there need to point the moral of the silly Soudan
spree
When we helped to choke a people “rightly
struggling to be free ” ?
Are we still less human-hearted than the British
of the East
When they gave the Sepoy rebels for the cannon’s
morning feast ?
Driven from beyond the waters, from their homes
by Northern waves,
Left they old-world feuds and factions buried in
the old-world graves,
Sought they out the land primeval set beyond the
Gape of Hope,
Mile by mile they fought and conquered, plain
and mountain, vale and slope ;
Scarce had smiled the sun of Heaven on the full
fruits of their toil
Ere the greed of British commerce marked them
for the Lion’s spoil,
And the gallant Christian nation of the Bible and
the Bag
Dispossessed and hanged and butchered for the
Glory of the Flag.
Hunted down for England’s glory, glory that is
often greed,
Victims of the flag symbolic of the “ golden
cross ” of creed,
Pass the hardy pilgrim fathers towards the
waters of the Vaal,
Mile by mile they trek for Freedom far beyond
the natives’ kraal;
Write the story fair, untarnished by the prejudice
of race—
Brave men need no London hall-mark, deadly
shots no English face !
Need we worship Cant and Humbug, must we
indignation sham
When the Lion’s cub is cornered in its foray for
the lamb ?
Friends of Freedom, take ye solace, learn to shoot
as well as pray ;
Right is strong, but sight is stronger when the
guns begin to play.
Every ballot has its bullet now or in the future
days,
North and east the foe may menace—fix him with
unerring gaze.
Straight your aim, and ’midst the nations they
will learn to let you be,
For the rifle rings the orders of the Goddess of the
Jbree ;
Take a leaf from war-worn burghers, sons of
pioneers of old,
Men who proved that lead has virtues far surpass-
ing good red gold.

or irregular bowels, flatulency, gravel, weakness or
pain across small of back, loss of muscular power,
gloomy, remorseful, fearful of something going to
happen, disturbed sleep, moaning, talking, grinding
teeth, tearful or want to laugh.
I have known instances in which,
most of these symptoms were present
in one patient. As a rule a great
many are present. In no case are
many absent.

IRISH MOSS.

One of many Testimonials.
Bonnington & Co., Manufacturers of Irish Moss.
Adamstown, 11th September, 1896.
Dear Sirs, —Being a sufferer for years from fibroid phthisis and bronchitis, I desire to
add my testimony in favor of your Irish Moss. As a soother of irritating cough, with much ex-
pectoration, I have found it to be the most effectual of anything I have tried. It loosens the mucus,
which, therefore, comes more readily away, and the cough'is thereby considerably pacified.
Yours respectfully, THOMAS STOKER.
